The Red Page
Kiddy Verses
Not all the verses by Annie R. Rentoul in
the gorgeous volume entitled Elves and
Fairies of Ida Rentoul Outhivaite (Lothian,
Melb.; £2 25., published by subscription and
ip* J at the beginning of last October, but
arriving at this office only last week) are
up to the whimsical standard of those quoted
here. The majority lack that original note,
and unfortunately bear the impression of
having been written merely to illustrate her
pictures. But that, after all, is their
subordinate function; for this book claims
attention as an art publication. The decora-
tive and color-work of Ida Rentoul Outh-
waite definitely places this artist in the
ranks of the leading Australian illustrators,
and in her special department, the illustra-
tion of fairy and kiddie stories, she
stands alone in the Commonwealth, and has
to look for comparison only overseas. Indeed,
she is doing for us what Arthur Rackham is
doing for the English kiddie. Artistically,
she is Racliham’s pupil; her work is a
feminine derivative of Raekham’s masculine
art. But the genealogy of her art is no dis-
paragement ; for Ida Rentoul Outhwaite’s
work is in itself so charming, so feminine,
that its dainty grace lends itself to fairy
stories even more usefully than Backhands
grotesquerie. And she has here a field that she
has alone exploited: she has domiciled the
fairies in an Australian environment. Her
pen drawings are delightful, examples of
dainty and extremely decorative work, and
her color illustrations are artistically satis-
fying. All are full .of delicate humor. Com-
pared with Norman Lindsay’s native bear,
this of Ida Rentoul Outhwaite is much too
Teddy-ish. but her other examples of our
fauna are admirably observed. Undoubtedly
this volume deserves to be ranked with the
important artistic productions in Australia,
and fortunate, indeed, must be the Aus-
tralian kiddie who owns the book. The
author and artist have decided to give all
their profits to the Red Cross Funds.
MOTHER.
I have cut my finger, mother; tie it, mother,
quick !
See, the sticky's off again; mother, make it
stick!
Mother, give me sweeties now; so I shall
not cry!
Mother, I have ate them all; mother, shall
I die?
Mother, see, I made a hat for my dolly dear,
Made it from my petticoat; look, I cut it
here!
Mother, sew me up again, where the old
hit's gone!
Here's a button off me, mother; mother,
sew it on!
Mother, God is watching me, 'cos I took a
tart;
Mother , make Him stop it, 'cos He hurts
me in my heart!
Mother, I pulled up the pansies, 'cos I
want to know
If I plant them on their heads, icill they
ever grow.
Mother, teas it God made skeeturs? Mother,
surely not!
If He made them, mother, well, He made a
blessed lot! _
Mother, I've been playing heathen in among
the coal;
Mother, I’m as black as black, and haven't
any soul!
Mother, I am awful tired, all my face is
wet;
Kiss the tears off, mother dear, so I shall
forget.
Mother, you are all the world; if you ever
go,
Take me with you, mother darling, 'cos I
love you so.
The Boomerang.
The boomerang booms and it bounces and
pops,
And continues to bumpety-bump till it
stops;
It goes round in a ring till a circle's com-
plete,
And returns to the starting-point right at
your feet.
O duckety duck and 0 swampety swan,
Keep out of the way till the boomerang's
gone;
O wallaby, waits, and O scoot, kangaroo!
For nothing will stop it unless it is you!
The brown piccaninny lives under the gums,
He doesn’t wear clothes and he doesn't do
sums ;
But he twirls a big boomerang right from
his nose,
Till it ends up a circle by kissing his toes.
O witchety grub and O sneakety snake,
The brown piccaninny prefers you to cake!
O parrot y pie and O cockatoo stew,
The boomerang's after such morsels as you!
The brown Aboriginee, sleek as a bun,
He goes out a-hunting, but'not with a gun;
Mind the bunnies so fluffety shake in their
skins
When the roundabout boomerang hurtles
and spins.
O baby brown bunny and baby brown bear,
The boomerang bumpety-bumps in the air;
For, to wrap a brown baby in, nothing will
do
But the fluffety furs that are growing on
you !
Annie R. Rentoul, in “Elves and Fairies.”
Trites
Trites again! This page had begun to
wonder whether W. B. Trites hadn’t been
listed as “missing.” His Life was a remark-
able novel; his Love (Mills and Boon, per
Dymock’s Library) is equally remarkable.
For Trites can write, whether you like his
stuff or not. In the case of Love, it will
probably be not; for there is much that is
distinctly disagreeable in “Brian Banaker’s
autobiography up to the age of 24 years.”
The question for the reader is whether
Trites means it to be disagreeable. This
page thinks he does, and pays its tribute
to his artistic sincerity. For Brian Banaker
is a millionaire; not only can he help being
a millionaire, but he revels in it. But
Brian is no best-seller’s puppet.
When Brian was 12 he determined to get
drunk. “Drunkenness,” he argued, “was
execrated by all respectable men and women.
Yet there must be pleasure in it, or other-
wise none would disgrace themselves, none
would wallow in the mire for its sake.....
What was this pleasure like ?.. . . And I
would go over in my mind the pleasures I
had myself enjoyed—the pleasure of sea-
bathing, the pleasure of reading. These
were superb pleasures, but would I accept
ruin and obloquy for them? No; never.
Clearly, then, there must be in drunkenness
a pleasure sweeter and profounder than I
had ever known. So, to plumb this pleasure,
I resolved to get drunk.” His solitary
debauch is successful.
The picture that Brian draws of his school
and. college days is, to us, an amazing one.
At his school practically all the boys used
elaborate cribs during their examinations.
A number of the boys played truant for
a week, forging their school reports for the
satisfaction of their parents. “The third
day we devoted to shop-lifting. We went
from department store to department store,
stealing.... We stole, in a kind of joyous
delirium, four or five dollars’ worth of
goods. What fun I found it!... ..” And at
his university Brian was admitted into the
most exclusive and expensive society. For
Brian claimed descent from a Prince of
Powys, and his father was president of the
College Royalists, “an organisation admit-
ting only collegians of royal blood.” His
rooms were sumptuous. He occupied the
largest suite in Haakon Hall, furnished with
Persian rugs and other appointments in
keeping, with old masters borrowed from his
father’s home on the walls. He had a bil-
liard-table, mobs of horses and a man-ser-
vant. As he complacently remarks, “It is
not astonishing that my welcome at college
was warm... .Thanks to my birth and
wealth, I rose from crest to crest of social
glory. What with my horses, my clothes,
my valet, I was the leading freshman of
the year.” Pah! But then Trites means
you to say “Pah!”
J*
“Loving beauty, I loved beautiful raiment.
My long lines of boots in their brass-
mounted forms; my cravats of rich, fresh
silk; my coats, with their trim balance; my
linen, heavy and white for evening wear,
crisp and delicately colored for the day.
..... !” That is the painful picture Trites,
with a terrible artistic sincerity, allows his
hero to draw of himself. But there are
pathetic scenes between this snob and his
parents that show the other side of
Brian ; and when he chose, as, for instance,
to win a running race, he easily swept aside
his sybaritic habits and trained with the
strictness of a professional athlete. But
he soon returned to drink. “Because I got
more happiness out of drink than out of
study and athletics.” And his birthday
party was a drunken orgie. The next step
in his education was provided by an actress.
He took her a trip to Bermuda, and on the
voyage he came into contact with the
American business man.
“As they talked my compatriots rubbed
their palms over their unshaven chins,
producing, like those Japanese insects that
rub one leg across another, a strange, harsh
note, a natural music, the anthem, as it
were, of the American business man.. And
they kept fiddling with strange objects—
with fountain-pens attached to their waist-
coat pockets by silver clips, with nickel
cigar-cutters that unfolded into foot-rules,
with combination nail-files and ear-picks
fixed to the ends pf watch-chains.” And at
Bermuda his girl deserts him for another
man. But Brian recovers, with the aid of
alcohol. “I drank with the long, tasteless
and lukewarm meal a half-bottle of cham-
pagne. I drank a liqueur with my coffee.
And during the evening I drank two or three
brandy-and-sodas. All this drinking, I was
not displeased to see, caused the pretty girls
to stare at me with wide, awestricken eyes.
At eleven I retired to my room, where,
if the spirit moved me, I drank another
brandy-and-soda while undressing. An ex-
es s- of alcohol.”
Brian determines to work. And in his
search for a profession he gives his bio-
grapher the chance to write a sickening
description of a scene in an operating
theatre, eompnruble only to Trites’ memor-
able description, in liis previous novel, of
a man dying from tetanus. Then Brian
becomes a recluse, a sybaritic recluse,
banned from society by the rumors .of his
wickedness. But he meets the beautiful .and
wealthy Marcelle. The rest of the story is
chiefly Marcelle’s beauty and Brian’s court-
ship, interspersed with elaborate menus and
lists of rare wines, and with a full list of
the bridegroom’s gifts to the bridesmaids.
This is evidently the “love” that gives the
novel its title; but though the pair are left
on their honeymoon, the careful reader re-
ceives vague hints that the marriage will
not be a happy one. liow could it, with
Brian for the husband?
A disagreeable and unnecessary book?
No; a remarkably well written book of a
disagreeable personality but a real human
being. And Trites, speaking of a genius
in his book, maybe confesses the secret of
his style: “My secret is that I can tell good
work from bad. Yes, I can tell a good sen-
tence, a good paragraph, a good chapter,
from a bad one, as you can tell a good from
a bad billiard shot. And so I crossed out
and re-wrote every bad chapter, every bad
paragraph, every bad sentence, till it was
good, as you might try a billiard shot over
and over till you got it. But there were
shots here and there I couldn’t get; so I
left them out altogether. That’s what makes
my book a little fragmentary.”
Jargon
Jargon was discovered by Arthur Quiller-
Couch, Professor of English at Cambridge
University. Full particulars are to be
found in On the Art of Writing (Cambridge
University Press, per Angus and Robertson,
Sydney; 10s.). Jargon is not Journalese.
The writer of Journalese at his worst is an
artist; “he daubs paint of this kind upon the
lily with a professional zeal” : he is trying
to embellish our language. But the Jar-
goners are not journalists. Jargon is spoken
and written by quite respectable persons. “It
is becoming the language of Parliament: it
has become the medium through which
Boards of Government, County Councils,
Syndicates, Committees, Commercial Firms,
express the processes as well as the conclu-
sions of their thoughts.” The two main
vices of Jargon are that it uses circumlocu-
tion rather than short straight speech, and
that it habitually chooses vague,, woolly
abstract nouns rather than concrete ones.
An obvious example of Jargon is to be found
in any House of Parliament. When a Minis-
ter is asked a question he cannot say “No.”
Being a Jargoner he replies: “The answer
to the question is in the negative.”
Here are some rough rules, laid down by
Quiller-Couch, for the discovery of Jargon:
“Whenever in your reading you come across
one of these words, ease, instance, character,
nature, condition, persuasion, degree—when-
ever in writing your pen betrays you to one
or another of them —pull yourself up and
take thought.” E.g., “A singular degree of
rarity prevails in the earlier editions of this
romance.” Translated into prose, this runs
simply: “The earlier editions of this romance
are very rare.” So: “He was conveyed to
his place of residence in an intoxicated con-
dition,” is, in mere prose, “He was carried
home drunk.” Here is another, extracted
by this page from a Sydney Morning
Herald editorial: “Appearances much favor
such a state of affairs being brought about.”
What the leader-writer meant to say was:
“Apparently so,” or even “Apparently.” And
here is one from The Argus: “For even
those who are convinced that a sedulously-
circulated rumor is false have not always the
means of demonstrating its falsity.” What
the writer meant was: “....can’t always
prove it.” The same writer says: “To form
leagues and to examine their operations with
a view to bringing about as complete effici-
ency as is possible.” All he need have said
was “to form and supervise leagues,” or “to
form leagues and see that they were
efficient.”
“The whole cloudy host of abstract terms”
is thus suspect. Another trick of Jargon is
that of Elegant Variation, so rampant in
the sporting press. In a student’s essay
upon Byron the writer thinks it indelicate to
repeat the poet’s name "twice on one page.
Byron becomes in turn “that great but
unequal poet,” “the meteoric darling of
society,” “this arch-rebel,” “the author of
Ghilde Harold ,” “the apostle of scorn.”
“this ex-Harrovian, proud, but abnormally
sensitive of his club-foot,” etc. As Quiller-
Couch says: “In literature as in life he makes
himself felt who not only calls a spade a spade
but has the pluck to double spades and re-
double.” Another rule is never to use such
remarks as as regards, with regard to, in
respect of, in connection tvith, according as
to whether, etc. “They are all. dodges of
Jargon, circumlocutions for evading this or
that simple statement.”
Very delightfully Quiller-Couch re-writes
Hamlet’s soliloquy in Jargon:
“To be, or the contrary? Whether the
former or the latter be preferable would
seem to admit of some difference of opinion;
the answer in the present case being of an
affirmative or of a negative character accord-
ing as to whether one elects on the one
hand to mentally suffer the disfavor of for-
tune, albeit in an extreme degree, or on
the other to boldly envisage adverse condi-
tions in the prospect of eventually bringing
them to a conclusion. The condition of
sleep is similar to, if not indistinguishable
from, that of death; and with the addition
of finality the former might be regarded as
identical with the latter; so that in this
connection it might be argued with regard
to sleep that, could the addition be effected,
a termination would be put to the endurance
of a multiplicity of inconveniences, not to
mention a number of downright evils inci-
dental to our fallen humanity, and thus a
consummation achieved of a most gratifying-
nature.”
Jargon is the product of a foggy mind, a
mind that dodges facts, a mind that is indo-
lent and timid.
War
Jolm Galsworthy collects A Sheaf from
his occasional writings (Heinemann, per
Sydney Book Club). Poor stuff, with the rust
in it. “Wild oats of a novelist, which he has
been asked to bind up.” Yes, but he needn’t
have agreed. A large portion of the collec-
tion deals, sincerely enough, with his usual
ante-bellum topics, the treatment of animals,
prisons and punishment, the position of
women. For a Dutch journal Galsworthy
attempts a diagnosis of the Englishman.
“The Englishman must have a thing brought
under his nose before he will act; bring it
there, and he will go on acting after every-
body else has stopped.... The Englishman,
partly through his lack of imagination and
nervous sensibility, partly through his inbred
dislike of extremes and habit of minimising
the expression of everything, is a perfect ex-
ample of the conservation of energy. It
is very difficult to come to the end of
him. ...The English character has a kind
of terrible coolness, a rather awful level-
headedness. . . . England produces more than
its proper percentage of cranks and-
poets; this is Nature’s way of redress-
ing the balance in a country where feelings
are not shown, sentiments not expressed,
and extremes laughed at.... The Eng-
lishman absorbs ideas slowly; would rather
not imagine anything decidedly till he is
obliged; but in proportion to the slowness
with which he can be moved, is the slowness
with which he can be removed.... For the
particular situation which the Englishman
has now to face, he is terribly well adapted.
Because he has so little imagination, so little
power of expression, he is saving nerve all
the time. Because he never goes to extremes
he is saving energy of body and spirit....
Let it be freely confessed that from an
msthetic point of view the Englishman,
devoid of high lights, coated with drab, and
superhumanly steady on his feet, is not too
attractive. But for the wearing, tearing,
slow and dreadful business of this war, the
Englishman, fighting of his own free will,
unimaginative, humorous, competitive, prac-
tical, never in extremes, a dumb, inveterate
optimist, and terribly tenacious —is equipped
with victory.”
J*'
An interesting pendant to John Masefield’s
brilliant narrative of Gallipoli is Suvla Bay
and After, by Juvenis (Hodder and Stough-
ton, per Dymock’s Library). The author is
a British captain who took a small part in
the pitiable failure of Suvla Bay. He writes
a simple story ; but, unconsciously, he betrays
the cause of the failure. For if many more
of the British captains had so little knowledge
of their task, possessed so little nous, and
were so nonplussed and confused by their
experiences in the battle, the anti-climax of
that expedition was inevitable. The arrange-
ments for water supply were horrible, and
the author does not seem to have tried very
hard to remedy this fatal omission. Appar-
ently he owed his wound to his lack of know-
legs of the use of cover. But he is incor-
rigibly cheerful about it all. Doubtless if
he had not occupied that “little eminence”
exposed to the enemy, he might have de-
veloped from the cheerful amateur into the
experienced soldier, but he became a casualty
within an hour of getting under fire.
“Stapleton”: Don’t know much of Billzac,
but do know something of Outbackers and
their literary tastes. Their tastes can be
roughly divided into blood-and-thunder,
sentiment, science. And the one thing Out-
backers do not want is books dealing .with
their own lives. Bill and Jim, knowing
there is more prose than poetry in their
environment, enweave the grey of their lives
with the crimson of a detective yarn and
the gold (pinchbeck it may be) of a love-
story. Billzac and Jimzac have not altered
from the commonplace Bill and Jim. To
the men in the trenches I would send detec-
tive yarns, sentimental tales and good Aus-
tralian magazines. To Billzac in hospital,
short stories, poetry and light reading, all
Australian for choice. Henry Lawson,
“Den,” Ethel Turner, E. J. Brady, Steele
Rudd, to mention only a few. , To Jimzac,
convalescent, up-to-date scientific, theologi-
cal and philosophical works. And, above
all, Australian books and papers, not
papers printed .in Australia by scissors-and-
paste English editors, but papers giving the
real Australian outlook.

Your Picture in
His Watch
should be a smiling picture. It’s your smile
that recalls the charm of your personality.
Your smile cannot be attractive unless your
teeth are sound and white.

VARIOVS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Summer Day.
In my heart soft music throbs.
Like a lilt of some air sweet,
Played bv bands afar,, that sobs
Faintly through a silent street.
It was such a time as this,
In the Warm embrace of day,
All the world, attuned to bliss,
Swooning in its rapture lay.
Lazily, the sea-lips fell
On the warm edge of the sand ;
Bound within the summer’s spell
Life and Love went hand in hand.
Languid laughter filled the air.
Heavy with the scent of flow rs ;
Thistledown flew here and there—
Thoughts as light as that were ours!
Carnival all nature held,
And its harmonv of sound
With a gentle music spelled
All the earth in joy profound.
Beating to the rise and fall
Of its splendid melody,
Human hearts obeyed its call,
Answering with laughter free.
High above the crowded beach,
Where the murmur of the throng
Faintly to our ears might reach
Like the echo of a song,
On a bank of couch-like grass,
Pleasant in a ti-tree’s shade,
Letting hours idly pass,
Through the we stayed.
Here and there a cloud was wrought
’Gainst the sky’s warm blue —a toy,
Like a soft and tender thought,
Floating on a sea of joy.
All the glory of the earth,
All the sunshine of the day,
All Life’s love, and all it’s mirth
Shrined in you, beside me lay.
You are gone.... And yet I feel,
Born anew in summer heat,
Thoughts of you within me steal,
Memories of pleasures sweet.
In my heart soft music throbs,
Like the lilt of some air sweet,
Played by bands afar, that sobs
Faintly down a silent street.
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Grief.
Night in her caravan as black as Death „
Came to my chamber, saying “Follow me,
Shut off the clamor of my startled breath —
Led me, red eyed, into Eternity.
O Grief, I died upon thy stinging lips,
Feeling the tempest of my soul arise,
Fronting the heavens as a ship that dips,
Cabled and cargoed with a freight of sighs!
God sat there spinning in a silent place,
Watching me walk the bitter past again ;
Wrinkled the blossom of my fair young face.
Bruised were my eyes with many stings of
pain.
“O woman that I made so very fair”—
The passion of His voice chained me to
earth —-
“Why hast thou crouched so constantly
with care,
Bartered thy body for a meed of mirth?”
Answered I nought. “O God, my very God,
How I have sinned! My body is a pool,
White as sweet wine, where multitudes have
trod,
Sipping the cup and calling me poor fool.
And in the morning of a splendid day
Love came unnoticed through the moving
throng,
Mouthed like a rose with eyes of holy grey,
Chanting the burden of an old sweet song.
God, O my God! I heard him knock in
vain—
How could I listen to a strain so sweet?
Grief drowns my longings in a moaning rain,
Leaves me to sorrow and the open street!
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
A Hospital Tragedy.
She looked a snorter as she lay in bed,
Although her pretty lips and cheeks were
white;
Her little hand was pressed against her head ;
“Poor dear,” the nurse had sighed, “she
groaned all night.”
I saw her first inside the slicing room
While Dr. Kutem sharpened up his knife —
I wondered if she was another’s wife.
I visited her thence onward every day
(What’s that —professionally? Of course,
of course),
And took her heart-beats in the usual way,
And then inspected her dear tongue, by
force.
The more I saw of her the better pleased
Was I; but soon (ah me!) a day there
came....
She left! I didn’t even know her name.
And many more have occupied that bed!
Since she I speak and dream of- went
away;
And some I’ve seen made better, and some
dead,
Much blood has flown from art’ries since
that day,
But none can come up to my brown-eyed
maid,
And even though I know she felt much pain
I hope —l hope she’ll soon get sick again.
N.S.W.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence'’ column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. Tiie Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 4, 1917.
CAN HUGHES SPEAK FOR
AUSTRALIA?
It is generally possible, even under the
Party System, for the head of a Government
to be regarded as at least nominally repre-
sentative of a majority of the people. But
this is not now the case in the Australian
Commonwealth. No sane person can imagine
that William Hughes as Prime Minister
represents to-day anything except an acci-
dent, Elaborate hair splitting about the con-
stitutional status of the Prime Minister,
about the significance of the recently-
expressed opinion of Parliament and all the
rest of it, amounts to nothing more than so
many efforts to give fiction precedence over
fact. For the moment, of course, the man’s
legal position is unimpeachable. The people
make politicians, and politicians, in turn,
make Ministries. By the luck of the game and
his own cunning, Hughes is thus where he
is. Under ordinary conditions the calamity
might be left to correct itself in the usual
way, or be straightened out by the electors
advancing on the ballot-box. But the condi-
tions are abnormal. At any time i.t would
be scandalous to have an unrepresentative
Government in office. At this period of
Australia’s history it is wicked. The
dangers inherent in such a position are now
magnified a hundred-fold. That the Hughes-
Pearce Administration would be on the box-
seat if the public could give expression to its
views is probably not believed by a single
member of the Federal Parliament. That
this is the Government Australia would have
if the Legislature were free to elect Ministers
of its own choice is a supposition too prepos-
terous for serious discussion. And serious
discussion of this Ministry and of the
prerogatives and duties of Parliament are
urgently needed. Every day’s postpone-
ment of this will be equivalent to trifling
with the nation’s future and a betrayal of
those democratic principles of which the
political mouth is accustomed to be so full.
A week or two ago the Federal Parliament
by a vote that was flagrantly dishonest—by
a vote that on both sides was cast with a
single consideration for party tactics and no
consideration for Australia—rejected a
motion of want of confidence in the Adminis-
tration. It was a vote -that meant nothing.
But Australia cannot be satisfied with a
travesty of her democracy such as this, nor
endure such an outrage upon popular gov-
ernment that a- vote to decide an amendment
of the income tax should determine issues of
vital concern to this Commonwealth.
Cook' and Irvine have called on the pub-
lic to realise the gravity of the hour. If
this talk is not shoddy, The Bulletin calls
upon them now to prove it with something
other than nebulosities. Is it within the
power of Cook or Irvine to produce in
simple language any justification for the
Federal Parliament leaving in the hands of
the Hughes Ministry the task of represent-
ing Australia —of speaking on behalf of Aus-
tralia, or making promises, or entering into
undertakings of any kind whatever, in the
name of this Commonwealth? Jf it is, they
should produce the argument. If it is not
possible, then they should explain how*
they can reconcile with duty to the
people the allegation that their busi-
ness is for the present to support the
Government. In this paper’s judgment the
paramount consideration should be the
authority of Parliament. It is sheer mad-
ness to suppose that the functions of the
Legislature are being discharged when
national affairs are left in the custody of an
unrepresentative Cabinet without appreci-
able following either in the Legislature or in
the country. The occasion is close at hand
when the views and feelings of Australia
will have to be with sobriety of
judgment—when the spokesman should re-
flect the opinions of a people instead of
parading his own personal notions. Are
there anv reasons why Parliament should
not be heard-from upon the great questions
of our national life? Are there any reasons
why the representation of Australia, instead
of resting on sound foundations, should be
dependent upon the hocus-pocus that oozes
from partisan organisations? This paper
knows of none. But it knows of a hundred
reasons why, because Australia is at war,
because the melting-pot is on the fire, and
because questions of incalculable importance
to the Commonw*ealth are taking shape in
the gloom and shadow, the Federal Parlia-
ment should be in session directing the
policy to be followed by the Ministry: and
there are as many good reasons why the
Government of Australia should at this time
be national in the very highest sense.
» * * *
The argument in favor of the Reps, and
Senate having a long holiday at this stage
was that members should have an oppor-
tunity to go on the stump after recruits.
The obvious answer is that there are other
people ready for that contract. The argu-
ment in favor of abandoning the holiday and
attending to business is that circumstances
have arisen that call for the opinion of
Parliament on a very material subject: and
it is imperative that negotiations of the
utmost importance on behalf of Australia
should not be conducted by a Government
which in no sense represents Australia.
A SCHEME FOR DRAWING THE
STING OF AVAR.
The persistence of human ideas through
long ages of chance and change is one of
those facts which must be taken account of
even when science is chortling her loudest
and up-to-date philosophy bows her acknow-
ledgment of the last round of applause. In
a remote corner of France the people still
worship the stones which were erected for
some sort of primitive religious observance
before the dawn of history. Those stones
were once red with the blood of sacrifice
to gods as rude as the primeval rocks; but
they continued to have power over the minds
of the succeeding race of civilised Celts in
spite of their elaborate pantheon of natural
and high-intentioned deities; and they still
have a superstitious hold on the newspaper-
reading Christian, who even presses the mod-
ern priests and his crucifix the pro-
cession around the monoliths which were
once a whole Pagan religious system in them-
selves. Ages ago church councils cursed
those stones, and cardinals excommunicated
them from the pale of decency; yet they
have lived down the curses, 'and superstition
works overtime around them to this day.
The fact is characteristic of human thought
and action. The world whirls on from
change to change, from horse-power to steam,
from steam to electricity, from electricity to
Heaven-knows-what; but a lot of things
come along with it, and are never wholly
cast aside. We still have crys.tal-gazers and
witchcraft and astrology applying their
fraudulent powers to the mystery of the Mel-
bourne Cup; we still have the ring-fetter on
the finger of the wife who "faces the Divorce
Court with a practised smile; and we still
have a strange faith in law as a cure for
social evils after 10,000 years have shown
that it is of no more value for the purpose
than turtle soup is of efficacy as a cure for
ringworm. Most persistent of all is the
amazing faith of mankind in war. Right
from the days when man wore a rudimentary
tail and grasped his prey with his hind foot
as well as his front one comes the belief that
if a lot of human creatures combine to inflict
all the punishment they can on a lot of other
human creatures some good must result. All
history shouts in about 450 different lan-
guages that it is a lie; but the world is as
deaf to that shout as it is blind to the fact
that cutting off its nose is a second-rate way
of being avenged on its face. War, stays
with us in spite of all our progress, and
Woodrow Wilson, even with Swiss assist-
ance, doesn’t seem to have discovered a likely
way to kill it.
* * * * *
Yet there is a very decent possibility that
war —at least the form of war that is loud
and bad-smelling and murderous—may be
brought to an end if it is tackled in the right
way. Most things have an Achilles heel of
some sort where the arrow of destruction will
bite even if fired by a person as futile and
soft-handed as Paris. If there is one thing
more remarkable than the persistence of old
ideas it is the utter oblivion into which old
forms and uses fall. Julian the Apostate,
big. clean man as he was, with an adminis-
trative ability worthy of his imperial job,
and a literary skill not often the possession
of a soldier of his size, could not restore the
old gods or get old Pagan talk a hearing.
Paganism was alive in spirit in the new
Christianity, but the world Had done with its
temples and altars for ever. "■ Similarly, in
the modern English courts, you couldn’t get
the ordeal by tire seriously considered. It
has gone out for good, though the element of
luck and chance, which was the essence of
the ordeal, remains in the temper of Judges
and the powers of endurance of juries. A
change of forms is not by any means an
easy matter to accomplish, but once it is
accomplished there is very little chance of
things ever taking the old shape again. The
liquor reformers of Scandinavia converted
Sweden and Norway from a universal drunk
on crude native spirits to a Softer and milder
sozzle on imported beer; blit they did not
shift the deeply-rooted northern desire for
soul-expansion through some sort of alcohol.
In tackling a problem like war the person
whose umbrella flutters ' with dove-like
enthusiasm and whose whiskers sprout in
olive-leaf designs should lay it down as a
basic principle that the war idea is per-
manent, but that the war forms may, by
strenuous effort, be permanently changed.
* * * *
The present war being the biggest in the
history of man offers a real chance of peace
to humanity—not an essential peace with the
element of war eliminated, but a practical
peace in which throat-cutting forms no part
of future controversy. To fight things out
must still be the last resort of angry nations.
But the way in which things are fought out
may-make all the difference. Wilson and
Switzerland, even with the assistance of the
other neutrals, are not likely to get results
by merely trying to call off the dogs as a
preliminary to rearranging the world. In
1914 the Teuton peoples stood forth armed
to the teeth and with murder in their eyes
yelling aloud to Heaven and earth and fate
for a licking suitable to the size of their
shameless treachery against mankind. Now,
with boots bloody and hands foul, they still
yell for the Same licking, and ale not by any
means satisfied with the foretaste of it they
have received. To stop such a war in mid
career is merely to keep things exactly as
they were. The peace possible to arrange at
such a time could only be a truce between
wars; and whether the truce was long or
short, the murder would start again in due
season. Promises to submit all things to
arbitration embodied in treaties would only
be so much waste paper, because arbitration
does not contain the essential principle of
war. The only hope for the world is the
invention of a form of war which, while it
can be waged to the point of exhaustion, will
get rid of the murder and destruction of
property which make present disturbances
horrible. To manage such an innoyation
would be difficult, but it should not .fee im-
possible. It should be within the bounds of
human ability to discover some gigantic
development of the game of bluff or poker,
on an international scale, by which all the
essential features of war- would be preserved
without its horror. Nations could stake
their wealth, their resources, their nopes,
their credit, their future on the skill and
bluff of Generals and diplomats just as they
do when the guns are roaring and the
stretcher-bearers toil with their loads of
pain. The whole •affair would be quite as
interesting, and the results quite as satisfac-
tory ; but the blood and smell and pain
would be largely cut out. Of course, there
would still be suffering. You couldn’t wage
conflicts of the sort without it; but the
whole thing would bear the exact relation-
ship to the cut-throat war we now know
that trial before an allegedly honest jury
does to the ancient ordeal by fire. As for
nations keeping faith about the things and
not cheating too flagrantly, that also could
probably be fixed up. Treaties are out of
date, but cash isn’t, and the nations might,
hand over to each other hostages of gold
coin and gilt-edged securities as guarantees
against foul play. There is room for the
big inventor to come along with a definite
scheme,; and as long as we remember that
war is war, and not croquet or ping-pong,
there is no real reason why the change of
form should not be permanent—that is, as
soon as the German has been thrashed into
brotherly kindness by the humanitarian
efforts of the Allies.
SYDNEY HARBOR SERVICES.
Some curses came home to roost in Sydney
last week; and Sydney did not lil?e them.
Successive increases have been made in the
cost of the season tickets issued by the co.
which has a monopoly of carrying people
across the harbor; and at the end of the
year, along with another rise in fares, came
a wholesale reduction in the services. By
half a dozen -State-owned train' and tram
routes people are poured down on to the
northern shore of the Harbor; and there the
Government leaves them to the tender mer-
cies of this monopoly. * When the Parlia-
mentary Labor party was young and uncor-
rupted it not only clearly saw its duty, but
was quite prepared to do it. Then the
party gradually cooled off. The northern
side of the Harbor sends very few Labor.men
to Parliament; find why, it asked, spend
money in carrying out the Labor platform
for the benefit of Liberals? So after five
years of Labor rule we still have the State
railways and tramways feeding this private
monopoly.
* * * *
For a while, when it looked as though the
Labor party would do something, there were
misgivings among investors; but when it was
made disgracefully clear that this monopoly
had less to fear from the Labor party'than
even from the Liberals, the share market,
with a cynical smile, recovered its confidence;
and there was a time when Sydney Ferries
shares were in such demand that the divi-
dend showed less than 2f per cent, on the
investment. The explanation was that the
co.. which had made a practice of hiding its
profits, was now in a position to frequently
water its stock—that is to say. to issue free
shares to the shareholders, paying for them
out. of the undivided profits. A couple of
years ago The Bulletin set out the his-
tory of the co. as follows: —
1893 — Old co. in existence; capital, £40,000.
1894 — Still the old co. ; capital watered to £60,000.
1899 —New co. formed; capital watered to £120,000.
1902—20,000 shares issued at par.
1906 — 20,000 free shares issued.
1907 — 40,000 shares at par.
1909 — Authorised capital increased from £200,000 to
£400,000.
1910— new shares issued ; 6s. per share paid
on them as a bonus ( £30,000 of water), and
4s. called up in cash.
1911— —-10 s. more called up on the last issue.
1912 — ‘lOO,OOO shares issued at par; ss. (£25,000)
called up.
1913 (.June) —Authorised capital further increased to
£BOO,OOO.
1913 (Dec.) —-£116,666 of reserves capitalised and
ss. per share ( £25,000) called up ori Shares
issued ih 1912.
In June, 1900, the eo. valued its assets at
£124,703. At the end of the year before the
war the total was £460,385. Thus the net
assets had increased by £335,682, of which
only £155,000 had come out of shareholders’
pockets. In other words, taking the vessels,
etc., as worth only book value, the public,
besides providing a dividend running from
10 to 15 per cent, per annum, had added more
to the funds of the co. than the shareholders
had done in 14 years. Now, of course,
because those rrofits have' been capitalised,
the public is expected to pay a dividend on
the money which it has itself provided; and
in order to maintain this dividend, while
meeting the increased' labor and other
charges, the directors have been steadily
pushing up fares and finding a limit there
have now begun cutting down services. The
whole business reflects very little credit on
anybody. It is a disgrace to the Labor
party, which, coming into power to national-
ise public utilities, not only failed to
nationalise this public utility, but continued
to spend large sums of money in feeding a
private monopoly. And it says little for the
intelligence of the Liberal party _ which
allowed itself to be bluffed out of doing_ its
duty to the _ people by the old cry against
“Socialistic interference with private enter-
prise,”
* * * *
Even now it is not too late to act. The
ferry services are not going to stay where
they are. There will be stock-waterings in
the future just as there have been in the
past; and even if the whole concern were
taken over at the present market value of
the shares, there would or should be some
immediate saving, because the State would
not require to extort 7 per cent, or more on
its investment. In any case, and whatever
should be the basis of purchase, it is certain
that this link between the State railway and
tramway systems on the two sides of the
Harbor should be taken out of private hands,
if for no_ other reason, then because the public
could kick with effect if anything went
wrong. Under present conditions the direc-
tors are under no obligation to consider any-
body but their masters, who are the handful
of shareholders.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“A Game Behind Closed Doors."
According to a London newspaper the invi-
tation extended to various Prime Ministers
to attend a conference in London will “exer-
cise a powerful influence on the imagination
of the oversea Dominions.” Massey, of
Maoriland, considers that the incident repre-
sents “a great, advance.” Joe Ward, of the
same place, who is running in double-harness
with Massey, announces that “we are
going to play a new game in Empire diplo-
macy and play it behind closed doors.” And
there is much tootling and footling about
the tremendous enthusiasm created by the
prospect of several mighty and
dominating personalities being summoned to
confer about something that is only vaguely
understood, and in any case, if all that has
gone before is not a dream, has already been
conferred about at great length. Now.
whatever ideas on the subject there may be
in London, it is only a fair thing to have
it understood there that all this banging of
the big drum and portentous talk about
Imperial responsibility is not at this end
of the world causing any joy, nor is it
likely to. The only advance that this coun-
try wants to see just now is an advance by
the allied armies. It js to assist in promot-
ing this that Australian troops are fighting.
If Maoriland is in the scrap for any other
purpose, no one has yet mentioned it. Cer-
tainly it has never been suggested that sol-
diers are going from here in order to create
conditions favorable to the invitation' of
wandering politicians to do something or
other behind closed doors. The people at
this end of the world are in the war be-
cause they were attacked, and their only
concern is to see the war won—to do the
best they can to help. If the operations at
the Front can be assisted by having more
conference, there is nothing to object to.
But it is not seriously alleged that this is
the case, and no one supposes that it is. The
whole business is, in the eyes of this country,
at any rate, surrounded with suspicion of
being an elaborate sham, the real design
being to drag the Dominions in an hour of
heat and passion closer to the net of an
organic Imperialism. It was said by
Hughes over and over again before he went
to London last year that the duty of Aus-
tralia in the war was to “do what we are
told.” _ To a great extent this has been the
governing idea in Maoriland, Canada and
South Africa. Criticism has been system-
atically throttled. In some respects it is a
penal offence. Under the stress of a great
war, discussion of the relationship between
Britain and the Dominions and colonies and
India has. been suspended. Is it to go on
now behind the backs of the people? Appar-
ently there are influences at work sufficiently
maniacal to encourage this back-door schem-
ing. It is a pity. The wash-up when the
war is over is going to be tremendous. It
would be much wiser, much more decent,
infinitely more patriotic, if efforts to win
the war engaged the undivided attention of
those men to whom the people look for
leadership. The war will have borne verv
evil fruit if it shall be found by the people
of this Commonwealth to have placed in any
Lou! a dagger to stab Australian nation-
alism.


The Red Tape that is Strangling Us.
A State Commandant recommended the
promotion of a junior assistant in the cleri-
cal branch of his Headquarters. The
anomalous position had a'risen that this
clerk, receiving £lO4 a year, was directing
the work of temporary clerks receiving £156
a year. The following is a precis of the
correspondence which thereupon passed
between State Headquarters and the Defence
Department, Melbourne :
June 12.—District Commandant recommends pro-
motion of junior assistant.
July 13. —District Commandant sends request for
reply to his communication.
July 17.—Secretary for Defence Department re-
fuses promotion on the ground that junior assistant
should pass through all intermediate grades.
July 24. —District Commandant again urges pro-
motion, pointing out that the official in question has
passed ail the necessary examinations to qualify for
proposed position, and that the work he is doing
should properly be done by higher-grade official.
August 17. —Reply from Adjutant-General’s office
to the effect that promotion is not considered neces-
sary, and suggesting that temporary assistance be
procured.
August 22.—District Commandant again urges
promotion. No other permanent clerk is available,
and it is impossible to procure efficient temporary
assistance. Suggests, failing approval of promotion,
that qualified permanent clerk be transferred from
another district.
August 28. Reply from Defence Department that
no permanent clerk is available for transfer.
September 14. —Junior assistant, whose proposed
promotion has caused all this correspondence, makes
personal application for promotion, pointing out
that he is qualified for promotion, and that clerk
in a similar position in another branch has been
promoted, though he failed to pass the necessary
examinations.
September 25.—Commandant writes to Defence
Department recommending junior assistant’s applica-
tion for promotion.
November 27. —Junior assistant asks for reply to
his application.
November 29.—Commandant writes to Defence
Department asking for reply to his recommendation.
December 3. —Commandant repeats request for
*^fccember 12. —Defence Department (letter signed
by i Brigadier-General this time) again refuses to
consider suggested promotion. Suggests that tem-
porary assistance be procured.
Two : cr three weeks ago this paper said
that, although men are so bitterly needed
at the Front, large numbers of apparently-fit
men are being kept in Australia at the
expense of the Defence Department. The
foregoing history of a case shows how they
are employed. Because a State Command-
ant has no authority to pay a clerk an extra
pound a week, though he has qualified by
examination for the rise and is supervising
the work of men who are getting more, the
whole lumbering machinery of the Depart-
ment goes on grinding out this absurd and
interminable correspondence. As a plain
matter of fact, the work of getting recruits
and preparing them for shipment to the
other side for training isn’t military work
at all. Half a dozen business men are
wanted for the job, which is one purely of
organisation. All the “military” we need
are a dozen or two sergeants. Instead, we
have an elaborate War Office which is wast-
ing hundreds of thousands of pounds because
it employs the wrong men to do things—
men who do things in the utterly discredited
“military” way when they clamor to be done
in a plain business way.
Billjim Spends a Bob.
The alarming attitude of Billjim on the
Somme, as shown by his devilish disregard
for the conventions on Christmas Day, has
caused Philip Gibbs, of the London Daily
Chronicle, to write home about it: —
The Australians and Maorilanders were observed
flinging money about in booths and market-places
upon trinkets ‘destined for a long sea voyage. These
fellows, wearing slouch hats, wandered into the best
restaurants in the region of the battlefields and
chose dishes without worrying about the price.
Here we have the blank amazement of the
average Briton that a common soldier should
have a penny to spend, or that he should
have any desire even at Christmas to get
something decent to eat, instead of thinking
himself lucky to half satisfy his hunger with
any old grub that might be filling. In the old
wars the soldier was mostly a miserable
beggar who went ragged and dirty until the
opportunity for plunder made him a million-
aire for a few minutes; and the tradition of
scarecrow heroes proud to salute their offi-
cers and remain hungry has remained as an
expected and natural feature of warfare.
If Australia has done something to break
down that tradition, the world owes a bit,
after all, to William. All up and down a.
thousand miles of trenches men are probably
asking why others can’t pay their soldiers
enough to buy a decent Christmas dinner
if Australia can ; and it will be asked with
more and more enthusiasm as time goes on.
The reply of the old military clique that
such high wages cannot be offered without
bringing the whole war industry to a stand-
still will be met with the laugh of derision.
For how patently monstrous it. is to tell
any man who is risking his life for his
country that it cannot afford to pay him a
decent wage at the very moment when it is
paying other men fine wages for staying at
home and risking nothing! How far-reach-
ing the consequences of this awakening may
be it is impossible to even guess. Certainly
one reason why wars have " been lightly
entered upon hitherto has been that the cost
to those who have not done the fighting has
been wretchedly small; often, indeed, war
has to them meant huge profits. We have
not yet wholly eliminated the profits; but
Australia has at any rate gone a good way
in the direction of assuring- to the fighter as
good a wage as, on the whole, he would get
if "he were a shirker. Now it remains to see
that the right people pay the bill. If it
falls on the right people, war is going to be
a - much less popular pastime than it was
when the class that did the fighting while
their families starved afterwards naid most
of the bill while their families went on
starving.

Can the P.L.L. Reform Itself?
While Octavian, Antony and Lepidus
schemed and bribed and fought for supre-
macy in the Roman world ancient
Senate talked and talked. When civil war
had raged through England, overturning
old and sacred things, beheading the theory
of the divine right of kings to govern wrong
in the person of Charles, and otherwise
manifesting to all who were not blind that
it was a season of change and action, the
Long Parliament still sat on and talked
until Cromwell ordered it out. The fact
that at the opening of the Inter-State Labor-
league conference there was not a single
Parliamentary representative from Victoria
and only one from N. S. Wales suggests that
history may be repeating itself, inasmuch as
Parliamentarians go on talking while the
P.L.L. smiles and prepares to smite them
to the dust. Unfortunately, however badly
the Parliamentarians have behaved, it
hasn’t yet- been shown that the P.L.L. or
the P.L.C. or whatever its name may be
is a democratic institution, founded on the
popular will and competent to perform the
Cromwellian service. Indeed it is a poor
thing as seen in its local groups. A
handful of people who subscribe a
small coin for the right of member-
ship gather in a remote corner and dis-
cuss political affairs in the name of tens
of thousands who are never consulted, and
who are seldom directly asked to join a
league. Anybody who could bring along a
hundred supporters, pay their subscriptions
and get them enrolled could sway the local
organisation to his will. Yet the aggregate
of these little hole-and-corner meetings
makes a power in the land which dictates
to * Parliaments and shapes the future of
Australia. As it thus stands, the P.L.L. is
a machine to defeat democracy ; and though
it has so far proved a very powerful machine
its weakness lies in the fact that at any
time it may be found out. Yet there is no
good reason why it should not reform itself
and broaden its own basis by making its
local leagues more representative. Hitherto
it has always been to the interests of the
Labor member for a district to keep the
League which selected him as a candidate
moribund between elections. If it awoke
to life in his absence it might easily de-
throne him in favor of another ; and this
attitude of the politician who has reached
Parliament has had a lot to do with the
hole-and-corner nature of the entire organi-
sation. Now that the Parliamentary person
has been mostly shot out, the chance for
better things seems to have come. The
widespread excommunications could well be
carried just a step farther. The Leagues
might all declare themselves bogus and start
afresh with the idea of bringing into the
fold the whole Labor intelligence of the
districts they represent. Then their voice
might be known as the voice of Labor and
not as the clamor of a few exploiters of a
slipshod system.
The Cost of Decorating Australian Feet.
As a general thing The Bulletin holds to
the opinion that Australians ought to be very
thankful just now to have any boots at all.
And it also thinks that there is a considerable
volume of disordered blither in circulation
about high prices. The- world has been
knocked endways, so to speak, by war,
and the price of nearly everything has
gone for a trip up. The same world
will probably be knocked upside down,
inside out and both ends together by
peace, and the prices of quite a lot of
things will fall with a dull thud, whereupon
there will be more moans of quite another
sort. Incidentally, the reason for giving
three cheers for dear coal and frequent groans
for dear boots is not altogether clear. Still,
that is no reason why people should have to
pay more for boots than represents a fair
thing in all the circumstances of the case.
To that extent the Federal Government is
justified in ordering an investigation of the
footwear business to find out if there are
any robbers about. But whether the Fed-
eral Government can do any good by try-
ing to fix the price of the finished boot is
another proposition. This paper does not
believe it can. Unless there is control of
production, supply and distribution, price-
fixing is futile. Had public enterprise
been given anything like a decent
chance in Australia, then, if the pub-
lic could not get a fair deal from private
manufacturers, State factories would turn
out reliable coverings for Australian feet.
That would be the sane, practical way of
doing something definite. But, even if the
boot trade is a thief, who can seriously advo-
cate State boot factories under the kind of
control everyone knows they would get?
How much time have alleged Labor leaders
devoted to consideration of this effective
method of market regulation and how much
have they wasted in gassing about means
that have been proved futile? Not that the
politician is wholly to blame. For if there
were State boot factories and shops the
probabilities are that they would be out on
strike five days a week. Until the com-
munity contracts horse-sense and gets down
to the bedrock of its problems and sees that
vague talk about Socialism is a wretchedly
poor substitute for work and brains, it can
expect nothing better than the piffling
devices of politicians in search of easy
methods of getting around difficulties.
The Army Remount Again.
“The Coper” : “Aneb’s” scornful remarks
on the remount question (B. 30/11/T6)
arouse my enthusiasm. After 25 years’ inti-
mate association with the horse in every
shape, from Cup winner to plough team, I
have come to the conclusion tha| the mili-
tary buyer is the curse of the horse-breeding
industry. He (or his principals) cannot
stick to one type of horse for two consecu-
tive months. Just 12 months ago I sold to
the military buyers low-set half-draught
horses for £25 a head. This year horses the
same size and up to two inches higher have
been rejected at a glance with the curt re-
mark “Too low.” Last sale (one of the big-
gest held in Australia) I took two trucks of
what I consider the best type of horse in the
world for ■ either farm or gun work. To
make up my number I threw in a cocky’s
generally-useful horse with a side bone. Re-''
suit: Side Bone topped the day’s sale to an
accredited military buyer, and the best price
for the rest was £13 —this for one of the best
active gun-leaders that I have had through
my hands for two years! A wise outsider
got him. How can the puzzled horse-breeder
keep pace with the market in these circum-
stances? From the time his mare is stinted
till the progeny is ready for the army five
years must elapse; and in that time the
jumpy Remount Department has probably
had 10 different heads, each with a different
idea of the type of horse required. An old
friend of mine, who has been in the game
for a generation, has turned it up this year,
giving as his reason that he "doesn’t know
what the fools want.” Why can’t the
Government fix a standard and stick to it?

Working the Ballot Box.
The prospect of having a couple of Federal
general elections in the near future, with
odd State elections in between, and possibly
a referendum or two in the immediate
vicinity, suggests that without any infringe-
ment .of the principle of the ballot some
easier and less disturbing method of ascer-
taining the people’s will must be found. The
cry frequently raised, on the alleged ground
of economy, that several elections or refer-
endum votes should be taken on the same
day, can hardly claim to be based on a
sound regard for the citizens’ interests. The
issues at each time of voting ought to be as
clear-cut as possible. If, as is asserted by
those who hold to the double-barrelled Par-
liamentary system, the Senate really has
functions which differ from those of the
House of Reps., then the selection of Sena-
tors is worth a bit of separate thought on
the part of electors. On this principle
Holman in N. S. Wales has just wisely
wrenched the local-option poll away from
the day on which the State Assembly is
chosen. On the other hand, there seems no
good reason why the whole continent should
be thrown into confusion because-a poll is
being taken. If the idea of making frequent
appeals to the people by referendum gets a
■firm hold, as it is likely to do, the voting
days would be far too numerous to be re-
garded as voting days are at present. The
country would have to vote in a simple and
orderly way at post offices or other perma-
nent polling-places. After all, the business
of recording a vote is a no more desperate
adventure than that of posting a letter, and
could be done with as little fuss. The Regis-
trar of Births, Deaths and Marriages with
the assistance of a local policeman could pro-
bably hold the lid on the ballot-box to the
satisfaction of the community; and the elec-
tors could vote as systematically and un-
ostentatiously as they milk their cows or
miss their trains.
Soldiers’ Comforts.
“Fax” : Whatever be the fate of patriotic
subscriptions in other Australian centres, it
seems certain that some of the funds handled
in Melbourne are supplying other wants
than they were meant for. Notably the
fund managed at the Town Hall. All the
goods issued from there were, and still are,
supposed to go to the boys on active service
abroad. Their destination ought to be the
trenches, and other fighting places where
a consignment of “comforts” comes as
practical consolation and a message of soli-
citude from home. Instead the fruits of the
fund are largely bestowed on soldiers in
Melbourne. Kits, more or less luxurious,
are packed for smart khakied applicants who
may or may not be going to the war and
who will be supplied with a kit by the
Defence Department if and when they do go.
In excuse for the management it is
stated that officers of the Defence
Department are responsible for many
“local” applications. Batches of sol-
diers are sent along to the Town Hall
with orders for the goods. Why: How
comes it that certain soldiers in camp are
privileged to draw upon the proceeds of a
patriotic fund whilst their neglected brother
Biixjims have to rest content with the
regulation allowance of necessities? The
scandal calls for public ventilation. Libel-
lous rumor asserts that there is a regular
system of sale or barter in Melbourne of
“comforts” which should be available only
to soldiers who are out of the country.
Another aspect of the Northern Territory:
The recent Houtman wharf-laborers’ strike at
Darwin was as queer a titbit as you could find
anywhere. Few nationalities are not represented in
Darwin. At Vesty’s works alone 20 are repre-
sented —Greek, Spanish, Russian, even Patagonian.
And Darwin was on this occasion held up because a
Dutch captain said, or was supposed to have said,
“Serve him right” when a Russian lumper fell
overboard. The majority of the other lumpers,
Greeks, at once decided to suspend work until the
skipper publicly abased himself and apologised. The
Dutchman, supported by his general manager, was
not going to apologise to “a lot of Dagoes.” He
had, he said, to consider how the people of Holland
would take it. The refusal resulted in the complete
cessation of work, paralysing the Territory’s busi-
ness. As the Dutchman remained obstinate a meet-
ing was called to arrange for a general strike. Ninety-
five per cent, of those present voted in favor of it,
although not 80 per cent, could understand what
was said. All preparations were at once put in
hand for a blow that was to shake everybody out
of the Territory.
Then came the thunderbolt: the next morning
disclosed the fact that the Dutchman had lifted his
anchor and gone, taking with him the long-awaited
food supplies for the inhabitants, the drugs for the
hospital, the cement for the Pine Creek line, and
(which meant the turning-off of many men from
work) the piles for the new part of the jetty,
delaying the workmen for another month. And,
worse than all, all the beer for the hotels. The
Chinese storekeepers, the friends of the Darwin
unionists and their consistent supporters, were the
chief sufferers, for they had run out of flour. Hun-
dreds of the unfortunate settlers away in the back
country, who are doing their best (if any are) to
help along the Territory, will now lack food because
the wet season has set in and transport is next to
impossible.
The foreigners in the Northern Territory are neithc r
a success nor a blessing, and as Steak-an-da-Oyst is
the most frequent and can dominate the Britishers
in a British settlement simply because he pays £1
a year to be a British unionist —not a British subject
—-there is going- to be some lively times, I am
thinking, if war is declared against our kind friend
King Tino. For Greeks are arriving by every boat,
and already there is a Greek quarter.
Old superstitions do die hard. The N. S.
Wales Congregational parsons have just
resolved that the Law should compel every
male candidate for marriage (why “candi-
date,” anyhow—is marriage a junior exam,
or a horse race?) to produce a medical cer-
tificate of his freedom from venereal disease.
Presumably the reason the female candidate
is exempted is that the rev. gentlemen don't
care to insult a lady by implying that she
may become disease-stricken. If that is the
reason, then one sees once more at work the
venerable superstition that syphilis is the
“wages of sin.” The much more dreadful
truth is that one doesn’t necessarily insult
a woman by asking her to prove herself free
from syphilis, any more than one does by
requesting her to produce evidence that she
isn’t consumptive. Either disease may arrive
by accident, quite unsuspected, and to
absolutely innocent persons; and thousands
more probably inherit it —maybe from per-
sons who have also innocently contracted it.
There.isn’t necessarily any more disgrace in
having syphilis than there is in catching
(say) typhoid or smallpox. The disgrace
arrives in concealing any of these diseases,
and so risking the passing of them on. When
the parsons are able to realise this, and
when they thump their pulpits violently
enough,- then the good work of reform will
really get ahead. Meanwhile it is encourag-
ing to see that at last some of them are
game to mention the beastly subject ip
public without blushing to the ends of their
whiskers.
The evidence given by engineer Thomas
Hill, of the Home Affairs Department, to
the Canberra Inquiry Commission, explains
why matters didn’t proceed satisfactorily in
one respect at least. He said that he had
exercised no control in the selection of arti-
sans. Union secretaries in Sydney and Mel-
bourne were asked to send workmen and did
so. Many of them naturally proved unsatis-
factory ; a few deliberately practised the “go
slow” ’ policy. It was a condition of then-
employment that they should be returned at
the expense of the Commonwealth, and in
some instances their fares came to more than
the money they earned. Altogether, what a
beautiful system! And why spoil it by
obtruding Thomas Hill? Why not frankly
abolish engineers and foremen, let the men
select themselves, supervise themselves, pay
themselves, use what stores and material
they think fit. and have a jolly and fat time
generally? If we openly avowed that policy
we should at least be more honest than we
are to-day.
The Coming of Peace.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Peace wept within a quiet place alone,
Her ragged apron thrown about a face
That once a heedless world had lightly
known,
And, blind to all its perfect loveliness,
Cast on it foul aspersion and disgrace.
Earth deemed the wrongs that shrieked for
swift redress
Were of her working, that her robe unsoiled,
Whose dove-soft drapings moved like a
caress
And shimmered in the glory of the sun,
Was washed with tears of many hearts
despoiled.
A fearful purpose seemed to serge and run
Through lands and peoples. Wrath and
terror rose
And set the linstock to the waiting gun,
While Death with chattering jaws applauded
loud
And Valor perished, hissing at her foes.
Peace wept in silence; while the armies
proud
Made earth a reeking shambles far and wide.
And through the lightnings of the murder-
cloud
Tremendous agonies of pride and pain
Shook the pale nations as their bravest died.
Men dreamt of Peace in midnight tossings
vain,
Longing with passion for her touch and
smile,
Fearing her dead forever, while the rain
Of blood and tears soaked still the drinking
sod,
And shame linked taloned hands with force
and guile.
Then rose the harlot Talk with beck and
nod,
Draping white linen round her purple shame
To hide a thousand stains from earth and
god,
And loudly crying that her name was
Peace —•
Pure Peace returning free from scorn and
blame.
Red War gazed rudely on his beldam niece
And laughed to hear the rolling battle-call
Of his ten thousand cannon still increase.
“Ho! Ho!” he cried, “she acts so meekly
well
My sword shall drink until the curtain-fall.”
But, tortured wise and keen through yeans
of hell,
The peoples gazed upon the woman’s eyes
And knew her but too shrewdly and too
well —•
Her painted cheek, her garment’s snowy lie,
Murder’s last lure for dreader enterprise.
Then, with a shout that shook the angry
sky,
They rent her raiment from her where she
stood,
And pressed more wildly towards the flames
to die,
Or live that the true Peace might come
again.
And grim War trembled for his hardihood.
He trembled, shrank and failed. The bitter
rain
Of fire-urged steel made Horror fly afeared.
War died in agonies of larger pain.. . .
And Peace looked out upon the world and
saw
Her ancient standard o’er the ruin reared.
While Talk, the harlot, nursed a broken
jaw.
N.S.W.

Bayonets and Shells are still Trumps.
The yarns about the emptiness of the Ger-
man’s pantry may be right. Also, they may
be wrong. Being the kind of thing the
British people want to hear, the story of the
neutral “commissioner” loses nothing in
saleable value on the London market when
he reports that in every place he visited
the local Hun had long since parted with
his bingy and sacrificed the family poodle to
the Fatherland. Anyhow, if others are going
short of some things, the Allied armies are
not fighting against hungry troops. The
prisoners taken on the Somme are stated to
have been well nourished and warmly clad,
and the dugouts they inhabited heavily
stocked with reserves of grub. So long as
things are like this and the German army can
hold its end up, the kitchens at Hamburg
and Cologne are matters of little concern to
Britain. In any case, reference to the papers
of 18 months ago shows that the accommo-
dating neutral was even then keeping
London supplied with yarns suspiciously
like those of to-day. There were the same
old forecasts, too, about internal revolution.
These looked as poor then as they seem
now. Discontent is no doubt acute all over
Germany. The trials of the people must be
terrible. But revolution there, while the
German armies are in the field, fighting on
foreign soil, is as unlikely as the arrival
from Heaven of a few thousand fat bullocks
addressed to Von Hindenberg. Meanwhile
Notes are flying about in all directions.
Having repudiated the first effort of the Ger-
man to induce all hands to sit down and
talk about the blessings of peace, the Allies,
after telling him what they thought of
Kultur generally, are leaving the next move
to William. After further sparring for
wind, it is not improbable that something
definite may be produced. But that the
world will escape from its present hell with-
out more and terrible bloodshed is no better
assured than that Gabriel will tootle on his
trump to-morrow evening.

D. E. Hutchins, whom the Westralian
Government brought from Africa a year or
two ago to advise about forestry, hands a
thistle to all the States, but particularly
N. S. Wales, Queensland and Tasmania, for
the Avay they have treated their timber
resources. The ignorance shown in N. S.
Wales he describes as appalling, and adds: —
What heightens the crime of misgovernment in
N. S. Wales is the loss of its unequalled softwoods.
Before long the State’s hideous waste of its forest
resources may be expected to be costing at the rate
of £BOOO per day. All this has been stated by the
Conservator of Forests in season and out of season.
But the Government heeds not, and each year gets
more deeply involved.
Of Queensland he observes: —
With the forests in their present state a forest
expenditure of only £7OOO per year stamps the
present forest policy of Queensland as simply dis-
astrous in its national sense.... The Queensland
Government of to-day is robbing the State of its
richest asset for a comparatively paltry temporary
gain. When these facts become generally known
Queensland must appear before the world in sorry
guise, its fair credit dragging in the mud.
Yet, though it would seem difficult to picture
anything worse than that, Hutchins
declares that Tasmania is a viler sinner
still. The Speck is an almost bankrupt
State, the most heavily taxed in Australia,
with a handful of inhabitants; yet it ought
to be rich and prosperous, working valuable
and easily-accessible timber, the Norway of
the South. Even in their present depreci-
ated condition the forests would, Hutchins
says, pay the national debt. We have heard
all that before, though. What we don’t so
often hear is how they are to be made to do
it.
“8.D.”: All those people who are demand-
ing the compulsory notification of venereal
disease seem to take it for granted that the
victim always knows just whe.n he is a vic-
tim and what his ailment is. But the
medical profession is finding out how little
really is known about it. Twenty per cent,
of the male inhabitants of the city, 75 per
cent, of the females, and close on 50 per cent,
of the men from the Lush, with a greater
number of women, would not recognise
syphilis or gonorrhoea if they saw it. The
most sophisticated, of course, are those who
run the risk of infection by patronising the
street fairies; but those who have inno-
cently acquired it are slow to believe it when
they spot the first symptoms, even if they
know them. Very few of the perverts who
indulge in solitary vice realise that they
can contract gonorrhoea that way, too.
W hat is wanted is a printed placard setting
out the forms of the disease, the first symp-
toms, all the ways in which it can £>e
acquired, and where to go for treatment. The
notifications could be placed in lavatories
and other exclusive haunts of men or
women, as is done successfully in California.
It is no good telling a man what book to
buy to read about it. You have to give
him the information first-hand to impress
upon him the necessity for early treatment.

■
Sidney Criterion is happy once more.
“The House of Glass,” by Max Marein, pro-
duced there on Saturday night, is another of
the detective-criminal dramas that seem to
be the chief product of the U.S.A. It de-
serves to last no longer than any of the
others of the monotonous series. The first
act is merely the arrest of a young burglar
and the heroine’s discovery of his false pre-
tences just as she is -.on the point of
eloping with the cheerful young rotter. In
the second act we learn that she has been
convicted on circumstantial evidence of
being in leagpe with him. But the explana-
tion of this amazing development is kept a
secret from the audience till the last act;
and then it is quite an unsatisfactory explan-
ation. Instead of waiting till the man she
loved came out of gaol, the girl, released,
after a short term, on probation, and ordered
to report herself to the police every month,
clears to Kansas City and marries a young
man who subsequently rises to a position of
power. She realises she is safe as long as
she does not return to N’Yark, where the
cool, callous, sneering detective is implacably
waiting for her. But her husband accepts a
position of greater power in the home burg
of the detective of the steely glance. And
the long arm of the police captures her, now
the wife of a millionaire. The detective
turns out to be incorruptible; he cannot be
bribed ; he is not even the father of twins.
But the author, who has gone out of his
way, in the “curtain” of the first act, to
deceive the audience as to the detective’s
character, has to get his heroine out of her
trouble. He desperately calls in a dens ex
machina, who is ludicrously unconvincing;
and the heroine is freed. But the unsavory
moral is left that if she had been the wife
of a poor man she would have had no chance.
The conclusion spoils a poorly-constructed
melodrama.
Louis Kimball, as the burglar hero, is
admirably fitted for a part calling for subtle
acting and a big reserve of power. His sur-
“THE CINEMA STAR,” AT SYDNEY
MAJESTY’S.
Lord Clarence Wentworth (Stanley
Odgers) and Little Glutterbuck (Phil
Smith).
face lightheartedness and his growing
burden of fear, in the first act, are admirably
suggested ; in the scene where he meets the
girl, now a society woman, he succeeds by
finished acting in hinting the emotion he is
unable to show; and his passionate declara-
tion in the final act is a brilliant and con-
vincing display of emotional force. Frank
Harvey, the husband, has little to do but sit
with clenched hands and brood over his
agony, except when he is the Napoleon of
Railroads and says “No !” with pitiless deter-
ruination. Of course, lie clenches and
answers in the negative admirably. Boyd
Irwin’s long record of detectivism is against
him; with the assistance of the dramatist
he succeeds in deceiving the audience up to
the last moment. Arthur Styan is reliable in
a minor part; and Robert Greig furnishes
the amazing spectacle of a good fairy. His
lighter pirouettings are more convincing
than his final noble pose. Maggie Knight
makes the sort of landlady that every boarder
vainly dreams of. Florence Rockwell proves
herself a capable liglit-comedy actress in the
first scene, where she charmingly suggests
the gaiety and happiness of the young girl in
love. But when the play wantonly turns to
melodrama, she uses all the conventional
tricks of the harassed heroine without any
sign of genuine emotional power behind
them. Her make-believe signally fails to
convince.

Now that the Liquor Saving Bill has cut
off five hours of whisky, the best “peg” any-
one can legally have after 6 p.m. in Sydney
is the one at the Palace Theatre.
On Saturday night the Bert Bailey
players, at Sydney Royal, get back to “On
Our Selection” for 12 nights. “Mum Daw-
son, Boss,” the Sumner Locke play, follows.
“The Cinema Star” glitters prosperously
at Sydney Majesty’s, and the Royal Comics
have that settled look that comes over a co.
when the course is cleared for a long run.
The people of “The Passing Show” are
still gaily on the job at Sydney Tivoli. Syd-
ney James and Madeline Rossiter lead and
a wave of variegated talent pours along
behind. The interesting Belle Oliver is
somewhere near the crest of the wave.
Sydney films of note: —
Crystal Palace : “Her Father’s Son.” The adven-
tures of Bill Marion Edwards are trifling compared
with those of the boy-girl Vivian Martin, of Vir-
ginia. [On second thoughts, if Australian Bill
M.’s adventures were told and illustrated those of
the Yankee girl-hoy might look trifling and insig-
nificant. P.S. —To Showmen: No extra charge for
this hint.]
Lyceum : “The Beggar of Cawnpore.” An episode
of the Sepoy mutiny—which wasn’t much of a
mutiny, anyhow.
Hoyt’s: “The .Italian”; character-actor George
Beban as star.
At Melbourne Princess’s the out-and-out
Duke of Gloucester continues to butcher a
dethroned king to make a Christinas holiday.
Also he gives orders for his two nephews to
be killed and buried in the Tower, and he
provides his wife with a discreet family
physician who can be warranted to kill
within 15 days or thereabouts. Moreover,
lie is dimly understood to be responsible for
the assassination of brother Clarence, but
nobody bears witness to this fact except
the Duke’s ghost, who haunts him in his
sleep. The only melodramatic shortcoming
in Cibber’s version of “Richard III.” is in
the omission of Clarence and his Dream
speech, and the two murderers who decide
to drop him in the Malmsey butt and make
a sop of him. Otherwise it contains a bit
more than immortal Shakspeare intended.
Allan Wilkie’s production has some good
pictures in it. The first impressive scene of
Crosby Hall, with the young prince in the
chair in the foreground, is not more pic-
turesque than the next view of it, where
Richard has a tall seat in the background,
The exterior of the Tower and the interior
of the Royal Tent where the spooks come
and talk to wicked Wilkie are scenes that
hit the eye, and the tableau of the Battle
of Bosworth gets a double encore. But the
encores are managerial errors, in this jour-
nal’s opinion. The tableau should be one
fleeting spectacle and no more. A first
impression is enough. Afterwards the spec-
tutor gets used to the broad effect and pro-
ceeds to criticise the battle in detail and
count the casualties,

At Melbourne Royal the supply of “Dam-
aged Goods” will be suspended on Friday!
The bill for Saturday is “The Woman,” a
