“THE HOUSE OF GLASS,” AT SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Boyd Irwin and Carleton Stuart brow-
beating the noble-hearted thief.
“The House that Jack Built,” ajt Mel-
bourne Majesty’s, continues to be a necklace
of picturesque irrelevancies hanging on the
skeleton of a nursery rhyme. The panto. - has
the dash and inconsequence of previous hardy
annuals, and only recollects that it has a
plot when plump Fairy Industria or the
gaudy Demon of Misrule turns up to remind
Jack that he has a serious purpose in life.
As Jack’s job is to restore someone’s busted
fortune and wed the disguised royalty,
Gretehen Yates, he has plenty of chances to
figure as a revue time-killer in some of the
. featured interruptions. The interpolated
novelties this year seem as numerous as
before, and range in quality from the poetic
imaginativeness of Maggie Dickinson’s danc-
ing to the frank vaudeville belligerency of the
song about the Tanks; Some of the items
are knockabout turns of a moderately lively
description; a couple make pointed appeals
to the patriotism of the race ; and one by the
Rough but not Reckless Ritchie team is
merely a procession of weird bike shapes
apparently invented by an eccentric lunatic
suffering from an overdose of Euclid. But
those things are only secondary and unimpor-
tant beside the show’s greatest success—the
ocean of Girl that pours into the Tulip
Farm, glitters and whirls in the Ballet of
the Seasons, and finally bursts in a stream
of enchanting comeliness into the flag-
infested tableau of Empire. In its feminine
element “The House that Jack Built” might
well be likened to a luscious fruit-salad, with
peaches and plums as the prevailing ingre-
dients.

Fred Bluett, who has returned to Mel-
bourne Tivoli from England, has left some of
his voice and whimsicality behind him. His
warble is the ghost of what it was and his
humor is sickbed o’er with the stale past of
brighter _ Cockney comedians who have gone
before him. Two Yankee new arrivals, the
Flying Weavers, show the great Amurkan
Bite in a strange variation of the iron-
jawed act. The specialty has some pictu-
resque features, but mostly amounts to
monotonous rotation and shedding of gar-
ments whilst hanging by the teeth in mid-
air. The Follies are spread in reckless
profusion everywhere, and the management
continues to claim that there is not a dull
moment in the entertainment. Well, that's
the management’s business.
The Dandies remain a happy, untroubled
family at St. Kilda (Vic.). Pearlie McKen-
zie continues to advertise the harmless but
elevating sentiments of childhood, and Ealla
Wtu'd is showing her teeth in mushy ballads
“RICHARD III.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When York and Lancaster were names
Indicative of strife,
And skulls were cracked in tnerrie games,
And girls were “maids,” and wives were “dames”
Throughout their married life;
When belted earls shook flaxen curls
And sported silken hose,
And knights and lords wore gilt-hilt swords
To pink their caitiff foes,
And no one called his soul his own,
King Richard the Third sat on the throne.
A monarch’s might was might indeed
When Dickon did his dash,
To satisfy his royal greed
He’d often shed a traitor’s bleed,
Or settle someone’s hash ;
He’d win with guile a widow’s smile,
Whose husband he had slain,
Or curse and damn friend Buckingham
Who sued to him in vain.
A monarch reigned, and reigned alone,
And spoke in a commanding tone
When Richard the Third sat on the throne.
But oh, the tyrant’s time was brief:
He reaped what he did sow!
The world was not so full of grief,
So full of woe beyond belief
Four hundred years ago.
Those traitors then were titled men ;
Base barons fought and died;
No Kaiser’s roar could start a war
And drain his subjects of their gore
To gratify his pride.
Say, has the world much wiser grown
Since Richard the Third sat on the throne ?
VICTORIA.

Brock is broadening visibly. All last week
the rising silver in the thermometer was
producing a similar phenomenon in the box-
office.
At Melbourne Auditorium a disappointed
husband is still putting the question “Where
Are My Children?” to a foolish, frivolous
wife who can only answer “Dunno.” The
silent message of the film continues to be
delivered four times daily, and the brief blue
patches, wherein the Pearly Gates open and
angels hover against a murky background of
eternity, add to the terrible solemnity of the
sermon. Simple people who view the pic-
ture are naturally much impressed by the
realistic visions of Heaven and the angels.
Apparently they accept the unearthly fea-
tures as a sort of guarantee that’the story
has been prepared with the approval, if not
the connivance, of Providence.
Hoyt’s latest screenings in Melbourne
include a Fox version of “Merely Mary
Ann,” with the much-paragraphed Vivian
Martin in the title role. Zangwill’s comedy
.has been carefully adapted for the camera,
but Vivian is not the girl for Mary. Her
trivial prettiness and turkey-lolly smile turn
the slavey into an automatic ingenue of the
Amurkan uppah classes. Another Hoyt
offering is a long Thomas Ince production
called “The Mating.” Bessie Barriscale, a
movie beauty formerly connected with the
Lasky firm, is a discreet ogler in this produc-
tion. At the Olympia Hoyt’s are also
showing up that villain Derrink in a preten-
tious study of thirst and shattered nerves
advertised as “The Power of Evil.”
* * * *
Touching “The White Slave Traffic,” at
Melbourne King’s, it is only necessary to
add that Miss Adrienne has arranged her
superabundant tresses in a becoming coiffure,
and that the strange mixture of lurid drama
and milk-and-water comedy will be with-
drawn this week. On Saturday the same
people will bid for yells of laughter with
“The Accidental Honeymoon,” which is to
hold the stage for a week ere Allen Doone
happens once again.
“Damaged Goods” is to be played in
Adelaide, probably at the Royal; and mur-
murs of the storm to follow can already be
heard in the newspapers. The public did
not rush “While the Billy Boils” at the
King’s, but is making up for lost time now.
Some of the same players appear in 'the
afternoons in “Seven Little Australians,” a
pleasant stage-version of (mostly) “Miss
Bobby.” Vera Pauli, clever and pretty, is
honestly too old now to look like a kid of
13, but a big score is made by Cecil Haines.
By the way, the item of cropping Bobby’s
locks needs to be stage-managed a bit, being
quite unconvincing at present. “The Power
and the Glory” at the Tivoli brings in a
Christmassy touch with a transformation
scene near the‘end. The Majestic includes
two good playlets, one by King and Miss
Thornton, one by the clever “Bunty” Scotch
lot. Beerbohm Tree is on the screen at
West’s, Wondergraph daringly promises a
“red-plague” drama, and the Town Hall is
now in the field with a striking film in
which America seeks to convince Australia
of the righteousness of lynching niggers.
From an Adelaide critic: —
Nelson Wood has had a quiet one-man show of
brush-work. It is a regular Curate’s Egg of an
exhibition, a couple of heads first-class, others quite
crude, a bit of tea-tree brilliant, a piece of strong
figure-work for an exhibition of manufactures, a good
crayon or two, and all these mingled with other
things nowhere near the same class. Mr. Wood has
done and is doing good work in support of the
Tourist Bureau’s campaign, for South Aus. has only
lately woke up to the number of beauty-spots it
might boom. And as a theatrical scene-painter he
might, given the chance, be well in the lead.
Private Hugh Alplionsus Leffler, accident-
ally killed on active service in France, was
a fine young Australian who heard the call
above the clatter of The Bulletin’s
machine-department. Tall, wiry and upright
as a cornstalk, Leffler had only tasted 24
years of sunshine and brightness when he
went under in the Great Cause.

“Star” : Even death did not end the per-
secution of Cliidley. One departmental doc-
tor found it necessary to state at the
coroner’s inquest that syphilis was a contri-
butory cause, though strangely enough the
three other medicos did not mention the fact,
and one of them has stated that such a
diagnosis was rubbish. Naturally what the
one doctor said was set out in large type by
the daily press, which eagerly availed itself
of this chance of further vilifying the unfor-
tunate whose worst crime was that he
wouldn’t wear trousers.

“Mujik” : Would it not be possible to send
Hughes to deal with Constantine, and let us

When the transport Itonus was sunk by a
submarine in the Mediterranean the Huns
made the master a prisoner and landed the
crew. If the skipper was the same as when
the vessel was last in Australian ports, then
his name is Colin McDonald, and he’s a well-
known seadog in these waters. The Germans
in their campaign to cripple the British mer-
chant service are holding all the shipmasters
they can lay their hands on, and if the
threatened under-water campaign is prose-
cuted in its promised frightfulness, there are
hundreds of sailormen over the regulation
age who will get a chance they have been
waiting for. It’s an ill torpedo that doesn t
blow somebody good.
“Green Chartreuse” : The corridor of the
Vic. Chief Secretary’s office in the closing
days of December resembled the passage from
the stage door of a theatre which is recruit-
ing for the chorus. It was crowded with
bright girls, slight girls, arch girls and jolly
girls, all anxious to register themselves as
barmaids under the new Licensing Act.
Three forms had to be filled in for each girl,
and the impressionable Chollies of the Civil
Service made it a labor of love. One dam-
sel poutingly claimed to be physically fault-
less —a ewe lamb without blemish —when
asked, purely for identification purposes, of
course, whether she had any slight moles
or birthmarks. Her word was taken for it.
Another young thing, the color of whose hair
was being logged, hinted that it might
change if fashion demanded it. Behold a
barmaid in whom there is no guile! It is
unseriously stated that there are 775 appli-
cations before the Repatriation Committee
from returned soldiers who are anxious to
get jobs as Inspectors of Barmaids or Comp-
trollers of Birthmarks.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”: Tl\ere’s a line in “School for
Scandal” which always tickles the fancy of
yonng wives who have married something
elderly—“for the position.” When Sir Peter
Teazle asks whether a husband is not to
have influence and authority over his little
bit of fluff, his sprightly spouse replies:
“Authority ! No, to be sure! If you wanted
authority over me you should have adopted
me and not married me. I am sure you were
old enough.” The cynicism cuts both ways,
of course. Probably Ellen Terry was think-
ing of it when she spoke of her juvenile
third husband at a Melbourne tea-table. “I
was wrong in marrying that young man,”
said Ellen, with a humorous shake of the
head. “I ought to have adopted him.” Thus
the whirligig of Time brings in his revenges.
When Ellen Terry was the wayward young
bride of staid, middle-aged painter Watts,
she out-Teazled the heroine of Sheridan’s
comedy, and Watts soon regretted that lie
didn’t adopt the fascinating rebel instead of
marrying her.
George eiorgan, an Australian seaside-
show comedian of a while ago, has been
doing fairly well since he went to England,
lie got engagements as a handy man in
revues, and three months ago was booked
for juvenile lead in a co. which was about
to tour “The Private Secretary.” But
under Lloyd George’s new National Service
Act young man Morgan will probably have
to make himself useful off the stage.
“Mario” : Tempe Pigott, who introduced a
good many of Bernard Shaw’s amusing folk
to Australian audiences, and left last May
for New York, w r rites from Rochester, where
“Peg o’ My Heart” (with a Morosco co.)
is drawing like “East Lynne.” “Peg,” says
Miss Pigott, is fast taking the place of the
old weepy drama as the most reliable liouse-
filler in U.S.A. Miss Pigott plays the dis-
agreeable Mama.
“C. Ockney” : Although “Damaged Goods”
is vetoed, the works of Eugene Brieux have
had a fair show on the London stage. “La
Robe Rouge” has long been known, through
the translation of actor-manager Arthur
Bourchier, as “The Arm of the Law.”
Bourchier plays the part of the judge of
assize, and Violet Vanbrugh (Mrs. B.) the
character originally taken by Rejane. A ver-
sion of “La Femme Seule” was done by the
wife of Bernard Shaw and given the inelegant
title “Woman on her Own.” When staged
at a special performance at an outlying
metropolitan theatre the play justified its
title, there being hardly a man amongst the
audience. Another Barney—Fagin, to wit —
was responsible for the translation of “La
Foi” (“False Gods”), which Beerbohm Tree
put on at His Majesty’s. Tree mounted the
Egyptian piece sumptuously and cast it
with care, but the frost was severe and it
soon succumbed to the arctic conditions.
Clyde Cook, the dancing comedian who
deserves and gets such a big show in “The
Cinema Star,” had to go away and rattle
his feet in London for several years before
the J.C.W. people were aware of his worth.
Now behold him fresh from London dancing
on his native heath in a show imported from
the same place. Still Australia, thank
Heaven, can produce something. It at least
grows the J.C.W. audiences

“Peter”: A feature of Sir Thomas
Beecham’s new opera season at London Ald-
wycli is the conducting of Aylmer Buesst, a
member of a Melbourne musical family.
Buesst, who is alleged to be the first Aus-
tralian to command an opera band in
London, has a brother, Victor, a charming
pianist, who is now fingering lint and ban-
dages in the R.A.M.C. in Egypt.
“De Oriel:’: At the Melbourne Bijou re-
cently a helpless comedian failed easily to
extract laughs and guffaws from the audi-
ence. Meanwhile a man in the dress-circle
box fell asleep. A stray rat, in full view
of the audience, but concealed from the
stage, climbed the proscenium and jumped
hastily upon the head of the slumberer, who
awoke suddenly and almost fell into the
stalls. The audience screamed and applauded
vociferously. The artist (who seemed taken
by surprise) bowed continuously, but the
rat didn’t return for an encore.
“Row A.” : Australia surely produces the
most patient theatre audiences in the world.
Accustomed to yell or hoot his displeasure
at outdoor sports, the Australian sits glumly
through a poor indoor show, applauding a
little now and then out of sheer charity.
Then he passes out silently to warn Ins
friends, and the deluded manager who
imagines that he has taken the bait nicely
and that he is in for a decent season finds
his neglected show dying on its legs. Theatre
audiences elsewhere rage and curse if they
are afflicted with the wrong goods, and “the
bird” is publicly awarded to the performers
who deserve it; the Australian endures in
stoic silence, and then damns it privately.
It is months since an outrageously inferior
show was “birded” in Sydney; but the
city has suffered a lot since without making
a sign. I went to a pretentious entertain-
ment the other night that put the breaking
strain on the house several times. The
heroes and heroines present made no moan
while the curtain was up—only denounced it
during the intervals. It seems to me that
this splendid reticence retards artistic pro-
gress. We get the good stuff in small doses
by accident; the bad stuff is fed to u» in
barrowloads.
“De O.” : German vaud-artists have sud-
denly become French or English. A Con-
tinental mimic, who used to be Hans but
is now Henri, after cleverly impersonating
various birds and beasts in Melbourne
lately, undertook to give the cry of any
animal named by the audience. “Give us
an imitation of the Kaiser,” demanded some-
body who knew. “Henri” immediately
puffed out his cheeks and with a “He haw!
He haw! He haw!” drew great applause.
But just wait till he gets home!
“H. 0.”: This year’s Williamson panto-
mime is a triumph for three Australians —
W. R. Coleman, Minnie Everett and Maggie
Dickinson. Coleman has surpassed himself
in the scenery. The setting of the Prince
Regent’s Garden in the second act is remi-
niscent of Kay Nielsen’s beautiful illustra-
tions of Quiller-Couch’s book of fairy
stories, “In Powder and Crinoline.” And
as Minnie Everett has arranged her
ballets to suit the scenes, rarely a garish
note is struck. With Maggie Dickinson’s
dancing added —and the young Australian
has never danced so well —there is more in
the present panto, to please the aesthetic eye
than in any similar production for years.
For the show’s merits Australians are re-
sponsible, while for its chief defects we can
thank four of the most tragic comedians who
have ever tried to turn the milk of human
kindness to gall. Be it added that these
views are not the result of misguided patri-
otism, but were conceived in a coldly critical
spirit engendered by years of suffering as a
hired theatrical tripe-taster.

“Melb”: “Hindle Wakes” might have
been suggested by Jones’s “Case of Rebel-
lious Susan.” Jones did not go so far, but
suggested that because hubby did as he
pleased Susie went away to Egypt and did
as she pleased. This, again, was drawn from
“Francillon.” The wife there remains
innocent; but because the husband goes
with another lady to a cabinet particulier,
the wife goes likewise with another gent.,
and of course takes the next crib to the hus-
band’s. Thereupon his morality is shocked,
and he convenes a family council. Dumas
was so pleased with Mrs. Potter’s acting
that he presented her with the English
rights of “Francillon.” Bellew’s friends
wanted him to deodorise it. “No!” said he.
So Australia saw an exact translation.

N. S. Wales Full Court
registered two more marks
against the ancient Judge
Docker when on one recent
day it ordered two new trials.
One was for King and Fer-
guson, two of the men con-
victed for complicity in the
forging of Commonwealth
notes, and the appeal was
made on the ground of mis-
direction of the jury. The
other rehearing was in the
case of a man to whom
Docker had refused an
adjournment to secure the
attendance of a material wit-
ness. Of course the waste of
time and money doesn’t
matter. It’s merely part of
the price we pay for luxuries
like his honor.
Blaek-and-write brother Benson —George
Benson—writes from the land of trouble: —
Mud, my dear Iriend. Mud ! ’Tis not the end of
summer, yet I have already lost two pairs of boots
—sucked from my jfeet and lost forever in the ooze.
When we want to bury a “stiff” we cast him into a
space reserved, with his identification-disc on a cork
on a long string. When the dry weather comes they
just send out parties who put up a cross where they
find a disc. It is like being buried in a muddy lake
where a water-lily marks the place of interment.
* « * *
A Billzac who spent Christmas, 1915, in
the trenches records the visit of a friend :—-
Christmas Eve, and as cold as—some other place.
We were out in the first line, with nothing to do but
feel like frozen fish. Some of the English chaps
got talking of Christmas at Home and the time
they used to hang their stockings up. Jenkins,
from the North Country, and as simple and soft-
hearted as a kid, nearly blubbered over it. He
said he didn’t care —he was going to hang his
stocking up for Christmas. The boys took it as a
joke, until he got out his other pair of socks and
stood them up against the side of the trench. Next
morning—no Santa Claus! Jenkins seemed quite
cut up about it, and went to have another look in
his sock. Then Hell fell out of the sky. I never
saw a man disappear so completely off the earth.
Old Santa must have suddenly remembered he’d left
nothing for Jenkins. So he dropped Jenkins's present
fair into his sock —a bomb. I often wonder if
Jenkins caught up with Santa on the way aloft.
THE NEW RECRUITING CAMPAIGN.
“Marry me, marry me, darling, and save me.”
“R.C.8.” : The pride and joy of our crowd
is Bombardier Doleful, one of the most re-
sourceful nessimists alive. One day he was
sighted sitting in the door of our billet,
elbows on knees, gloomily nursing his jaws.
“Hullo! What’s un now —wife run away,
or what?” A low, watchdog growl came from
him. “No,” says he: “I was just thinking
what a cow it will be going home. A man
will be seasick all the blanky way!” That
was chalked up as his masterpiece till the
miserable day he was going up to the
trenches with an officer. The day before
Fritz had treated our post to a generous
share of high explosives and bombs, so as
thev were preparing to move off the officer
said cheerily: “Well, there’s one comfort.
Bombardier, if they do shell us to-day, I
know of a good 30ft. dugout.” But good old
Doleful had his answer ready. “Yes, and I
suppose a man’ll break his blanky neck
getting down it!”

“Reputed Pint” : “What shall he have who
killed the deer?” And what shall he have
who, under the provisions of the Act which
constituted the Vic. Licenses Reduction
Board, closed the Buck’s Head Hotel, Fitz-
roy’s historic pub? Removed in the middle
of last century to the corner of Napier and
Condell streets from further down the lat-
ter thoroughfare, the hotel was run for 25
years by Joe Snadden, who succeeded the
founder, J. Fogarty. It was here that a bul-
lock, Foasted whole, was distributed when
Edward VII. was married —in the olden,
golden days when he was still frankly
Tummy Wales. Here were the headquarters
of the stalwarts who brought W. M. K. Vale
up from Ballarat and ran the election which
sent him into the AssembV with a trium-
phant majority. Here of old were' polls
declared to rowdy mobs which cheered from
the cross-roads the speeches of successful
and defeated candidates. Joe Snadden, “the
Warwick of Fitzroy,” put into Parliament
Charles Jardine Don, the first working-man
to enter the Vic. Legislature. Don got a
gold watch and chain, afterwards inherited
by his son, who was brought from N. S.
AVales to receive the bequest from Snadden
as trustee. Don, senior, was a freethinker,
but in the end was converted to his earlier
faith by the Rev. R. Hamilton, who cele-
brated his exploit in a work entitled “The
Combat and the Victory.” In the Presby-
terian portion of Melbourne General Ceme-
ttery stands the monument with which his
Buck’s Head following perpetuated Don’s
memory. In an upstairs room of the pub
was painted the first Eight Hours banner
of the Moulders’ Union, and here, too, were
Hogmanay suppers held to introduce the
New Year in braw Scots style. Now the
glory has departed, and the license • perishes
at the Scriptural age of three score and ten.
“Trot” : The S. M. Herald put it this way
the other morning :
Australians are born and live in the sunshine, and
like butterflies justify their existence by exclaiming
that the day is theirs.
Of course. But the loud assertions made
by the Sydney butterflies are, after all, much
less confident than the conversation of the
mosquitoes at Brisbane. These creatures
appear to be utterly reckless. They insist
in the most arrogant tone that the night
belongs to them exclusively, and actually bite
anyone who contradicts. At one time the
cockatoos down the Riverina had the
audacity to openly claim their right to the
district, hut strangely enough their conver-
sational powers have diminished, and it
NOTHING TO GET WET ABOUT.
“Great Scott! You can’t go bathing in a
costume like that!”
“Certainly not, Daddy! I’m only going to
walk along the beach in it.”
seems only a matter of a few years when
the whole of the Australian fauna will cease
from gossiping in the old way. No doubt
the Herald has already detected that when
the dingoes capture a sheep outside the
office they divide the carcase in silence—
except for an occasional commentary on the
character of the meat. The likeness of the
Anzacs to butterflies, both in form and
speech, was one of the striking discoveries
of the last European summer.
EL MAGHARA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out east, on the sands of distraction, that dip to the
peep of the sun,
We waited the coming of action and sighed for the
sound of a gun.
Before we trekked over the waste in the night to
Maghara, and back,
We longed for the clatter of haste in the ruck of a
mounted attack.
We waited the dying of day with impatience and
simmering zeal,
We watched the red west make away with the sun,
and the sombre dusk steal,
All shadowy-slow and uncertain, to huddle the breeze-
bartered sands
In folds, like the folds of a curtain let loose from
invisible hands.
Like ghosts in the gloaming, ill-fated to march to
obscurity’s heart,
We sprang to the stirrup, and waited awhile in the
saddle the start.
A hush that was heavy and solemn grew tenser than
tightening fear,
And broke when the head of the column led off with
the murmuring rear.
Hearse-slow, as the mourners who follow a corpse to
the graveyard, we went
To gain from the depths of the hollow a point like
/ the peak of a tent.
The stars leapt ashine as we travelled wide-eyed in
the wilderness white ;
The moon swung aloft and unravelled a ribbon of
pad through the night.
Far over the wind-sharpened edges of razor-backed
- ridges that lift
Like pyramid summits and ledges, we surged through
the billow-like drift;
And discourse was strangely forbidden and hushed
by the laboring breath
Of animals never yet ridden so far in the silence of
death.
Like leaders a-tug at the traces, and hitched to a
floundering gun,
We clambered from hoof-hollowed places where
little sand rivulets run.
From gorges that muffled the task of each man and
his laboring beast,
We climbed ’neath the smothering mask of a mist
that came out of the east.
THE PALMS OF’EGYPT.
SHE : “Oh, and the witching scenery of the
East! I believe there are wonderful
palms in Egypt.”
HE: “Yes—mostly outstretched.”
We halted, unsaddled, and swiftly we hung on the
tibbin and corn;
Like corpses a-sprawl in the drift, we slept fast till
the glimmer of morn.
We lay in a wind-deepened hollow that gaped with a
seeming desire
To slowly envelop and swallow the force ere the east
leapt a-fire.
We hid till the vivid enamor of eve with its
crimson-lit wiles
Shed gold in the sweltering glamor, and mellowed
the sun-smitten miles ;
We lay till the camels were crawling all heavily
laden and dun
Out east, while the shadows were falling to smother
the vanishing sun.
In serpentine fashion out-stealing, the column
writhed hillward, to get
A glimpse of a low moon revealing high mountains
in stark silhouette,
When sudden there came without warning a mist,
like a scumbling pall,
That covered us as with an awning and hemmed us
around with a wall.
The sun pierced the mist with a glimmer of lances
down-thrust from the sky,
Ami burst in a soul-stirring shimmer that melted the
clouds floating high.
We climbed from the gloom of a bitter, grey waste
to a plateau of gold,
And gazed on its dazzling glitter like travel-worn
pilgrims of old.
We swept over gravel-strewn spaces, the hoofs
crunched the silence to flight,
And into white, haggard-worn faces there crept a
warm flush of delight;
We rode to the lilt of rude measure, when sudden
a view of the range
Fed hungering eyes, like a treasure in magical
regions and strange.
The horses swept forward ! The clatter of galloping
hoofs echoed strange,
Along with the opening chatter of maxim-guns find-
ing the range.
The rifles cracked fitful and ragged, the horses
tugged hard on the bit,
And sometimes an animal staggered, but seldom a
rider was hit.
We hurried for safety, for cover —tense-hearted, with
never a warp ;
And bullets strummed under and over like tautly-
drawn strings of a harp.
The Turks, in a wicked endeavor, were sniping with
purpose to stem
The tide of a race I shall never forget—the Anzacs
were swooping on them!
Distracted we travelled, worn wretches we crawled
through the fog-ridden night,
To burst on the green-dotted stretches in waves of
unbounded delight.
We leapt from a vision-dispelling grey waste to the
sunlight of God,
Which loses its charm in the telling and dies on
the grass-matted sod.
We felt the hard turf lying under, in one panting
gallop for life—•
Oh! all to the shattering thunder of guns in a spasm
of strife!
And high on the crag-sliadowgd ledges we hurriedly
covered the slain;
Then back over razor-like edges we trekked through
the desert again.

“Chasuble” : I daresay the pluckiest bit of
Australian churchbuilding, even among the
venturesome R.C.’s, has been Father Robin-
son’s Church of the Victories, at Camber-
well, Melbourne. He was unsuccessful in
various schemes, but hit the target with the
half-a-guinea-a-stone idea. The skeleton dome
has just been surmounted with a ton-weight
statue of the Virgin, pointing to Heaven. It
fs of wood, coppery gilt, the only thing for
which as yet Robinson has gone abroad.
Much honor to him for that!
A shudder comes from Ballarat (Vic.),
where an infant 10 months old became ill
and was supposed to be suffering from the
effects of teething. This diagnosis was par-
tially confirmed, but in consultation later
with, another doctor some kind of head
trouble was held to be the cause. In the end
the little sufferer died in great pain. While
the body was lying in the coffin someone
noticed a movement of one of the eyelids,
and on examination a centipede was dis-
covered. The supposition is that the insect
crawled into the ear while baby slept in
a perambulator under a tree, and then bur-
rowed its way into'the brain, finally emerg-
ing at the eye.

“Mujik”: Someone sent a couple of tur-
keys to the Snickjoslyns, who live in our
street. One was duly slain for Christmas;
the other, provided with a crate, was held
over until a day to be named. Then Smck-
•joslyn made a reasonably good job ot the
post mortem, while reserving an adequate
part for himself. He was
just about to give the
benediction, when a wild
cackle and flutter in the
yard stampeded the fam-
ily. A stray dog trying
to sample turkey No. 2
had. liberated the animal
and was pursuing it over
the fence. Snickjoslyn.
grabbing a rake, and pur-
sued by mother and the
kids, dashed into the sub-
urban street to retrieve
the vanishing live stock.
It was the most frantic
chase seen thereabouts
since some of the Jones
children robbed the
Chow’s banana basket.
Down the road rushed
the Snickjoslyn battalion
pursued by the advice of
neighbors and all the
vagrant poodles in the
district. Ranting if not
exhausted, the valiant
Snickjoslyn recaptured his
bird by what was left;
of its tail in Smith’s
front garden, and bore
the poultry back. Then
they all trooped in to de-
molish the food that had
been left behind —and
found the cat had been
there in the meantime.
Whereupon Snickjoslyn
cursed all turkeys, seized
the beer, ordered an attack on some sardines
and fiercely demanded to know why his wife
had not remained at home to protect the
household from starvation.
From Billjim in a London hospital: —
It’s the age of specialising all right. England
even specialises in charity. There are ladies, young
and pretty, old and ugly, who come into the* ward
and, if they see a man with a couple of fingers miss-
ing or a bandage round his head, it is “Oh, I am
so sorry you’re wounded! Does it hurt much ?
Would you care to come out for a motor ride and
have tea at our house?” The wounded chap says
perhaps, “My wound is all right; I’m only wearing
that bandage to please the doctor” ; but the woman
goes on pouring out sympathy and invitations till
the poor beggar is forced to accept. Next bed,
perhaps, there’s an enteric, dead sick of hospital
and feeling like nothing on earth. “Where were you
wounded?” the lady asks. “Not wounded, miss,
enteric’s my trouble” “0000-hh!” says the sympa-
thiser with wounded men only, and on she goes
to the next bed. No drives or tea-parties for the
men lying there weeks and weeks, while we that
are fit and well, except for a bullet hole or two
or ‘ a scratch of a shell, are fairly smothered with
attention. It makes, me feel fair sick and ashamed.
* * * *
Apparently the idea still prevails in Tas-
mania that an epileptic is a criminal tor-
mented by evil spirits and a lunatic is a ba.d
man who ought to be punished for his
wickedness. There is a corpse at Queens-
town to certify to these things. The
man was taken to the hospital and
refused admission “on account of
his mental condition.” Then the
police took him, and after having
him in a cell for a day or two for-
mally charged him with the offence
of being ill, and, withdrawing the
accusation, had him sent about his
bus ness. His business just at this
period was to have epileptic
seizures ; and in one of them he fell
into shallow water and was suffo-
cated. Previously a' doctor had
ordered the patient’s removal to the
detention ward at the local hospi-
tal ; and the only excuse so far
offered is that lie was recognised as
a fit subject for treatment, and
therefore was turned away.
Whether anybody will be charged
with manslaughter over the busi-
ness is doubtful. Yet it seems desir-
able, even necessary, to do some-
thing of the sort.

“B.B.” : I reckon this to be one
of the most affecting incidents of
the war. At dusk one day when
the steamer Ruahine was about 150
miles from Pitcairn Island a three-
year-old child who had been left
unattended in a cabin clambered up
to and fell through the porthole.
From the deck above, a liome-com-
ing Wellington trooper, named K. A. Bayne,
saw the baby splash into the water
and leaped after it. Lighting buoys
were thrown to his aid and a boat
was sent away promptly, but neither child
nor soldier was seen again. Sharks, it was
reckoned.

“C. Taylor”: Certainly “crucifixion” is
known in the Australian army. I’m positive
of it, because it has been my duty as an n.c.o.
of a guard while in Cairo to assist to strap a
gunner to the gun wheel. Also, two gun-
ners of my battery while at Anzac were
each sentenced to three days’ “field punish-
ment No. 1” (I think it is'termed), one
man for neglecting to obey an order and
the other for “refusing to obey an order and
insolence to an n.c.0.” That was only in
one section of an artillery brigade. I heard
of dozens of other instances in Egypt and
Gallipoli in other units—cases of men
strapped down to pegs driven in the ground.
If I remember rightly the conditions of the
punishment are that the prisoner must not
be strapped down more than three days out
of- four and not for more than two hours a
day.
“The Old Soldier” puts it this way:—
The Bulletin says that halt a dozen apparently-
competent witnesses write assurances that “cruci-
fixion” or spreadeagling is known to the Australian
army, as w.ell as to the British. I have not the
least doubt of it. At all times there has been a very
strong disposition among British commanding-officers
to inflict illegal and unusual punishments. When
the matter was brought up in the Commons the
War Office gave the usual sort of answer, i.e., that
practically all Generals agreed that “crucifixion”
could not be abolished: Sir Douglas Haig believed
that “its abolition would lead to an increase in the
death penalty.” The seniors said just the same
when the abolition of the “cat” was agitated for, 50
or 60 years ago. It is believed by the seniors of all
armies that- corporal punishment is necessary. It
is really only convenient. It saves trouble. But
whether it makes the discipline any better is a
question that no general officer is able to confidently
say. Before Sebastopol, in the winter of 1854-5,
when the British army was reduced to 12,000 men
fit for duty and could not find reliefs, soldiers were
often found asleep on their posts and their officers
simply would not bring them to a court-martial. I
was there and was one of them. The chief reason was
the bad effect the ensuing flogging would have on
steady men. As for General Haig’s statement that
the abolition of the new illegal form of corporal
punishment would mean the increased frequency of
the death-penalty, that is k mere guess. It would
be better to get rid of. all forms of corporal punish-
ment and ascertain the effect of that.
“Rio”: On page 223 of the 1914 edition
of the Field Service Focketbook the details
of Field Punishment (one time it was called
Field Imprisonment) are set out. Here they
are:—
Field Punishment.
Two Sorts. —No. 1 and No. 2.
No. 1. The offender may—-
(a) Be kept in irons.
(b) Be attached by straps, irons or ropes foi not
more than two hours in one day to a fixed
object in a fixed position. Must not be
attached for more than three out of tour con-
secutive days or for more than 21 days m all.
(c) Be made to labor as if he were undergoing
imprisonment with hard labor. ... , ,
No. 2. Same as No. 1, except he may not be treated
as above in (6).
The main points about it are: —
(1) It can only be inflicted for a crime com-
mitted “on active service.” . .
(2) The prisoner has to perform all other duties
besides his punishment.
(3) Punishment as in (b) above, t.e., Cruci-
fixion,” cannot be inflicted on the move; that is,
when the prisoner has been marching all day.
(4) Excepting as modified in (6) a court-martial
can inflict field punishment up to three months ; a
comanding-officer up to- 28 days.
(5) It was originally intended for infliction in
distant savage campaigns where prisons, etc., weie
scarce, but is sometimes given when the prisoner has
been trying to get into prison to escape service.
(6) It is usually imposed for crimes otherwise
punishable by death or penal servitude or for re-
peated drunkenness or bestial offences. .
(7) The usual way of carrying out (6) is by
tying the offender to a gun- or waggon-wheel.
In South Africa I saw “crucifixion” carried
out on both Tommies and Australians. One
N.S.W' Mounted Rifle, a splendid chap when
sober, was repeatedly drunk on trek and
became an utter nuisance to both his officers
and comrades. Had he been sent on for
‘court-martial he would have got several
years at least. His colonel at last gave him
seven days’ “Field Imprisonment No. 1,”
and for seven days he was tied to a gun-
wheel for two hours daily. He _was never
drunk again as long as I knew him. As to
whether “Field Punishment” is provided for
in the Australian Military Regulations, it
is not, directly. But there is a clause in
the Defence Act which says in effect that
for everything not mentioned or modified in
the Defence Act the provisions of the Army
Act are to be observed, which brings prac-
tically all active service matters under the
Imperial Act. Anyhow, as a matter of law,
is the Australian Defence Act in force out-
side the three-mile limit?
“Albatross”: The word “Blighty” (B.
2S/12./T6)is derived from the Indian “Bilati”
or “Bilaiti,” meaning “Home.” It has been
Atkinized into “Blighty,” and will go down
to posterity along with “Wipers” and other
classical names of the Great W ar.

“Jay-Jay”: While applauding “Murkah”
for his denunciation of the insanitary
(water-) drinking facilities provided for the
Australian railway traveller, I think the
pupils of the public schools are even worse
off. The traveller can get a clean drink if
he likes to wait and pay for it, but when the
penniless school kid“ wants a refresher he puts
his lips to the tap and sloboers up as much
as he can of the spouting water. A number
of youngsters in every school provide their
own drinking utensils, but the majority fol-
low the good old custom that poisoned our
uncles. My own Department of Education
solemnly instructs us teachers to disinfect
school pens and pencils, but allows the in-
valuable small Australian to drink like a
stray dog.
UNLEARNIN G.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Each year when I inspect the stock
Of wisdom in my. store
(The knowledge bought at some expense
From gay or sad F.xperience
The maxims terse of modern note,
That mentally I often quote ;
Odd little bits of lore),
I always get a painful shock
On finding here and there a space
Where something was, and in its place
I put a notion newly-born
Which causes me to think with scorn
Of what I thought before.
Those empty spaces year by year
I mark with grave concern,
Old lessons as I older grew
Were set at naught by lessons new ; .
The little truths I treasured erst
Are now by greater truths disperst—
Gone, never to return.
And Wisdom whispers in my ear:
“Much knowledge that you dullards gain
Is brushed in ashes from your brain ;
The very thing you learn to-day
May be to-morrow —who shall say-?—■'
A thing you must unlearn!”
Ah, well! The truths! that pleased me best
Arc not yet proven lies ;
‘ Sweet lessons, deeply underlined,
Learned from the book of womankind,
Of tenderness and gratitude
Outlasting passion’s passing mood—
These do -I dearly prize.
. And fables which have stood the test
Of ages since good AUsop’s time ;
Old proverbs, writ in prose or rhyme—•
I am resolved to treasure these,
With Love’s fair seeming-verities
If wisdom must unlearn all things
Whereof the glamored poet sings,
’Twere folly to be wise.
VICTORIA.

SPLENDID REMEDY FOR
CHILDREN’S COUGHS.
Mothers all over Australia are making in
their own homes as good a remedy as money
can buy for Coughs, Colds, Whooping Cough
Croup, Influenza, Bronchitis and Sore
Throats. All they do is buy from their
chemist or store 2s. worth of Hean’s Essence,
and add to it water and sweetening matter
enough to make up to one pint. Such a
quantity of ready-made cough mixtures
would cost at least 12s. The beauty of the
Hean’s Essence mixture is that it contains
no paregoric, laudanum or other opium
drugs, which are apt to do untold harm to
young children. — Advt.

London was evidently a disappointment.
After it George Reid said :
I am full of gratitude to the country which
made me.
Pathetic evidence that either the food restric-
tions are too drastic or George is losing his
old form with the knife and fork.

In the interests of economy Vie. Law De-
partment has decided that the remaining
County Court Judges shall divide between
them the work of Judge Box, who retires
very shortly, and that no successor to ’sonah
shall be appointed. As Virgil would put it:
“Box et prwterea nihil.”
December 28 was the 80th anniversary of
the foundation of South Aus., and was kept
in the usual way at Glenelg, where Governor
Hindmarsh —who had fought under Nelson
at Trafalgar, and was, like him, one-eyed-
landed from the Buffalo, and “proclaimed”
the infant province. Nearly 100 of arrival
or birth not later than 1840 were gathered
together by Mayor Alfred Roberts, and con-
gratulated by Premier and Governor. Among
them was James Chittleborough, a passenger
by the Buffalo, and Mrs. Mansell, by the
slightly-earlier Tam O’Shanter. Old Mrs.
Mantagani, who was in Australia a month
before Hindmarsh, and had reached the
mature age of 11 when the Proclamation took
place, was not present last week. She
declares that the bent gum to which Glenelg
pays honor is not, by about a hundred yards,
the real scene of the Proclamation; and she
cannot bring herself to assist in perpetuating
the error.

Passed out at Wellington (N.S.W.), John
Restall, in the 87th lap. Born in Surrey
(Eng.), Restall came to Australia in his
twenties and started as a baker in Grenfell,
also running a business at the Quandong dig-
gings. At his shop door in Quandong he
was shot by bushranger Ben Hall in the
course of a willing scrap due to Restall's
trying to out Hall with an iron oven-rake
when the ’ranger was commandeering the
contents of his till. He kept the bullet till
his death, and it was buried with him, as
the doctors had never succeeded in removing
it from his anatomy. In later years he ran
a bakery and a brewery in Wellington.
Sergeant Arthur Joyner, killed in action in
France, enlisted in the A.I.F. in the first
month of the war, and had worn khaki for
two years and four months without a spell
He was in the Gallipoli landing, and in the
intervals of potting Abdul secured pro-
bably the best lot of camera, snapshots yet
shown of that graveyard. After the evacua-
tion he was one of the first draft of Aus-
tralians to France, and at Pozieres was con-
gratulated in orders by General Cox for
“conspicuous gallantry and devotion to
duty.” Joyner came from Paddington
(N.S.W.).
Major Ernest Frederick Powys Sketchley,
of the Royal Marine Light Infantry, lately
killed bv a shell in the Flanders trenches,
aged 35, was gentleman help and secretary
to Gov. Bedford, of W.A., from ’O5 to ’OB.
Sketchley was one of the naval brigade in the
expeditions to Ostend and Antwerp in the
first months of the war, and later was a staff
officer of the naval division at Gallipoli,
where he won the D.S.O. and was twice men-
tioned in despatches. He was the only son
of an English country rector.
Charles Edward Pilcher, K.C., recently
dead, was born at Maitland in 1844, the son
of Henry Incledon Pilcher, a solicitor of the
’3o’s. He was first articled to J. P. Abbott,
but afterwards transferred to G. D. V.
Pilcher, his brother. Then he entered the
chambers of F. M-. Darley, afterwards Chief
Justice, to read for the Bar. Darley had
three pupils who all did well —C. E. Pilcher,
Jack Want and R. E. O’Connor: tutor and
pupils are now all dead. Pilcher married a
daughter of John McPhillamy, of Bathurst,
with whom he cot a dot of some magni-
tude. and that,'the big money he had made
at the Bar and his saving habits encouraged
him to take life lazily for many years past.
Robert Miller Laird, one of Australia s
oldest shipbuilders, cut his cable at nn-
liamstown the other day. He arrived in
Victoria from his native Paisley in the
Calliope in ’56, and thereafter helped to
make boats in many parts of the Common-
wealth. Thirty years ago he supervised the
building of the tug Advance and the dredge
Platypus for the Melbourne Harbor Trust;
and two years back he was still giving
orders at the Sydney naval dockyard. Be-
tween times he was —well, wherever the
rare ship was building in Australia. But
mostly it was a mere overhauling job.
Retired from N. S. Wales Public Service,
“Old” Banks, the veteran messenger of- the
N S. Wales State offices. It was in
February, 1867, that he started his. job in
the Lands Department, his Ministerial boss
being Bowie Wilson, father of the lawyer-
mining man who was the chief of the Intelli-
gence Department in N. S. Wales in the
early days of the war. After Wilson’s re-
tirement Banks saw 34 Ministers come and
go at the Lands Office.
Passed out suddenly at Grafton (N.S.W.)
one of the “sports” and popular men of the
Northern Rivers- —champion bowler, one-
time intercolonial rep. cricketer, good golfer
and billiard-player Joe Spencer. Partner
of Red Cross Commissioner E. G. Elworthy,
now in Egypt, he controlled stores in North
and South Grafton, and in his spare time
cheerfully won sweeps at the Club with so
much consistency that a “shilling-in” at
Grafton is “a Spencer.” He was 58 and had
indigestion and a crotchety heart.
From black-and-white artist Benson, who
is in France with the other good people:—
I've been lucky, so far —perhaps fate has a worse
end in store for me. One tiny splinter from Fritz,
but not enough to earn a piece of gold braid, is all
I claim at present, and is likely to be all for some
time, because I’ve “done me nerves.” To tell truth,
I can’t stand action for any sustained length of time
. now. I regret it, too. It’s the only part of the
game that’s worth while. I’ve been enjoying a spell
in a country village in France, and when asked,
“What did you do in the great war, Daddy?” I’ll
be able to say: “Sketches, my son ; I did sketches !”
“W.R.P.”: In Bulletin 30/11/T6 there
was an account of the death of Sergeant W.
Young, followed by the statement that he
fired Australia’s first shot in this war. As
one who was at Point Nepean when the Ger-
man vessel tried to run out and was fired
across, let me say that Young was not at
Point Nepean at the time, but arrived a
week or two later. Anyhow, no one person
fires the gun, but the individual who actu-
ally pressed the lever to make electric con-
tact when the other twelve had done their
bit is now in France with the Heavy Artil-
lery.
Tasmania’s latest Rhodes scholar is
Lieut. Alan Payne, now serving somewhere
in France. The son of a doctor, he won a
lot of prizes on the mathematical and science
side, and now squirts decimal points at
the enemy as boss of a machine-gun section.
Just about 21.
CHRISTMAS CUSTOMS AT BILLY-
GOAT FLAT.
The Annual Whitewash.
Francis Chapman, who died in London in
December, was a Brisbane business man 40-
odd years ago, and took a hand in the
liquidation of the J. and G. Harris firm m
the ’7o’s. Later, he was London manager
for McLean Bros. & Rigg, and about 25
years ago joined James Dickes and put up
signboards in London and Sydney as hard-
ware and machinery merchants. Sons will
keep up the old shingle at both ends.
Died at Wollstonecraft (N.S.W.), Robert
11. Harvey, who first came to Australia as
master of the Rockton for the old A.S.N.
Co. Harvey remained with the co. for some
years as skipper in the Queensland trade,
and then took charge of the Government
yacht Merrie England in Papuan waters.
For the past 12 years he had been a Torres
Strait pilot, and recently brought his last
ship, a Jap liner, through to Sydney.
The youngest Billzac? The kid was 16
when he shamed Perth’s cold-footed brigade
by parading in bor-
rowed breeches be-
fore, the local mili-
tary doctors. His
tale that he was 19
was accepted.
Fever smote the
precocious young-

home and his dis-

Rillzac junior soon
brought him into

was “being an un-
controllable child.”
TOM MUTCH, of
the A.W.U. and
the WORKER, one
of the new leaders
of the N.S.W.
Labor movement.
A reinforcement recently embarked had
three officers. Their names were Swallow,
Green, Grapes.
Died at Bega (N.S.W.), William Rixon,
aged 72, who for over 40 years had been sell-
ing fat cattle twice a week. Rixon came of
an old South Coast pub-keeping and farming
family, and was related more or less to nearly
everyone between Moruya and the Vic. bor-
der. He was one of the founders of the loca 1
Jockey Club, and some years ago was about
the best-known racing man south of Sydney.
A family of 12 will help to keep the name
going.
Tasmania claims another centenarian:
Mrs. Machen, of King’s Meadows, has some
evidence that she was 100 on December 14.
She. has lived for 56 years in the same
house, and perhaps that accounts for it.
Cann, Labor member for Broken Hill,
who goes out of politics after a 25
years’ uninterrupted run, being given a
pension in the shape of a Railway Com-
missionership at £lOOO a year, has had
a record easy passage. In nine
general elections he has been opposed
only twice, and then only by also-
rans.
H. S. P. Sawyer, just appointed
headmaster of Shrewsbury, one of the
big English public schools, is the third
son of that Dr. Sawyer who was the
first to wear Anglican gaiters at
Grafton (N.S.W.). The see was
then known as “Grafton and Armi-
dale,” with a cathedral in each town —
an arrangement that lasted till two
years ago. Div Sawyer bished for
only a' few months, being drowned in
the Clarence River one Sunday night
in IS6S on his way home from church.
The son, who was then three years old,
has lived ever since in the Cold Coun-
try where he has been canon of Car-
lisle Cathedral and headmaster of St.
' Bees College, in Cumberland. His
predecessor at Shrewsbury has been
moved up to take charge of Eton Col-
lege, England’s toniest school, whose
late headmaster’s appeal not to humiliate
Germany lead to strong hints that he had
better resign. .
Lance-corporal Clarence Page, of the
A.1.F., killed in action at Pozieres, had just
got his B.A. degree at Sydney Uni. when
Bill broke into Belgium. Page was starting
a course of training for the Scotchbyterian
ministry, but he dropped his books to enlist,
and went with the first Australian troops to
Egypt. ■ He was in the landing at Gallipoli,
and saw the whole job through till the evacu-
ation, being thrice mentioned in despatches
and once recommended for the D.C.M. Going
to France, lie was first reported wounded,
then missing and finally killed. An elder
brother was killed in Gallipoli, and a second
is now being patched up in an English hos-
pital after an argument with a German shell
in France. The family hails from Tamworth
(N.S.W ).
THE PARTITION OF FRANZ JOSEF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Franz Josef's heart will be deposited in St. Augus-
tine’s Church. His intestines will be deposited in
the crypt of St. Stephen’s Cathedral, and the corpse
will be buried in the vaults of the Capuchin Church.
Concerning Madame Katherine Schratt, to whom
the Emperor is reported to have left the sum of
£15,000, etc.— Cables.
They’ve chopped up his carcase, and handed bits
round
As chaste souvenirs for the churches,
Where faithful reports of his virtues resound
From a thousand parsonical perches.
They’ve pickled his body, and bottled his heart,
And canned his imperial giblets;
They’ve cut up his savings, each pal gets a part,
In liberal helpings or driblets.
They’ve even rewarded his feminine “friend,”
That fondest of footlighty friskers ;
Cut up all his life, and carved up at the end—
But what have they done with his whiskers?
QUEENSLAND.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.

1916. .108,196 2615 8 „ ~43,155 17,000 498,270
It is in some ways a disadvantage and a han-
dicap for this hank to carry on every balance-
sheet a reminder that it was once in diffi-
cnlties. There are other banks, no more
thoroughly sound than this one, whose simi-
lar difficulties nine-tenths of the people ha.ve
forgotten all about. Perhaps, when the world
settles down again, it may be desirable to
make a rearrangement by which the traces of
the old trouble will be removed. Under the
old arrangement one-fourth of the profits,
after various payments have been made, must
be applied to the repurchase of certain de-
ferred stock, which was issued in lieu of part
of the old deposits. This deferred stock
carries 3 p.c., with an extra IT p.c. when
profits permit; and the full 44 p.c. has been
paid for very many years. At the end of
1915 £14,385 was the amount of the residual
one-fourth of profits before-mentioned, and
with that money £17,000 worth of the de-
ferred stock was .repurchased, and then can-
celled. Hence the £2615 of profit shown in
the last line of the table and the £17,000
written off. This year another £14,385 is
available; and, as money is dear now, pro-
bably that will purchase £17,500 or more of
stock. These purchases have exactly the
same effect as if the-profit was put to reserve.
Actually, since £17.000 disappears from the
liabilities, it is necessary to write down
something on the other side by an equal
amount. Hence the premises account is being
gradually reduced ; and as they still stand at
£269,256, or just half the share capital, they
can stand reduction very well. But later on,
if the assets are found to be well worth their
book value, with a bit over, the bank would
certainly gain more by using these written-
off thousands to build up its visible reserves.
Of course that would require an alteration in
the articles of association.
The bank’s old deposits were represented
on reconstruction by four lots of paper ; and
this is what lias happened to them in 20
years:—
1896. 1910. 1915. 1916.

The three classes of stock now in existence
are interminable, except for the provision re-
lating to the purchase of the deferred stock
already mentioned; hence this money is as
firmly fixed in the business as if it were
share capital. The debenture and pref. stock
carry 4 p.c.; the other 44 p.c. Especially at
a time like this it is all cheap money ; and as
the surplus earnings belong to the sharehold-
ers (with the reservations already referred
tot they are likelv to have a very good time
It isn’t every bank that has two and a
quarter millions of 4 and 44 p.c. pref. capital.
It certainly couldn’t be got to-day.
On the other hand, it has to be remembered
that the real share capital has to guarantee
the whole of this stock capital; but that is a
risk which gradually diminishes as the re-
serves grow, because the reserves guarantee
the share capital. These reserves are now so
aor v substantial that, though dear money
generally means abnormal risks for invest-
ments. it is probably true that no other body
of Australian bank shareholders are likely to
do so well out of a period of dear money.
Besides its perpetual stock the E. S- and A.
owes £8,292,467 to depositors and other
creditors; and as that is equal to only
£2 7s (id. for every £1 of the bank’s own
funds (including perpetual stock), the credi-
tors are exceedingly well protected. But here
is the whole posi tion

pay the balance just about twice over. Thus
creditors can afford to sleep quite comfort-
ably.

The co. pays 25 p.c.; but, then, it hasn’t
capitalised profits like most other Austral-
asian insurance offices have done. Except
that a few shares were sold a long while
ago to bring the figures to the even £lOO,OOO,
the capital stands today exactly where it
stood 30 years ago. But the capital is about
the only thing that has stood stationary. In
just over 20 years the net premium income
has doubled ; arid the reserves have doubled
in 12 years. But the voyage hasn’t been all
so rapid. • The c-o. was born in 1873, and
by the end of 1885 it had £193,314 of re-
serves. Fifteen years later it had only
£128,815. All the surplus profits made out
of the business during that long, weary
period, and nearly £70,000 besides, went to
write off losses made through bad invest-
ments in the years when heavy loan expendi-
ture was giving a fictitious value to Maori-
land estate.
It is a good thing at a time like the pre-,
sent to recall what happened in those -days.
In 1882 this co. thought it was doing a very
safe thing when it lent £BOOO on a South-
hind property which had been valued by the
usual expert at £15,400. The interest was
paid punctually for years ; then the bottom
fell out of Maoriland values, and in 1890 the
security was written down to £2OOO, and it
was thought that that much could be got for
it with patience. What happened after-
wards in that instance this writer doesn’t
know. Maybe something was recovered on
that account subsequently. At anv rate,
during the last 15 years at least it is pretty
certain that practically none of the current
profit has had to go down the sink. Values
have been generally on the up-grade; and
nobody has been throwing in his mortgage.
But how Maoriland is going to fare in the
near future depends mostly upon what lamb
and mutton will brine- after the war. What
a knock-out can amount to the foregoing-
figures show.
This is the only feature of the National
that The Bulletin can’t feel sure about.
Of its total assets of £555,321 no less than
£343,640 is out on mortgage and £83,007 is
in that vague security described merely as
“debentures.” However, so splendid a pro-
portion of the earnings has consistently been
put aside that this feature need not give the
policy-holders any concern. First, take these
figures :

The losses in the past five years have aver-
aged under £'<s,ooo a year. The present net
assets are £520,000. Add £lOO,OOO for income
in excess of exes, during the current year,
and it will be seen that before September,
1917, the co. could pay 84 times its average
losses —assuming, of course, that it could
realise its assets at book value. If through
some dreadful catastrophe it could get only
half of their book value for the non-liquid
assets, it could in the coming year have
five times worse luck than usual and still
come up smiling. And the position goes on
improving: in the past year, for instance, in-
terest and rents brought in £24,879. which
was practically the full amount of the divi-
dend. leaving the whole of the fine under-
writing profit to go towards the further
strengthening of reserves.

Steadily for several years past the co. has
been increasing its advances: 1913, £15,757 ;
1914, £96,971; 1915, £118,071; 1916,
£125,617. Considering how very uncertain
the future is, the directors have adopted a
policy which lots of men would shrink from.
It may pan out all right. But there are
large risks attached to it. The one thing
which excuses an increase in advances under
present circumstances is the sale of fore-
closed properties. No doubt balances are
owing by the purchasers of these assets.
But there have evidently been large advances
apart from that. The question is, suppose
there was a slump, could the co. nurse its
securities? That depends upon whether it
could, without realising properties, pay off
those creditors who would want their money.
It owes £62,955. Against that it has
£214,276 of assets —not much liquid, it is
true; yet the total of all assets is quite
enough to make it possible for the co. to
borrow except under the worst possible cir-
cumstances. But the co. ought to protect
itself against having to pay through the
nose, as it would then have to do, by in-
creasing its liquid holding to 25 p.c. at least
of its liabilities. Then it could argue with
its creditors, and it would be reasonably sure
of never having to throw securities on to an
unfavorable market.
W. E. Smith, Ltd., formed to take over an
old-established printing and stationery busi-
ness in Sydney as from October, 1910 (the
deficit to start with represented goodwill
and preliminary exes.) :

Apr., 1915 .., 1656 Nil 627 1,129
Oct., 1915 . . 2066 Nil 539 2,656
Apr., 1916 . . 2222 5 p.c. = 1125 537 3,216
Oct., 1916 . . 3204 5 „ „ 1125 549 4,746
There. is a sermon to investors in that. Old
W. E. Smith had been in this business for
years, and had carried it on in his own way.
ije took the whole load on his shoulders.
When lie was ill and tired he sold to this
co., finding a promoter who was prepared
to look at the results and not at the manner
in which the results had been produced.
Then, as anybody could have forecasted, the
man who had taken the whole business on his
shoulders left it, and dividends shrank from
10 p.c. to nothing. Then, the descent going
still further, the capital was reduced from
£75.000 to £45,000. By this time, however,
some other people had seen possibilities in
the business ; and they got hold of it. A first-
-rate Board was appointed and the result
is shown in the table. Of course the co.
is not out of its troubles yet. It never will
be; for it is a business which calls for a
lot of supervision. And it is continually
in competition with small shops which don’t
know anything 6f wages board awards. But
if a business owned by shareholders is at a
disadvantage in the matter of supervision
compared with one owned by a single indi-
vidual or by two of three working partners,
it has advantages in other respects. These
people are all buyers of more or less print-
ing and stationery supplies; and, given
reasonable management, a large volume of
business will flow to the co. almost auto-
matically. Hence W. E. Smith’s has possi-
bilities of being a steady dividend-payer. If
it isn’t it will be-to a very great extent the
shareholders’ own fault.
When the new directors wrote off £30,000
of the capital they cleaned up the balance-
sheet and made it quite a respectable docu-
ment. There is no goodwill in it now, the
Bridge.-street lease has been written down to
a mere £l6O, and a much-needed stocktaking
put values about where they should be.
The total assets as at Oct. 31 were £77,530,
and of that lot £47,788 is in stock, £13,383
in book debts, £6229 in a comparatively new
York-street factory, and the balance in plant
and odds and ends. Total liabilities at the
same date were £26,659, including £18,500 of
6 p.c. debentures. Since April, 1915, when
the whole business had just been overhauled
the co. has got £2990 further into debt, and
has used up £5502 of its cash balances; but
it has added just over £15,500 to its stock
besides making various smail additions to the
plant. So things are going the right way
very definitely.

Apparently some people had dined well;
and in gratitude to the chef they offered him
£10,500 for the goodwill of his beautiful
business. The chef said he didn’t mind;
and when he left the business the far-sighted
people discovered that what had gone was
precisely what they had paid £10,500 for.
To-day the co. doesn’t run the expensive
eating-house any more, and it has written
off half its capital—from £14,000 to £7OOO.
As a sort of side-line it acquired a neighbor-
ing pub, the Balfour, on terms which have
never been disclosed ; and nowadays it runs
only that. So its assets consist of leasehold
and plant, £8355; stocks, £2104; etceteras,
£495. Total £10,954. On the other side it
owes £2370, inluding a few debentures.
There may be profits in it. But they are
by 110 means sure; and the assets would of
course be a poor lot for a forced sale.

It is fine scenery to look back over. Ten
years ago, and that was well after the
Westralian boom was supposed to have sub-
sided, the co. had £348,954 of assets, and
owed £64,588. That gave it £279,366 net.
To-day it has £539,524 of assets and
£39,907 of liabilities, or £499,617 net.' That
means an increase of £220,251 of net assets
in 10 years; but of that total £50,000 is
due to an issue of pref. capital. Assuming,
therefore, that the wasting assets have been
sufficiently written* down, the profits have
in 10 years been £170,251 in excess of the
dividends. Now let us look at the allowance
for depreciation. Two lots of properties-
stand in the books at cost, or so little less
than cost that the difference doesn’t matter
—brewery lands and buildings, £84,600;
hotel properties, £274,675. On the other
hand, brewery plant, etc., which cost
£153,427, stand- in the books at only £59,254.
It is very likely that some of the properties
bought in early days, including the brewery,
are worth far more to-day. But when
large sums are derived from the rents of
premises which will have to be pulled down
and rebuilt some day, it seems to this paper
that a replacement fund should be provided
out of revenue. When the premises are left
in at cost, the management practically takes
credit for a rise in market value, and says
that that rise counterbalances the deprecia-
tion. Anyhow, here are the facts: the co.
has a total share capital of £252,900; and
to represent that it has £143,853 of brewery
premises and plant, £275,307 of pub and
other, property, and £80,456 net of miscel-
laneous assets, mostly liquid. Since the war
started the results have been just about as
good as they were before. Total assets are
£924 less than they were as at September,
1914, but liabilities are £29.444 less, so there
has been a net improvement of £28,520,
which- is all right for two years of war,
especially as during that period about

The extraordinary success and popularity of the
Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Bofler, so univer-
sally used where the economy in steam-raising is con-
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. —-Advt.

£15,000 has been written off the book value
of plant, etc.
What is going to be pumped out of the
future The Bulletin really can’t afford to
tell for sixpence. Prophecies, are extra. But
there is a large probability that things are
not going to be any better for the brewer
and the pub-owner—especially the owner of
the pub that depends mostly upon a bar-
trade. It isn’t only the wowser who is in
this early-closing and anti-liquor campaign.
If it was a wowser’s fight the prospects of
success in Westralia wouldn’t be brilliant.
But there is a huge body of the Labor vote
going the same way, especially that vote
which is most influenced by the new leader-
ship. We saw .that in N. S. Wales, where
the Worker was more teetotal in the six
o’clock fight than the teetotaller.
