This co. isn’t making the progress it ought
to. Seven years ago the value of the trust
and agency business in hand was £1,404,818.
To-day it is £1,955,757. As this co. had
the field to itself for a long while the figures
ought to be better. That they are not is no
doubt largely due to the fact that so many
Westralian property-owners are absentees,
and that there is always a large disposition
among the first generation to go back
“home” to die. However, the movement of
the figures is the right way; and time is
fighting for the shareholders. The co.’s paid
capital is £15,000, in-£2 10s. shares paid to
155.; and this and the reserves are in pre-
mises (£12,823), mortgages (£6133), and
public stock and debentures, with a few
etceteras. There are no liabilities worth
mentioning.
The Electrical Oil Co., of Melbourne, is
making claims that almost take the breath
away. It owns a process, invented by C. F.
Lungley, for transforming kerosene into
petrol. This process “takes a waste oil
worth Id. a gallon and makes it worth
Is. Bd. a gallon at a cost of another Id. a
gallon.” Residual oil left after various pro-
ducts have been extracted amounts, it is
alleged, to 40 p.c. of the original petroleum.
It js “practically worthless,” yet from it the
claim is made that this new process extracts:
, £ s. d.
25% Petrol .. . . Worth 1/6 a gallon, 1 17 6
15% Engine Kerosene .. „ 1/6 ~ 12 6
30% Illuminating-Kerosene „ 1 10 0
15% Lubricating-Oil .. ~ 4/- 3 0 0
15% Tar . ' .. ’,,1/- „ 15 0
100 gallons „ £8 -5 0
The substance used in the transformation has
been submitted in confidence, to Dr. McFar-
land, vice-chancellor of Melbourne Uni., who
certifies that it is “of such low commercial
value that its cost need hardly be taken into
account in estimating the cost of carrying
through the process.” As ■ the world’s
petroleum supply is 15,000 million gallons, of
which 6000 millions are residual oil, “almost
valueless,” and as it is claimed that this pro-
cess converts such ■ oil into petrol, kerosene,
lubricating oils and tar at a cost of only Id.
per gallon, there are either millions in it or a
big disappointment for somebody.
* * * *
All preliminaries having now been agreed
on, meetings of the shareholders in the Royal
Bank of Queensland and the Bank of North
Queensland have been called to authorise
amalgamation. The new title is to be the
Bank of Queensland. The Royal has some
pref. shares; and as it is proposed to have
only one class of shares in the new bank, the
holders of these prefs. are offered either
deposit receipts or ordinary shares of the
full face value of their present holdings. In
view of the fact that the assets are to be
written down and only enough capital issued
to represent the assets on a conservative
valuation, the prefs. will be well secured if
they accept shares, while their return will
certainly be better than the old guaranteed
• J, i P-c. In other words, in the present bank
they can’t get more than 3| p.c. unless a lot
of dead capital shares in it. Under the
amalgamation they will share fully in a
dividend which has to be paid only on live
capital. With ordinary shares they may take
rather more risk than with prefs. ; but it is
better to have ordinary shares in a vigorous,
progressive bank than prefs. in one which
suffers from the handicap of long-past indis-
cretions and makes poor progress in conse-
quence.
Metropolitan, Helensburgh (N.S.W.),
during 12 months to March 31, raised 229,518
tons of South Coast coal. But costs were so
high that after writing off £3OOO it made a
net profit of only £3866. That means no more
than 4Jd. per ton. Dividend appropriations
were 8 p.c. on first prefs.= £2288; 5 p.c. (on
account) second prefs. = £4953. There was
£21,574 brought forward, making £25,440
available, and after deducting dividends
totalling £7241, there remains £18,199 to
carry forward.
* * * *
Mt. Kembla, also on the South Coast,
raised 244,419 tons during the year ended
June 30. Its net profit was £20,689, and
that means about Is. 9d. per ton. The co.
owes this rather exceptional profit to its
modern electrical equipment and its prox-
imity to port. The capital is £350,000, so a
modest 6 p.c. costs £21,000, which is a heavy
load for a colliery producing less than 250,000
tons in 12 months. On June 30 there was
after paying the dividend only £3012 to
credit of profit and loss, or less than the
year started with.
# * * *
From Westonia:—
Edna Central maintains its improvement, . and
although nothing sensational has been disclosed (or
even reported) the reserves are being built up to a
figure that will not shame the next half-yearly
report. An excellent block of stone is being stoped
near Edna May boundary, No. 3 level on the main
lode, and the east drive on the new lode (No. 3
level) is improving from nothing to 5 or 6 dwt.
The new lode is likely to be connected with main
shaft at No. 4 level within two or three months.
The mine is now well handled, and, although we
miss the old 10 and 20 oz. assays, the present vigor-
ous development policy promises to result in the
mine paying well on present prices—which is better
than appeared possible a few months ago. The
Central is now quite respectable. It is hoped the
standard will not fall when it locates the “deeps”
of the Consolidated new lode, as it is pretty sure
to do eventually.
The Deeps’ debenture holders, subscribers of
£lO,OOO, are not feeling well. Out of £lO,OOO,
£5OO went as underwriters’ commission, £4OOO in
repayment of loan advanced by director Duff and
£lOOO in payment of lease acquired on the north.
Then there is a boiler and compressor to pay for,
besides settling. with sundry creditors, so there is
not much cash for the manager to play with. The
ore reserves above 485 ft. level are being rapidly
exhausted, and revenue from this source must soon
fail. The company is also faced with a main shaft-
sink of 75ft. to 100 ft., notwithstanding the fact that
the level below 485 ft. must be less valuable than
the one at that point.
Edna May is driving- hard for the reef at the No.
5 level. Except for the water the mine is looking
well. The Nos. 4 and 5 levels are worth viewing
by those who appreciate good underground work
accomplished under trying circumstances.
■* * * *
Bob Turner, having made his bit with
Tricks and others in Edna May, was given
the go-by at the shareholders’ meeting by
some of his old pals. The secret is- that the
Deeps amalgamation idea is “on” again
and Turner is off that. Of course some say
it isn’t an amalgamation or a fusion, but a
price to be fixed. Whatever name it may be
called, the big body of Edna May share-
holders ought not to be caught in the mood
to give much for Deeps until they see how
the shoot is going in that cemented-up show. v
The argument of the amalgamators is that'
the Edna will be able to work out its last
two levels at a greatly reduced cost if it
gets hold of the Deeps shaft. True; but
to equip that working, put up pumps, and
do other needfuls will eat up a pretty penny.
So the Deeps shaft may be a dear bargain
except it falls into the lap of the big mine.
And more unlikely things than that have
happened in mining.
* * * *
Oroya Links (W.A.) has just raked in a
thousand or so in royalty from a tribu-
tor’s crushing out of portion of the old
Brown Hill workings. The gold was got
out of a branch of the old Oroya shoot, that
remarkable flat pipe of ore which traversed
the Brown Hill, Brown Hill Extended, Asso-
ciated Northern and Oroya North Block, and
from which several million pounds were
extracted. It spread out from 30 to about
100 feet in width just before reaching the
Associated boundary and proved a great
disappointment in that property. Its origin
is now a matter of much argument among
experts, though in the fat days no one
seemed to bother about how it came to be
where it was. Sufficient that it yielded
succulent dividends and directors’ fees.
* * * *
The Government experts who eyed recent
experiments have reported that to get the
best blasting results in the W.A. mines future
supplies of explosives will have to consist of
four parts of 50 per cent, gelignite and one
part of standard 61.4 per cent, gelignite.
The consent of the authorities for the use
of that mixture is now being sought.
* ** * *
philde Harold, near Laverton (W.A.), is
being nibbled at again. This time a Mel-
bourne crowd is sampling and claims that
assays over a width of from 7 to 11 feet of
quartz are satisfactory. The local paper
predicts that the option will be completed.
Then it must be on something new. On
what was formerly exposed the show was
unpayable.
Ivanhoe (W.A.) still struggles with
impoverished values at depth and doesn’t
show any signs of getting clear. The latest
bore put down is from the 3620 ft. level and
so far only makes of miserably poor ore
have been struck.
* * * * *
For the first time in its history the Ben-
digo goldfield has produced less than
IOO.OOOoz. in 12 months. The total for 1916
was 85_,6220z. Divs. were only £13.275, and
calls totalled £46,419. Carlisle Co. headed
the list with 53520 z. for the year. In the
old days when its three tributes were
making the pace a cracker, the Garden Gully
has often had a cake of retorted gold in the
Bank of Victoria’s Bendigo window of that
size for a fortnight’s crushing. In the 1870
boom the Great Extended Hustlers thought
nothing of showing a rough cake of 4000 oz.
for the fortnight in the old Oriental Bank
window on a Saturday night. The old names
of the steady producers still occur in the
list: Virginia, 35720 z.; Great Northern,
30500 z. ; Catherine Reef United, 32090 z. ;
South New Moon, 27030 z.; Hercules and
Energetic, 26950 z.; and famous Garden
Gully United, 23670 z.
* * * #
“Janus”: There may be labor trouble
because Justice Powers has ruled out 44
hours a week for deep-lead Victorian claims,
but if the men want the industry to live
they will accept the decision. Cathcart Cen-
tral is worked out, and that leaves only
the Duke and Main Leads and' the Duke
Extended in the Maryborough district; Great
Extended Consols and Chiltern Valley at
Rutlierglen and New Langi Logan and
South Langi at Ararat—the weakling pro-
geny of the old alluvial giants of the State.
The two Maryborough claims are doing a
hobble, only a shade better than the miser-
able crawl of the two Langi Logans. It’s
a toss-up whether the Extended will pull
through to test the western ground, and the
Duke Consols isn’t very sure that the wash
down the gutter will pay. The silence about
Chiltern and Rutherglen is because no single
volume could ever- contain the tale of disap-
pointment there. If Justice Powers had given
the 44 hours claimed he would have
cut the working days to 10 per fortnight,
for with the time taken to get from the
shaft to the face the Saturday shift would
have done only two hours’ mining. That
arrangement isn’t possible in deep-lead
mines where the water runs rivers, and the
clank of the pumps must ring out from day
to day and never stop. With this side of
the industry up against it owing to a
dribble of divs. coming from only the Cath-
cart Central and the Duke Consols, the
Judge’s finding was the only one possible
unless he wanted to knock the claims out
of existence. Over half a million of_ expen-
diture would then go beyond recall. ' As he
gives the mines a chance it is up to the
unions to back him up and avoid adding to
employment, as they will do if they jib at
his 47 hours ruling.
* * * *
Stock Exchange .magnates let a bank
official abolish the dole paid to Bill Rain for
acting as managing director of the Nuggetty
Ajax and shave down D. Murray’s salary as
secretary to £3 .per week. It is all typical of
the economy which the banking community is
forcing on mining folk, though word of it
rarely gets outside the bank parlor. A good
managing director is a jewel; and Rain’s
work entitled him to better treatment. But
past records don’t count much with some
bankers, who have still to learn that the
cheap man is generally the dearest when the
life-and-death struggle is faced.
* * * #
Chillagoe shareholders are naturally dis-
appointed with the sale of the copper works,
mines and railway to the Queensland Gov-
ernment. The debenture-holders had, of
course, the whole say in the matter. By the
way, in addition to the purchase of the rail-
way and works for £450,009, Etheridge rail-
way debentures are to cost the State
£225,000, raising the total price to £675,000.
* * * *
Little North Mount Farrell (Tas.) had a
gay time last half-year, for it' splashed up
£14,000 (3s. 6d. per share), partly out of
accumulated profits, The output of concen-
trates was a record; but in the old days,
when larger, quantities of high-grade crudes
were available, profits were more certain.
After writing off £2365 for depreciation and
paying taxes and dividends there was £10,487
to carry forward, which compares with
£19.259 brought • forward from the previous
half-year.
* * * *
“Sulphide”: Re South Bischoff (B.
21/12/T6). The first return from 43 tons
is 25 bags of 70 p.c. concentrates; no 45 p.c.
concentrates have been bagged, but the whole
of the tailings, assaying 11 p.c., have been
stacked for future treatment. The present
treatment is very crude, the ore being sent
direct from the battery on to a Card table
without any classification. Hence only about
half of the tin ore is being recovered. The
distance from the big mountain is about five
miles in a direct line due south. The por-
phyry face at the end of the tunnel is 9ft.
wide, showing rich ore ; and the run of stone
has so far been proved for a distance of
462 ft. and is still going strong.
Meetings of shareholders in the North
Tas. Copper and the Colebrook P. A. were
held in Launceston lately to consider the
offer of Ex-Senator Clemons, now of Lon-
don, to purchase the properties for £BOOO
each. The North Tas. meeting lapsed; at
the other the West Coast shareholders,
headed bv J. S. Munroe, led the opposition,
and the offer was turned down flat.

Sydney, January 1, 1917.
My Dear A 1:1 ,\i. —
Recent race-days were chiefly for the per-
son—prosperous and otherwise—who goes to
Ilandwick to bet. The dress-parade, joy of
the scribes and Philistines, died within the
first year of the war. It was a lingering
agony, with purple patches when somebody or
other got out for a dash of publicity. But
the Old Year fled with Tatt.’s meeting wear-
ing ordinary summer frocks to dare Satur-
day’s .sulky, spitting clouds of smoke grey.
The few regular lawn-mowers of note in-
cluded the Spencer Bruntons, who keep the
holidays in their water-lapped home at
Double Bay. The Arthur Allens, who rarely
miss a race, took daughter Margaret in foamy
pink and ivory muslin. The squatting Nevi-
sons, of Ohio, in town for the icecream
season, were in the official stand. A Bathurst
M'Phillamy or two; Lees, from the same
downs; Una Clift, the goffing lady; Mrs.
Kenna, in a pale-biscuit frock ; the Augustus
Ilookes, of Walcha, regular race visitors—
these were amongst a crowd of strange coun-
try faces in the grandstand.
The year 191 G saw the evolution of the
official stand as the Place for the Clotted
Cream. It used to be a merely male
resort. Then the ladies of the house-
bold, having been given the earlier
penned-off lawn and members’ eyrie as
something apart from the alleged “grand”
stand, began to worm their way to the house
of solemn bettors. They did it one -by one.
Mrs. Fitzribbon, having seen Mrs. Baa Sheep
climb this select altitude, inquired sternly
why she should be condemned to pace the
members’ bit of grass. The last horse carni-
vals of the weary old year saw the official
stand no longer a safe retreat for hardened
bachelors. _ Man is still permitted to con-
sider it his. But his wife personally ' con-
ducts him there!
To the long list of Judges’ sons who will
never return—one thinks of Ferguson, Street,
Simpson, llich and Duffy—is added that of
Captain Mervyn Higgins, only son of the
distinguished Equity man of the High Court
Bench. He was at the Bar and promising
well when the gong sounded and he put on
a private’s uniform.
The cable brings word that a son of Justice*
Simpson, who is a major in the A.1.F., was
married on Boxing Day to Marjorie Blax-
land, of Dubbo —great-granddaughter or
thereabouts of that Gregory Blaxland who,
with Wentworth and Lawson, found the first
way over the Blue Mountains when last cen-
tury was an infant. The Blaxland clan
traces its pedigree back someway earlier
than that, for it claims that one of the
family .save the land on which St. Augustine
built his first church when he came to Can-
terbury to convert the heathen English in
a.d. 597. The Simpson-Blaxland knot was
hitched bv parson Fred Campion, who as a
young layman in the ’9o’s was in Sydney as
secretary to his uncle, Governor Hampden,
and, afterwards returning to this country as
an Anglican priest, started in 1902. with
“Brother Charles” Matthews, the “bush bro-
therhood,” which works from Dubbo as its
headquarters. Campion went back to the
Cold Country in 1907, and had a parish at
W est Grinstead. Sussex, in the High
Church south of England where he belongs;
but. he has lately gone as rector of Leigh, in
Low Church- Lancashire, where Archgaiters
Wright comes from.
For three weeks the W. A. Holmans listen
to the wind soughing over the sea at Mona
Vale. They’ve rented a decorative cottage
near that one so often tenanted by the A. B.
Piddingtons.
Daylight Saving fell on sleepy suburbs
which had spent ISew Year’s Eve listening
to the hideous noises of the season. There
is still money to waste in this hamlet. A
. fusillade of crackers was the 1 a.m. lullaby
in various near-by suburbs. A few over-
truthful city clocks stuck to the old time,
and the 8.30 birds saw 7.30 implacably
hoisted as they hopped off the footboard.
Well get used to the taradiddle in a week
01” so.
Meantime one other little lot joins the
cow-farmers in moaning over the innovation.
Those who have charge of the Sabbath even-
fng_ church collections foresee a further
shrinkage in congregations. The ungodly
don t so nnich mind setting off to the taber-
nacle in the dusk, when there’s not much else
doing; but to go almost in the middle of
the afternoon, with at least two hours of
daylight ahead—the deacon moans and says
it is asking too much of the unrighteous.
The week’s “social” history is chiefly a
record of local movies. That portion of'the
population which gnashes the cooling salad
ia Tasmania or climbs the mountains to
catch the sunrise has been setting out after
its prey. The Andy Stuarts packed up—he
for" a trip to Adelaide, and the slim Mrs.
Andy for the Blue Hills not so far away.
One of their hopefuls came “head” of Barker
College this last term.
Mrs. Norris,'the youthful widow of the late
Colonel Bert, had a thrilling voyage to Syd-
ney. With her baby in her arms she was
more than once ready—ready to the last tag
of the lifebelt —to leap into her allotted place
in the liner’s lifeboats. But the skipper hap-
pened to be a smarter seaman than the com-
mander of the Hun submarine, and the
enemy periscope melted into the waste of
tumbling water. The feeling of the foe at
missing a ship with a lot of women and
kiddies aboard cannot be imagined.
Harry Julius takes his little wife and
Julius junior on a trip to the U.S.A. They
leave about the end of February. The Syd-
ney black-and-white artist, whose theatrical
caricatures have graced The Bulletin for
many years, has done some of his best work
under incredible difficulties. His connec-
tion with the theatrical side of Bulletin
work led him to experiment with the carica-
ture for picture-show purposes; and his suc-
cess in that line here now dures him abroad
for six months to seek a larger market for
his wares. Incidentally he is going to send
The Bulletin the Harry Julius idea of
theatrical and other celebrities who keep
Woodrow Wilson company.
The State school-teacher is a strenuous
body who dabbles in learning right up to the
moment for boiling the Christmas pudding.
The recent art classes in the Fort-street
school-grounds bring up the old question:
“Is it worth while to spoil a summer holiday
dabbling with work that keeps an artist’s
nose' to his palette all the year round?” An
exhibition of the “art” turned out by the
teachers was recently hung in the Educa-
tion Building. It answered the query with
a forceful “No!”
Another Cornstalk, lan Macleod (Mrs. H.
Randall), is getting more than a flash of
limelight because of her Australian romance,
“Hack's Brat.” Hodder and Stoughton, who
handed Katherine Prichard a plum for her
story, are the publishers. “lan Macleod”
was a West Maitland High School girl
before she tucked up her hair, and the school
still remembers her as the founder of a
magazine of some merit. Here’s the lady!
Went over the shadowy Border on
Christmas Day, Mrs. Pat MacCabe, widow
of the late F.P., of Russell Vale, in the
Wollongong district. The mother of several
handsome daughters—one is Mrs. Edward
Jenkins, and the late Harry Merewether
married another—and some upstanding sons,
Mrs. Pat lived to see her 87th milestone
ahead. The old lady’s life bridged the gap
between the Australian pioneers’ slow-toiling
bullock-drays and the modern sheep-king's
herd of motors.
In the attack on the Christmas chicken,
the Soldiers’ Club, commanded by Dr. Mary
Booth, was beaten by none. From dusty
morn to sultry eve Billjim was taken in and
very nearly done for. At about 10 p.m. the
voluntary waitresses were handing round the
final refreshments. With his' soul full of
peace, and the rest of him full of ham, poul-
try, sweets, etc., Bill gazed softly at the
aromatic coffee that breathed its incense
under his nose. “I can't do it, lady,” he
said. _ “You’ve got me heat!” Then the vol-
untaries removed mountains of earthenware
and played an impromptu carol amongst the
washing-up.
Concerning the numerous festivals
arranged for soldiers and their kiddies, the
keynote was generous super-abundance. If
we are at the same business this time next
year—a likely event —the tendency to over-
lap ought to be seen to beforehand. Too
much perishable stuff from private stores
means bursting garbage-tins when the feast
is over. Mrs. Jones didn’t know exactly
how much material comfort Mrs. Brown’s
suit-case and other baggage contained. In
consequence both good ladies were wondering
what to do with superfluous food when the
Christmas chimes rang 12.
A Sydney woman. Mrs. H. C. Macfie, went
to Melbourne the other day to take delivery
of a live legacy—an Italian greyhound of
high degree, valued at anything past 50
guineas. The breeder was the late Mrs.
Chubb, of Lichfield, famous for the delicate
dukes which attend shows in their own
motors. Mrs. Chubb, who died early in
November, was as keen a patriot as she
was a dog-fancier. On Referendum Day she
was carried, a dying woman, to the vehicle
that took her to vote “Yes.”
Manager Freddie Shipman and his small
commanding-officer with the shining locks
poised in Sydney for turkey-time.
Kate Rooney (Mrs. Kirkham) gave a
Christmas party in her parents’ home “to
meet little Billee.” The infant, of two sum-
mers or so, handed grandma Rooney a white
silk shawl and passed on an antique opal
scarf-pin to grandfather. This is our favor-
ite contralto’s first Christmas in her own
country for several years.
That blooming young matron, Mrs. Carty
Salmon, came to Sydney from Melbourne for
the holidays. She is staying with dad —Mat
Harris of Etham —till the New Year is
nicely aired.
The wedding of Private Milburn Marsh
and Miss Tucker, of Kirribilli Point, inter-
ests the oldest inhabitants. The ’groom
descends from a former S.M. who before he
took to the Bench was mixed up in pas-
toral matters with the Scotts of Glendon.
The latter entertained the explorer Leich-
hardt on the eve of his last journey.
The brothers Scott also got on to the
Bench. One Helenus, father of our own
Rose, was stationed at Newcastle. The other
delivered the law at the Old Central. The
Tuckers built Carabella House, the pic-
turesque chalet now the R.S.Y.S. head-
quarters at Kirribilli.
The Alma Bakers are summering at Mitta-
gong, where they’ve taken a house. One
does not wish to throw anything heavier than
a peanut at the Maoriland man who has
raised a pile of money for battle-planes. But
we’re getting a little weary of the “Thank
God I’m not an Australian!” attitude
assumed by Maorilanders who won’t live out-
side this continent.
Andrew Higginson, the first of the Merry
Widow’s Danilos to be heard in Australia,
has left a moderate job in “The Amber Prin-
cess” in Noo Yark, and taken a ticket back
to Sydney. The Tait brethren have given
Andrew a contract, and he will appear with
Barry Lupino and some imported Yankee
artists in the Tait production of “Very Good
Eddie” at Sydney Palace on February 10.
The Italian Opera Co. in its Maoriland
shipping experiences met the hurried stew-
ard who sees no difference between signor
and signorina. Things became beautifully
mixed when a signor, pulling off his boots
in a two- or four-berth cabin, faced some-
body else’s signora or a signorina or two,
who with flashing black eyes dared the in-
truder to even wait to pick up his debris.
After one or more acute situations the
travellers may stick to the English Mr. and
Mrs. and Miss till they get back to their
native olive-groves.
A Manly girl, Violet Rowling, only daugh-
ter of the late E. A. Rowling and Mrs. Lang-
ley. said she would when Frank Watson pro-
duced the ring. The bride, who was given
awav by her brother, Griffith Rowling, was
gowned in ivory, with a fantasy of pearl-
embroidered lace on the foamy corsage. The
usual acreage of satin train fell from the
shoulders. A cloud-like veil of white tulle
was lent by Mrs. Duguid, the Killara lady
\ylio used to be Miss Watson. Diamonds were
the glittering gems the ’groom gave his Best
Girl. Bridesmaidens Muriel Watson and
Gwen Fraser were in white, with pink mil-
linery—a cool, icecream effect these warm
days. ’Moon by motor to the Mountains.
Some famous British sculptor who turned
out the first Edith Cavell bust—l think for
Westminster Abbey—exaggerated the bows
of the nurse’s official bonnet. In marble
immense white tags under the chin make for
painful caricature. Theo Cowan has, in her
memorial of the noble nurse, kept clear of all
that. There’s just the small, neat finish
under the firm chin, with the simple lines of
the uniform to give the necessary dignity.
An Albury girl, Dorothy Andrews, daugh-
ter of a local doctor, is making herself heard
in Londop. Her good-bye recital here was
about three years ago. Since the war-cloud
burst, Dorothy has been rushing up and
down the ivory keys under a London music-
master. After her recent concert in the Big
Smoke, she was to have packed up for home
and father.
One of Pilot Murray’s pretty daughters
■was married on December 23, at St. Philip’s.
The ’groom was Horace Knight, eldest hope
of Mrs. J. P. Ward, of Vaucluse. Party at
Petty’s.
Wonder if Dr. Elizabeth Macdonald, of
the M.L. Education Department, will say
a few nice things about Sydney when she
gets back to the Hot Water Isles? Any-
how, this hamlet was very nice to her while
she was here for the pow-wow of school
doctors who examine the eyes, earache and
back teeth of the Young Idea.
Horticultural ladies ought to go and see
“The Bunyip” at the Opera House—nee
Adelphi. There’s one scene—a station de-
voted to fruit and sheep and a squatter in
brown velveteen knickers—that settles the
question. I mean: “Shall we go on the
land?” Certainly! It looks like a frolic,
and the pretty chorus girls solve the sar-
torial question. Wide and shady hats and
blue linen overalls —cut like father’s when he
pulls them on to see what is the matter with
the motor-bike —and shirt-like blouses. Just
the thing for climbing ladders and dragging
down the yellowest apricots. Only the horti-
cultural ladi-es will have to cultivate willow-
wand figures if they don’t want to look like
aldermen in sky-blue trousers.
The Ist and 53rd Comforts dames, who
did great things in the year that is cinders,
are shaking up their workers for super-
woman efforts in the January dog-days.
Hon. sec. Mrs. Glanville sounds the get-to-
work trumpet.
St. James’s Hall palpitated with hair-
bows during the “Dot and the Kangaroo”
season, arranged by Grace Stafford. The
late Miss Pedley’s charming little story was
played by enthusiastic youngsters without
missing a line. A native bear (B. Gow),
a bush magpie (M. Doherty), the mild old-
man kangaroo (M. Armstrong), the tiny
heroine Dot who lost her way, and the
uproarious Kookaburra (V. Masters) kept
the show moving. The money helps soldiers’
kiddies. As a fact, if there could be such a
modern miracle as a real pantomime for
children, this all-Australian story would be
a thriller. Even the grown-ups—the merely
mothers and aunties—enjoyed Dot’s adven-
tures in Bushland. A dance by juvenile
native companions, with suitable plumage
and black stockings, was worth any mileage
of ordinary ballet from a youngster’s point
of view.
You wouldn’t think the busy head of St.
Vincent’s Hospital had. time to go a-
Christmasing amongst the kiddies. But
there’s a staggering record of 1000 poor
youngsters with their arms filled with gifts
and their small bodies full of material joys.
The mother rectress was chieftainess, and
sent them off to see the movies with joyous
bunting waving in baby hands.
Florence Rodway went home to Hobart
for the holiday weeks. Rodway, senior, is
an unworldly sesthetic individual who would
rather catch a new species of grasshopper
than a big dentist’s fee.
The Alexandra Hospital has been sending
out . S.O.S. signals during 1916. Dad
Christmas heard the call, Dr. Clubbe said
nice things about the full stockings, and
secretary Moss whispered of favors to come
in the shape of endowed cots. Over 30,000
kiddies were helped in this haven in the just-
dead year. . Mayor Ness, of Marrickville.
spoke for his hubbub, which means to endow
a Dulwich Hill cot in 1917.
Mrs. Mary Gilmore, whose facile pen
weaves melodious verse, is not going to write
a sonnet to ptomaine. She is just getting
over the nuisance, which laid her low in
the pudding season. Mary’s mother, by the
way. the late Mrs. Cameron, was the “Busy
Bee” of the first regular woman’s column in
the Sydney Telegraph. Before that Mrs.
Henry Gullett had written up such items
as the paper thought worth while. As Mary
herself has successfully wrestled with the
ptomaine fiend, I’m not going to take stock
of all her inky activities!
Surgeon-Colonel Marshall had a long
tussle with the Man with the Hour-glass.
But he didn’t live to see the New Year sun
that shone on the graves of Gallipoli. Severe
damages on that tragic shore were the
beginning of the end. He was a member of
the. Irish-Australian medical family to
which Dr. Hamilton Marshall also belongs.
The Battalion Comforts dames are like
a Diana of the Crossways, always armed for
the chase. They hunt either singly or in
couples for every empty shop, barn or kennel
in which to exhibit their wares “on loan.”
At the Highland Gathering the 30th per-
suaded the committee to let them brew tea
and dispense soft drinks. With heroic for-
titude. and by way of snaring the Havseed
contribution, they keep open at 39 Pitt-
street over the holidays.
.Tennis is going through its annual revival
this week-end, when the country comes to
town to knock the balls over the Double Bay
nets. They begin calling “Love” on Jan. 6.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
'Melbourne, January 1, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
When the war began, Stanley Collier
dropped his course at the Uni. and enlisted.
As a lieutenant he was laid low in Meso-
potamia, invalided home and nursed for
several months in the hospital at Roseneath
(Tas.). Not to waste any time, while still
ill he took to his books again and at the Uni.
exam, the other day passed in his second
year Arts. A man of that calibre takes a lot
of stopping.
Alfred Rutter, a Melbourne scientist
whose process for sterilising milk has been
put to severe tests at the University bacterio-
logical laboratory and has survived them, is
going on with another idea which may mean
a lot to the babies of the Commonwealth.
It is the creation from cow’s-milk of a baby-
food, germless and allegedly having, in
chemical composition and physical constitu-
tion, a character almost identical with
mother’s-milk. Rutter’s scientific record and
especially his doings with calves’ food have
evidently given him a solid standing among
men of affairs, for a group of wealthy Mel-
bournites to whom hard cash and a safe •
profit are first considerations have backed
him to carry his scheme through.
Edward Pescott, horticulture-expert to the
Victorian Government, who every now and
then is obliged to come forth from the shade
of the old State apple-tree and bow his
acknowledgments for some mark of public
esteem, has once more had a free advt.
thrust upon him. A species of native bird-
orchid, new to science, has been discovered
in Victoria, and the Plant Names Commit-
tee has called it Ghiloglottis pescottiana. It
is a signal honor, of course—though the
Victorian kid will continue to call the flower
Yeller-nose, as before. The new species, by
the way, was found in the Tallangatta dis-
trict, through which the Mitta Mitta flows
down to the Murray from the high wilds—
the region whither Sentimental J. Dennis
lately retired to devote himself to the fur-
ther ‘ pursuit of litritchoor and something
new in Blokes.
May Riley, daughter of the Melbourne
bandmaster, has joined J. Bull’s army of
munitioners. The damsel went to England
in September, and, after six weeks’ special
training, was given a seat in a munition
shop. ' She mentions that every grade of
society is being thoroughly toothcombed for
munition labor. Lady Mary Hamilton is
toiling in the same shop as May Riley, and
living a life that would make her butler
swoon with horror. The noble person has
to wear blue overalls, munch her tucker at a
deal table, and absorb her tea from a
degraded tin mug—all for about 30 shillings
a week! All sorts, however, are cheerfully
accepting the conditions. A while ago the
luscious chorus of a revue co., doing a week
in a munition centre, deserted in a body and
took on cartridge-filling at a humiliating
decimal of their former screws. When the
news of their enlistment reached head-
quarters in London, the authorities had to
hobble various officers who had suddenly dis-
covered urgent public business in that
locality.
The Mina Moore-Tainsh wedding at Scots’
Church last week was an extremely subdued
affair. The hero was attended by a second,
but the bride, without hat or veil, and
dressed simply in a lace frock with pink
appointments, gave herself away in a most
independent manner. She and her hubby
are now honeymooning at Airey’s Inlet. On
their return to Melbourne they will set about
building a house at Ivanhoe.
Mrs. Sprigg, wife of the ex-sec. of the
Tramway Co., died last week. The deceased
lady, who had lived in Victoria for over half
a century, was a native of Cardiff, and was
patriotically Welsh throughout her long life.
Like W. M. Hughes and Lloyd George, she
considered the Welsh the greatest people
that this old earth grows, and their language
the finest noise ever made by the human
tongue.
Little Sumner Locke’s marriage sets a
friend of the family talking:—
None of the talent that Sumner Locke and her
many sisters inherited* from their parents (now
deceased) has taken a musical turn, strange to say.
Locke paterfamilias, a Church of England parson,
married to another English clergyman’s daughter,
was a wayward man of culture who seemed most
“divine” when he sat down to the piano. He was a
soulful amateur musician, with a pretty taste in
improvisation'. The girls would probably have taken
after Dad in that respect had he prospered as a
shepherd. But he was not the sort that runs smoothly
in a groove. He started with the disadvantage of an
artistic temperament, anyhow, and just when he
should have been making a rise in clerical life he
fell foul of his bishop—old Field Flowers Goe.
Thenceforth the struggling parson became obsessed
with his grievance. There is a tradition of how,
taking the pulpit at a fashionable South Yarra taber-
nacle whilst the vicar was on holiday, he preached
a sermon on the shabby treatment he had suffered
from Goe aforesaid. Doubtless the congregation sym-
pathised with the railing shepherd, for Goe was a
hard, evangelical bishop with “a d —d disinheriting
countenance” —a face that had made many a baby
howl at its christening. But it queered the
preacher’s church prospects finally.
Ettore Cadorin, husband of Bendigo
songstress Erna Mueller, is responsible for
the statue that rests on top of the church
that Father Robinson built at Camberwell.
This is the first sign of Cadorin for many
months, but it is understood that he will
come to Australia via America pretty soon.
The latest list of wounded includes the
name of Captain Victor Lansell, son of
Bendigo’s departed king. Mrs. Victor is
still in Bendigo cabling for further parti-
culars.
Mrs. Seaton Williams went to spend
Christmas ’midst the gum trees at Mitcham
and hoped to escape the minor trials of
country life by taking a truckload of
menials along with her. But the atmo-
sphere so demoralised the staff that
she herself had to go in person to the local
store to buy the tinned meats. Can you
imagine the Queen of Sheba going to the
butcher’s, or Cleopatra attending at the
grocer’s with a string bag? If you can’t
. then this story is wasted on you.
Some satirical “Hints for War Brides”
in a new English gift-book give an idea
how the Australian girl is getting left just
now. Her British rival is instructed to
“take one large soldier —preferably Canadian
or Australian, as they are much more tract-
able —smile sweetly until he blushes with
confusion, then seize him by the elbow and
guide him gently but firmly home to tea.”
Then he is to be tied up and hypnotised
and the marriage-service read over him to
the “Wilt thou have this woman?” point.
Papa will whisper in his ear “Will-you-have-
a-drink?” and on the victim eagerly exclaim-
ing “I will” the minister will continue, the
service. After untying him, the rest of the
family will retire. Wildly exaggerated, of
course; but it was written in England by a
Canadian soldier, and how did he get the
idea?
Women seem to have plenty of money to
spend on their little selves. The Alexandra
Clubites have .taken on a Collins-street build-
ing which is costing £2OOO to renovate; and
now the Quamby (or “Sour Grapes”) is
trying to float itself into a company, Ger-
trude Rennie having tired of single owner-
ship.
By the way, the all-prevailing bunny-wrap
is now styled the “Blood Coat” by cynical
Billjims, on the ground that the soldier’s
best girl gets the covering out of the price
of active service. In other circles the luxury
is known as the “Wages of Sin.”
Hilda Stevens, daughter of the energetic
doctor, has published a small volume of
verse. It’s a family affliction. Brother Alec
has a couple of volumes to his account.
Esther Fairbairn of the titian locks, who
had some nursing experience in St. Vincent’s
and then went to London to do war work
at a soldiers’ rest home, sends word that she
has orange blossoms in view. The bride-
groom-elect is Colonel Leonard Wheatley,
D. 5.0., a Highlander who once toiled
as a genthelp in this State with the late
Governor Fuller. The Fairbairn parents
will perhaps shut down their South Yarra
flat and go off to England to give their child
away to her soldier, who, it seems, is safely
billeted in the War Office, where there is
no fear of losing a head or a leg or a
wing before the wedding-day.
From “Rosna” in London:—
Mary Grant Bruce, the Gippsland girl, dodged the
submarines the other day and ran over from Cork,
where her major-half is training Tommies. A bonny
infant with curls all over his head and a truly Aus-
tralian grin, came with her. Her new book, “Jim
and Wally,” jumped into a second edition in a few
weeks; and she has a further volume on the way.
Miss Myrtle McMeekin, who came in on the Oster-
ley from Mortlake (Vic.) this week, was duly and
speedily knotted to her Captain Frank Robinson
Kerr, at Hampstead. Dr. Eric Kerr, the bridegroom’s
brother, gave the maiden away. Afterwards the pair
rushed off to look at Oxford, where Captain Kerr
was a Rhodes scholar before he took to chasing the
Huns and winning his D.S.O.
There were Australian parties all over London to
see the new Lord Mayor and his minions ride through
the city in their gilded chariots on Lord Mayor’s
Day. The Union Bank and the Bank of Adelaide
stopped juggling with cheques and invited hordes of
’Roo-landers to come and gaze at the show through
their windows. Also there was lunch for the hungry,
and plenty of wounded Anzacs among the guests. All
the Government offices in the Strand had tea-parties,
and in each case loads of the State’s own wounded
warriors were deposited on the premises and given
the place of honor. Wild coo-ees burst from these
windows as the Australian slice of the procession
went by; but the slice in question was on its best
behavior and refused to blink an eye.
Margaret Palmer, whose sterner parent used to be
Engineer-in-Chief of Westralia, and now oils the
Indian Public Works or something, was wedded in
this village the other day to Dr. William Burt, now
mending Tommies in the R.A.M.C.
Stella Power starred at the,Theatre Royal
Concert on Christmas night in white silk
flounces with a laurel leaf binding her dark
locks. She is a bright young Miss who looks
as if she could get to the top of the tree and
collect the money that grows there. But I
wish she would forget the startling trick
she has of lifting her dark blue eyes until
nothing is left in view but the whites. Other-
wise her stage manner and alluring smile
deserve the Order of Merit. Claude Schilling
shook himself furiously while demanding in
song, “Why do the nations so furiously rage
together?” But the young girls present re-
pented of their giggles when his silvery tenor
warbled tenderly “She is far from the land”
as an encore.
Adelaide’s harvest:—
The Port Adelaide races drew a big crowd, in
khaki and otherwise, to Cheltenham on Saturday and
Tuesday. Thirty-one thousand pounds passed through
the totalisator, so that soup tickets are not visible
yet. On Boxing Day the Goolwa Regatta, with
hydroplane racing, and swimming sports at Mount
Pleasant, led us astray. Mr. R. T. Melrose was
mainly responsible for the swims, which were held at
his Wangara farm, near the Mount. Proceeds were
for the Y.M.C.A. war fund, and our hard-working
Christian brothers are well pleased with the count-
up.
The holidays and a heat wave have completed the
exodus from town. The Fred Porters have gone for
a five-weeks’ trip to Ballarat, and Dr. J. A. G.
Hamilton has migrated to Westralia. Seaside hotels
are stuffed to the coal-cellar, and all the Mt. Lofty
and Aldgate dust-sheets are put away in the cup-
board.
The Cheer-Up Society took under its kindly wing
the soldiers who could not get home for Christmas,
and sent something special in the way of dinner to
the camp. Also lunch, dinner and a Christmas tree
were provided at the Hut; 4Off or sffo men will
vouch for that. The Hut, by the way, nearly came
to grief on Saturday night, when a mysterious fire
was discovered smouldering amongst some papers on
the balcony. The Hut is mainly asbestos sheets, but
it has a wooden framework, and the Christmas
arrangements would have collapsed disastrously if the
blaze hadn’t been discovered in time to be summarily
arrested.
Mr. R. J. Hawkes was farewelled by the executive
committee of the Soldiers’ Fund at lunch a£ the
South Australian Hotel on Wednesday. The entertain-
ment was steered by ex-Mayor A. A. Simpson, who
returned the day before from a trip to Melbourne.
His journey included an aeroplane flight in its inci-
dents.
The Port Adelaide City Council is up against a
claim for Is. lodged by an indignant medico who
complains that the disgraceful state of the Port
roads bounced his watch from his pocket against his
motor-car’s steering-wheel, to the complete destruc-
tion of the timepiece’s face-glass. The demand has
been solemnly referred to the finance committee. If
the doctor’s claim is sustained local bodies responsible
for the roads in their jurisdictions will find them-
selves on the high road to insolvency.
Mr. Ligertwood, who has been Acting-Master of
the Supreme Court for 16 months while the Master,
Major W. L. Stuart, has been on military duty as
camp-commandant at Mitcham, is going into private
practice owing to Major Stuart having resumed his
job. A farewell the other afternoon gave the officers
of the court an opportunity of wishing the auxiliary
good luck and handing him a presentation.
Lance-Corporal G. C. Davies has been credited with
the Military Medal. He was the second son of Dr.
Harold Davies, and was killed in the Big Push lead-
ing to the victory of the Somme.
English girls imported for the panto, are
struck with admiration at the graceful pre-
cocity of Australian infants. VThe children
are wonderful,” says Gretchen Yates, fresh
from London. Their teachableness, their
ready-witted response to the ideas of Miss
Everett, amazes the newcomers. And the
youngsters never weary of their work. The
rehearsals that are tasks to the grown-ups
are sport to the small fry and early-flappers.
Joy of life in English kiddies is discounted
by growing pains and anaemic languor, but
the youngsters bred in Melbourne have vital-
ity to burn. The extra vigor, alertness and
good humor are set down as the natural
results of meat and sunshine.
Willie Kelly has a new motah-cah, painted
the color of a stagnant pool and fitted with
dinky spokeless wheels. They, are solid discs
like those you see on some wheeled toys.
Willie is frankly proud of the fact that he
is among the first to get one off Fortune’s
Christmas tree.
Official methods of observing and checking
the enemy in our midst are purely Gil-
bertiau. A woman artist, after doing a
portrait of a child of mixed parentage
(brought along to her studio by the Aus-
tralian mother), was confronted by two stern
military detectives. “You have been holding
enemy meetings here!” chorused the pair
importantly, and waited for her trembling
confession. The poor artist managed to
establish her innocence, and the invaders
strolled off to worry somebody else.
Gretchen Yates, the pretty girl who plays
Princess in this year’s panto., graduated with
Courtneidge’s company at ' the London
Shaftesbury, and had a little part in “My
Lady Frayle” when he produced it a few
months ago. She comes of a stock which
mixes grease-paint and ink. Miss Gretclien’s
father’s father was the late Edmund Yates,
longtime boss of the World, a novelist in
his younger days and the cause of the
estrangement between Dickens and Thack-
eray. The “smart” journalism of 50 years
ago must have got its inspiration from the
footlights. Edmund Yates had a mummer
ancestry. George Augustus Sala’s mother
and grandmother were actresses, and
“G.A.S.” started life as a scene-painter.
Henry Labouchere, the M.P. who ran the
original Truth, married an actress and had
his dash as a theatrical speculator. Strangely
enough, not one of the trio made any sort of
hit as any sort of dramatist.
Some of the get-ups at the first night of
the panto, were truly wonderful. One d.c.
female had plundered two feather-dusters
and the Christmas poultry to provide the
material for her imitation Sioux head-dress.
Another charmer, who brought something
young and innocent as her powder-monkey,
wore an animal skin over one shoulder like
a Kaffiir kaross. But the latest thing out
was the upper portion of a damsel whose
waistbelt and corsage were interchangeable
terms, and flimsy shoulder-straps merely
kept up appearances.
Half the local peerage hurried out of
town to eat plum-pudding at seaside or
mountain refuges, not because they wanted
to join the crowds but because Mary Annette
and her mates were having a spell off for
Christmas frivols. When Mary lets go the
whole domestic world seems to fall in ruins.
The Bowes Kelly tribe have done the
annual shift to their Tasmanian sheep acres,
leaving grandmother and auntie Isabel
Millar to keep the moths out of their Mal-
vern mansion. Lieutenant Moncton, the
elder son, is now back in the trenches after
a spell in hospital with a wounded arm.
Warrnamhool started a decentralisation
league a while ago. It got a real live secre-
trary, and was going to make the old potato
town boom and cut Melbourne’s claws. The
other day I asked a visiting citizen what
had become of the movement. “Oh,” he said,
“the secretary shifted to Melbourne, and
then the thing busted up !”
Jessie Masson, one of our most conspicu-
ous pianists, lately headed for Maoriland in
the company of Elsie Treweelt, the dark-
haired soprano, flautist Amadio, and Max
Selinsky, the Russian fiddler. The party
are taking a bale of classical and modern
tunes, and threaten fo pour their best into
the Dominion’s ear during an eight weeks’
tour.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
Two Seas” : There seems to be a diversity
of opinion as to the jumping powers of the ’roo. As
one who has shot and snared as many of this wild
fowl as the next man, pass me the kip. Someone
entered a flier with a record of 31ft. “Pick” intro-
duces a methodical old plugger who covered 27ft.
“with undeviating regularity”—evidently trained by
a surveyor with an eye for cheap labor. Then “Hay”
weighs in with a badly-trained heavyweight that
crashed into a five-wire fence instead of clearing
it, and, incidentally, broke all the wires, uprooted
a post and removed the landscape—a proceeding that
suggests an enraged elephant hurrying to catch the
last boat to Manly after the barrier is closed.
“Hay” gives 19ft. as a fair average hop, and believes
that, when playing, the ’roo can leap as many feet
in the air as he can jump on the level. That being
so, how can we account for the fact that so many of
the tribe are found hanging with their hind legs
twisted in the two top wires of a fence? One would
think that when going for their lives they would
clear the top wire, say 3ft. Bin., in their stride if
they could uplift themselves 19ft. for a joke. I have
assisted in erecting wings and yards for ’roo drives,
hut have never seen the fences put up as high as a
telegraph-line. My experience leads me to believe
that the ’roo is not a natural jumper. Except when
he is startled or closely pressed in a chase, he
prefers crawling under the bottom wire to jumping
the top one. I have caught hundreds by hanging
wire-snares over the tracks they scratch under the
fences. I never happened to be carrying a tape-
measure when ’roo hunting, so can’t say how many
.\ards they took in a stride on the level; but I
doubt if it ever exceeded six. Up hill they declined
to tiavel at all, though down hill they can fall a
long way without striking the ground. But I’ve
done that myself when I wasn’t a trier. One thing
I haven’t seen mentioned is the extraordinary brittle-
ness of the roo s bones. Often when a mob has been
startled by a shot I have seen one break its leg in
the hurry to get off the mark quickly when the
old man on watch banged his tail on the ground
as a signal to start. His tail, by the way, is always
stuck straight out when he is sitting on guard ; yet
on at least one coat of arms exhibited in Sydney our
national freak is represented with his tail curled
aiourid his toes like that of Souter's cynical Tom.
* * * *
“McNorth” : The worst pastime I know of
is ti avelling per boot along the endless beaches of
the Gulf of Papua. There is not a particle of shade,
and the sun always seems to stoke up a bit extra
when it has a mere white at its mercy. An ever-
lasting curve of beach stretches ahead, and the hot
and weary traveller is certain that the next point is
his destination. When he reaches it he finds another
and yet another indentation stretching ahead into
the distance. As the day goes on the continual
movement of the sea alongside gets on his nerves,
and the beach seems to dance and sway about under-
foot. Occasionally a village heaves in sight, and a
native steps out with a karu (drinking cocoanut)
ready opened. The traveller, tired to death of the
eternal cocoanut juice, and thinking of cool streams
of running mountain water, and of the long, foam-
ing beers he had refused at Port Moresby, gargles
the half-sweet stuff and hurries on. Sometimes he
goes up from the beach and sits in the shade of
some bushes to await his straggling team of
carriers. Though some of them may be carrying
nothing heavier than a frying-pan or billycan they
never seem to be able to keep up with a white man.
When, at last, he reaches his night’s camp, usually
at a village, he scoffs a miserable meal that comes
mostly out of a tin, crawls beneath a mosquito-net,
and sleeps fitfully to the accompaniment of the shrill
yelps of a herd of degraded village mongrels and the
animated conversation of some natives under the
house. At daylight he boots his niggers into action,
and the previous day’s misery starts all over again.
It’s great fun.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: November is the season of
poisoned honey in Maoriland. The busy insect which
gathers flower-juice fails to note that the wharangi
or rangiora is as venomous as the breath of slander.
The white blossom carries a heavenly odor like unto
the cool cellars of a brewery or a refrigerated tub
of the balm of Gilead ; but many a bushman has
felt his skin cracking when he has been gassed by
whiffs of rangiora. Also, the leaves send any horse
which eats them into a state of riotous behavior.
Best plan is to lead the nag around or smoke his
nose with something like burnt feathers. As to
men, though mere sniffing isn’t fatal, I have known
several individuals who have passed in their checks
after a banquet on the bush honey of the rangiora
season.

It used to be the wolf that borrowed the
sheep s clothing now it s the snake. One of the
butchers at the freezing works near Forbes (N.S.W.)
got hold of a big woolly jumbuck lately and was
half-way through with the murder when a vicious
ut+io , , , . ~ „
little snake, bounced out of the fleece. When it
reads of this, the next shearers’ log will demand
that all sheep be searched for reptiles before being
penned.
“Tas”: Gordon River, Tassy’s largest
stream, flows through almost virgin wilderness to
Macquarie Harbor. It is a century now since Kelly
and his four white men —the first seen there —
rowed their whaleboat upstream. He noted the
abundant Huon pine, and his brig Sophia took the
first cargo thence to Hobart in 1816. Many mil-
lions of feet have since been floated down the Gor-
don and King, but a few timber-getting families
are still the only dwellers. The price is higher
than ever, but nearly all the accessible stuff has
been cut. The piners formerly went nearly 100
miles up the Gordon, disregarding its rapids. There
is much West Coast pine yet, but it is further
north, towards Zeehan. The custom has been to
roll the logs to little creeks and wait for a fresh
to send them down river, to be rafted at convenient
spots. A good deal of cut pin.- never got its fresh,
and there it stays. Horses and bullocks have not
been used. Rain falls nearly every day for eight
months in the year, and is frequent during the
brief summer. Plenty of fish, but little game.
Milder even than the Gordon country is that round
Port Davey to the south-west. Pine grows in nar-
row belts along the streams, and the rugged country
is lashed almost incessantly by westerly gales from
an ocean without a break to Patagonia. Throw
another log on the fire!
* * * *
“Bad Australian”: The algarroba tree is
an old friend of mine, and I have met several mem-
bers of the family in South America, some of them
petrified. The red algarroba ( Prosopis panta ) is a
small, bushy tree. The timber is hard, a kind of
orange color, and is used occasionally for cabinet
work. The seed-pod is eatable in its natural state,
and when fermented makes a diuretic and palatable
booze. The white algarroba ( Prosopis alba ) grows
much larger than the red. The wood is strong, light
and of a reddish-yellow color, showing very hand-
some grain and markings, and burns like dry white
box. It bears large quantities of an exceedingly
sweet pale-yellow fruit, which is a favorite article of
food with the Indians, and they run their jags and
dreadfully immoral saturnalias on a liquor called
aloja made from it. The black algarroba ( Prosopis
nigra ) attains about the same dimensions as the
white. Its timber is much darker in color, stronger
and heavier. It is also very gummy or resinous, and
the same kind of calamity-pop is extracted from its
fruit. Algarrobillo ( Prosopis liumilis) is a small,
scrubby variety resembling sticks of prickly acacia,
growing in bunches with thorns about an
inch long distributed on the stems at in-
tervals of about two inches. This plant
flourishes in places where the only moisture avail-
able is that which it extracts from the atmosphere.
All algarroba products except the wood make good
stock fodder, and the Indians produce a kind of bread
or johnny-cake called 'patay from the seed-pods or
fruit. Prom certain abstruse calculations I have
made on the subject 1 think it takes this vegetable
about 1000 years to develop into anything worth
considering.

IRONBARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It’s a very thirsty spot,
Always somnolent and hot —•
Ironbark.
It suffers from the drought,
And the quadrupeds about
Look as if they’d just come out
Of the ark.
A dusty road winds by,
And the creek is mostly dry
When you pass ;
The earth is cracked and bare,
Though you’ll notice, here and there,
Wisps of straw the worse for wear —
That’s the grass.
The local brand of beer,
Brown she-oak, is somewhat dear;
But it serves
To drown the town’s regret
For the ample lack of wet,
That in time is apt to get
On its nerves.
The whole place seems a-drowse—
Cats, humans, fowls and cows
Are inert;
While a dog with wistful eyes
In the empty horse-trough lies.
Only tireless ants and flies
Make a spurt.
Weary rabbits creekward lope
From the bracken on the slope,
Full of doubt
Of this truce of friends and foes;
And dejected birds in rows
On the paling fence, repose,
Tired out.
A horse against the rail
Whisks the flies, with languid tail,
When they gall;
He is spavined, old and spare,
But he yet contrives to wear
Quite a philosophic air
Through it all.
But the sun to westward rides,
And his wilting glare subsides
As he dips
To his sullen, sultry bed,
With a parting shaft of red
Which the trees catch, overhead,
At their tips.
Then a world more gently hewn,
Under wide skies, star-bestrewn,
Still, and dark—
Or,, when the moon glides bright,
Lit with a tempered light—
Is yours with the fragrant night,
Ironbark!
Then leaves exhale their scent
And night-winds sigh content,
And the dew
Refills the cups of gold
That thirsty plants uphold,
And bids their blooms unfold
And renew;
When, like a Wind of Ease
Blown through the rustling trees,
You shall mark
This as a sweetened lot,
Changed to a pleasant spot—
No longer dry and hot
Ironbark !
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

“R.E.”: If “Aneb” (B. 2/11/16) is still
inquiring how to use carbon for rabbits tell him to
get a. machine if he has much to do. It will run to
about a fiver, and will save its cost in no time. If
he intends to do the cottonwaste-and-stiek act let
him try this way. Open the drum of carbon, get a
kerosene syphon, fill the syphon with water and put
it into the drum. Draw off about a quart into a
billy, fill the drum up with water and keep full. He
can leave the syphon in it as long as he has water
on top. Put about a cup of water in the billy.
I his will prevent evaporation. To use, plunge the
rag on the stick through the water to the bottom of
the billy, and then push the rag as far down the
burrow as he can. Cover the hole quickly and tread
down hard. If the soil .is heavy and the burrows
deep it does not matter what time he gets to work,
but in light, sandy soils the fumes will work through
unless ground is wet. The reason for lighting- is
to make a smoke so as to be able to see any small
holes that otherwise would be missed. The machines
make a smoke as well as pump carbon. Did “Aneb”
ever try paper ? Get a good big wad of war-lies, dig
the burrow back to solid ground, ram the lies down
the burrow’s throat as far as possible with the end
of shovel and fill with earth. Spread a sheet of
paper on top, and half-cover with earth. This will
keep the visiting bunny from calling. If the ground
has been trapped over, this is one of the best things
going. Watch the burrows closely, and if one of
them is opened from inside get the dogs, wife and
gun and hunt for his nibs, as he will not go back
into the burrow again.

‘‘Leo Lear”: In his description of the
pheasant-coueal and his habits, “Wandi” (B.
23/11/’l6) leaves out the most interesting point
about the unique Northern Australian bird. It is a
true cuckoo, one of the largest of the clan, and yet
brings up its own family in its own nest. And
whereas all other cuckoos are migrating, this fellow
is stationary, or nearly so.
“Silas Snell” : This is the tale of a lady
on the land. She was a farmer’s daughter in York-
shire, and coming to Australia had always an itch to
have a try at inducing the wilderness to blossom. Her
opportunity came when her husband went to the war,
and was hit by something from Krupp’s.' Mrs.
Wheeler bought 300 acres at Blowhard, and got right
down to business. On the second day after entering
into possession she saw a tallish, hard-baked, be-
whiskered stranger in his shirt-sleeves slouch into the
yard, slouch round a bit, then slouch to the kitchen-
door. He leaned there. “Mornin’, Missus,” he said.
“Jist called neighborly t‘ say you was welcome t’
Blowhard. Name’s Bigger, Missus. Live over the
’ill. Anythin’ I kin do, d’yeh think?” Mrs. Wheeler
thanked Mr. Bigger, but she was managing very well.
Bigger moved away, then came back. “You’d like a bit
iv blackfish,” he said. He went away. Half an hour
or so later he returned, went through the same pre-
amble, and said: “Me name’s Bigger. We’re neigh-
bors. Thought if you was needin’ ’elp I might lend a
hand.” “No, thank you, Mr. Bigger. I am doing
very well.” “So-long, then,” said Bigger. But he
turned at the gate. “I’ll git yeh a bit iv blackfish,”
he said. In the afternoon Mr. Bigger called again.
I heard you was here,” he said. “Me name’s Bigger,
ma’am. Live ’cross the ’ill. Anythin’ I kin do for
yeh at all, I will.” “Really, no, Mr. Bigger; there
is nothing I need.” Bigger turned away. “I’ll p'r’aps
hook yeh a bit iv blackfish,” he said. At four o’clock
Bigger called again; at a quarter past he was back
once more. Practically the same programme on both
occasions. At eight that night Mrs. Cussin, from
down the “crick,” looked in. She found Mrs. Wheeler
in a pitiable condition, facing five large lots of black-
fish on the kitchen table. “They didn’t tell me I
was to live within half a mile of a wretched idiot
when I bought this place,” she said. “It’s a map
named Bigger. He has called five times, offering
help, and he’s brought me five different gifts of black-
fish- He frightened me nearly to death. Heaven
knows what might have happened if I hadn’t humored
him!” At this point Mrs. Wheeler screamed. A
tallish, hard-baked, bewhiskered man had appeared in
the doorway, and was saying, “Evenin’, Missus. My
name’s Bigger.” Mrs. Cussin came to the rescue.
“It s all right, my dear,” she said. “This one’s the
father. They dress alike, the Biggers do, ’n’ look
alike, n act alike, so that even one like me what’s
seen ’em grow up can scarce tell one from the other.
But they ain’t mad. The fact is, there’s really five of
’em, two twins and a single.”
“Leo Lear”: “Dead. Finish” (B.
30/11/’l6) hasn’t unearthed all the aliases of treacle
in the bush. Some years ago 1 went into a store in
Biggenden (Q.) and asked for a 21b. tin of treacle.
•‘Oh, we call it fly bog,” said the missus behind the
counter.

“Stapleton”: Twenty years ago, when I
went to school, one thing firmly impressed upon me
was that all Australian trees are evergreen, shedding
their bark but not their leaves periodically. But in
the N.T., during July and August, nearly all trees
shed their leaves, and a photo, of the N.T. during
those months might better represent winter in Eng-
land than in Australia. The majority of the trees
are then absolutely bare, only pandanus and paper-
barks retaining their verdure.

sioner west o’ sunset going below in a coffin made
from a kerosene case which displayed the following:
“150° fire test.” I never heard whether he benefited
much. ’

“Wart” : Can any Abo-fisherman identify
one for me? In 20 years’ fishing this is the first
time I have caught it, and it worries me. It came
out of quite fresh water, in the North Esk, near
Launceston (Tas.), with my hook and a common
worm in its gullet. Place, just above the tidal
influence. The prisoner was six inches long, light
grey, large head tapering down to a small tail;
in fact, it was so very like a flathead that if you
placed it in a box with a dozen small ’uns of the
latter breed you would need to look twice to pick it
out again. Good to eat, flesh white and sweet, not
quite so firm as flathead. Can a fish like a flat-
head live for a time in fresh water, or is there a
species of fresh-water flat ?
“Hay” : One of the quaintest birds I have
ever met is the cat-faced owl, which I think must
be the largest of the owl family. I have only seen
his queer-looking dial away in the far interior. Binghi
calls him “tandooka meeki,” meaning devil’s eye.
His body is darkish grey, and his broad, round face
is surrounded by a deep frill of snowy whiskers,
lie nas a. thick, hooked beak, and a pair of eyes
like small motor lamps. He makes his nest (if it
can be called a nest) in a hollow tree. I inspected
one and had to beat a hurried retreat. It looked
as if Mi. and Mrs. Catface had collected gohannas,
lizards and rats for many months before my visit.
Several small owls reposed comfortably in the
midst of the garbage, but I didn’t stop to count
them. In the daytime they retire to dark places
such as caves and hollow trees. Being inquisitive
and not at all timid, it is common at night to
see one meditating on the tent pole. On one occa-
sion a new arrival Outback bad made his bed under
a tarpaulin thrown over the shafts of a dray. When
he woke in the middle of the night and saw one
of these weird birds gazing solemnly at him from
the end of a shaft, he knew the spirit of Wowser-
ism had sought him out to call him to account for
his past.
“Abe”: Look out till I send along a
team owned and driven by Jim Prowse, Bob Crain
and another. The load was a winch and boiler on a
trolly which had to be shifted from the foot to the
top of the hill at the Great Gibraltar G.M. at
Adelong (N.S.W.) some years back. It took 54
as good bullocks as ever heard pure Australian and
two long days to traverse the round-about four
miles. It was a very wet winter and the outfit on
the trolly was generally just visible like a sub.’s
periscope above the water.

THE MISCHIEVOUS WAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
. R almost generally believed that the excessive
ram with which Victoria has been visited is in some
way due to the tremendous cannon fire in Europe.”
— Melb. paper.
The river broke its banks at Yam,
And spread a waste of water round.
Canoodle was a mighty dam,
And all the world seemed seaward bound.
E’en Murphy’s cow was seen to shift
On with the universal drift.
She was as round as any hull,
Her spars were hoisted In the air,
And Murphy said, with spirit dull:
“Me finest milker, I declare!
But mark you this—jist as before,
Thim fools will go on wid their war!”
VICTORIA.
