“M.P.A.”: At Colac (Y T ic.) the local pic-
ture show is entirely dependent on a wheezy
old steam plant to produce light for the
movies. The old chap whose job is to keep
the kettle boiling often raises a dreadful
smell in doing so. The other week such a
violent incense pervaded the hall that the
writer went in search of the nuisance and
found the ancient fireman heaping handfuls
of barbers’ clippings on the leaping flames.
The ancient said cocky farmers’ whiskers
brought the pressure up wonderfully, so he
commandeered the barbers’ boxes thereabouts.
Fortunately the local barbers don’t run the
show, otherwise a big-bearded cocky wouldn’t
be safe within ten miles of the town.
“E.T.”: Although Comyns Carr, whose
passing out in England is cabled, wrote many
“commercial” plays alone and in collabora-
tion, he was none the less an apostle of Art
with a big A. Consequently it will not sur-
prise anyone to learn that he died stony-
broke. It is only a. few months ago that the
British Government granted him a Civil List
pension. Carr was connected in his early
days with the Grosvenor Gallery in fashion-
able Bond-street, where the rejecteds of the
Royal Academy and their friends and sym-
pathisers used to exhibit. Afterwards he
was to the fore in the movement that cul-
minated in the opening of the New Gallery
in the centre of Regent-street, which pro-
fessedly was to stand as a rival to the R.A.
lie lived long enough to see it turned into a
picture-show of another kind. The movies
went into the building three or four years
ago, and have been there ever since.
“Ike Pagliacci”: The Fullers, starting
from a waxworks and variety show in M.L.
between 30 and 40 years ago, now run
vaudeville, etc., wherever vaudeville can be
run in Australia, and they have tried to
run it in places where it refused even to
hobble. In Melbourne they have vaudeville,
burlesque and Shakspeare. In Auckland
the firm has put stock drama on the market.
In Wellington the entire local spaghetti sup-
ply has been eaten up by its Italian Grand
Opera Co., and in Sydney it is busy with
pantomime. Incidentally it finds time to
be interested in a hundred or so picture-
shows.
“0.K.” : Apropos the theory that modern
juvenile crime is one of the sinful pro-
ducts of picture-shows, G. R. Sims reminds
ns that International Congresses for the
discussion of juvenile depravity were intro-
duced when Edison was a young man and
the movies had not been generally dreamed
of. Sims remembers the case of two Cock-
ney urchins murdering their mother in
order to rob her corpse of a shilling where-
with to buy their joint admission to a
cricket match. Also he remembers the case
of tlie two nine-year-old boys enclosing a
much younger kiddy in a dustbin, with a
view to getting rewarded for discovering his
body when he should have breathed his last.
This was in 1885, at which time there was
an epidemic of cold-blooded murder among
the young. And there were no movies to
incite juvenile depravity in 1885.
“Floater” : “Bob D.’s” list of Australians
in the American movies (B. 7/12/T6) omits
Elsie Wilson and Rupert Julian, who are
high up on the paysheet of one of the largest
film corporations. They are regularly allotted
the leading parts in the fashionable five-
reelers, and make a very striking couple, as
many picture enthusiasts in this country
have discovered. Rupert was a promising
juvenile here before he left to try his luck in
the Big Republic. Elsie, who is, of course,
Mrs. Rupert, and a sister of Nellie, the
former J. C. Williamson favorite, used to
shake her scarlet tresses in the legitimate in
Australia. She appeared conspicuously with
J. Knight in “Raffles,” “A Royal Divorce,”
“The Prisoner of Zenda,” and other pieces.
The opera lately written around Sheridan’s
“Critic” by the English composer Stanford
contains one of the most remarkable jokes
on record. He has brought in all sorts of
musical quotations, from Beethoven’s Choral
Symphony to “Auld Lang Syne,” and a bur-
lesque of Italian opera. Now, Sheridan
made Don Whiskerandos, when wounded in
a fencing-duel, exclaim, “O cursfed parry!”
And on that the orchestra now obliges with a
famliar strain from “Blest Pair of Sirens,”
by Stanford’s friend and rival, Sir Hubert
Parry!
“I Pagliacci”: I have just discovered
Mexico. It seems it isn’t all wild half-
breeds perpetually dodging somebody’s gun.
Talking to Sefior Carlos Yanzuelos, jun., he
confided to me that he had not yet found in
Australia the National Theatre devoted
entirely to grand opera such as exists in
Mexico City. Nor has he come upon any
theatre with the up-to-date Mexican idea of
having boxes in the wings for people who
are in mourning and who do not wish to be
seen. Neither has he noticed a law prohibit-
ing managers from exaggerating their shows
in lithographs, etc. The Mexican showman
must display exactly what he represents on
his advts.! A rotten place for musical-
comedy press-drummers, I should think!
THE DIVINE ARTIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Is acting man’s work?” was being warmly
debated in New York when the last mail left. The
bulk of opinion favored the idea that eventually
woman will possess the stage, leaving man free to
tasks more in keeping- with his majesty.
I put the case to Ichabod;
A fine old-fashioned actor he.
He listened gravely. With a nod
11c cleared his throat, and answered me.
“The greatest of the arts,” he said.
“Ah, here is grandeur if you will!
Here poetry and music wed
With passion and the limner’s skill.
“All that the painter does I do.
The writer writes, but I portray.
The sculptor’s forms I fashion, too;
All man’s emotions feel my sway.
“Great Shakspeare lives if I have speech,
And then no longer Homer nods.
Man’s work ? Nay, nay! Can mere man reach
The true vocation of the gods?”
VICTORIA.
STARGAZER.
Business, Robbery, etc.
“Don Q.”: There’s a good time coming
for Victorian brown coal. Yankee engineers,
searching for a substitute for the rapidly-
failing oil fuel, reckon that powdered coal
is the future fuel for steam production. The
New York Central Railroad is the pioneer
in research work of this kind. The co. has
for over a year been trying specially built
locomotives using powdered coal as fuel, and
reckons it will be independent of oil. The
-'process is to mill the coal, pulverising and
rolling it until it is about as fine as flour.
The finer the crushing the better the result.
A 200-to-the-inch mesh is recommended.
Another essential is perfect dryness. The
powder is blown into the furnace just as
oil fuel is sprayed in. It is consumed
instantaneously and burns practically with-
out ash or clinker. The very last ounce of
power is thus taken out of the fuel, whereas
by the old method of burning in lumps some
20 per cent, is lost. And there are no
sparks pouring from the funnels either.
The method of pulverising is peculiarly
suited to a soft or poor coal. The increased
heating power more than pays for the cost
of milling, while a considerable saving is
made in handling. The powder is loaded
through suction pipes almost as easily as
oil fuel or water is pumped in. The
greatest drawback is the necessity for abso-
lute dryness of the powder, but in our
sunny Australian climate this is an easy
•problem.
“Moo”: The American Meat Trust is
gradually getting its tentacles on the indus-
try in Australia and Maoriland. Its latest
acquisition is the meat-exporting business
of the late A. L. Joseph, Christchurch
(M.L.), and that was about the best of its
sort in the shivery islands. The capital
at the command of the huge combine is so
large and distributed with such lavish hands,
and its case is presented so speciously, that
the grower is easily seduced, particularly
when large immediate profits will accrue to
him. The combine is prepared figuratively
to. pay away a half-crown for a two-shilling
piece. Later it has them both.
* * * *
It happened outside the Melbourne Town
Hall the other night. Some persons of im-
portance were departing in a thousand-pound
motor, when a party in black, leaning on
one of the pillars, suddenly pounced upon
something lying on the pavement. It was
a nice, clean Treasury note. An envious
and excited youth chimed’ in. “It’s ’is,” he
said. “It’s ’is [pointing to the motor]. I
saw him drop it.” “Hush! Don’t make
such a noise,” answered the finder coldly,
as he pocketed his prize and moved off. “He
can better afford to lose it than I can.”
* * * *
“Foe”: Everyone but the live cottontail
will be glad to hear that rabbit-skins had a
record sale-in London the other day, prices
jumping from 20 to 50 per cent. YVe may
not have sufficient energy to manufacture
motor-cars and things, but we can catch
and skin the national curse with the short
tail and the lone ears. If only some
foreigner will next tell us how to make a
delicious breakfast food or new beer out of
the prickly-pear, Australia Will Be There!
* * * *
“T. 0.1.” : Just demolished in King-street,
Sydney, a building which in its time served
many purposes. It stood on the south side,
about midway ’twixt Pitt and Castlereagh
streets. In the first ’6o’s it was the Cafe
de Paris, kept by Gus Wangenhein, and for
many years on the walls and marble tables
were examples of his black-and-white art.
After Wangenheim left King-street the cafe
became an ordinary pub known as Schroder’s.
Then the very youthful Daily Telegraph
migrated from York-street and held the
premises for some years until it built a
few doors east. After that came Quong
Tart, whose tearooms held the place until
they went out with the century.
* * * *
“8.1. D.”: The other day I bought a
nicely-finished little toy boat for the kid.
As soon as he could get his mother’s hat
on he rushed down to the beach to sail it.
But he didn’t go a long voyage. The Dread-
nought had hardly touched the water before
it started to sort of melt to pieces, and I
found it was made of cardboard and paper,
ballasted with paint and sand. The marks
on the paper showed that it was made in—
well, where do you think? Right, first guess.
“Two Seas”: Mention of Bernacchi by
“M. or N.” (B. 30/11/T6) recalls the fact
that bull-kelp was not the only spec, that
that enterprising Italian employed his
talents unon at Maria Island (Tas.). Tan-
ning kelp for leather was an easy proposi-
tion compared with his attempt to grow
grapes and establish the wine industry upon
that desolate spot. Nevertheless, Bernacchi
spent a lot of time and good money in plant-
ing out his vineyard. The vines grew all
right, and in due course a large party arrived
on the island by invitation to sample the first
crop and incidentally to apply for shares in
the venture. No one was permitted inside
the vineyard fence, but quantities of fine
grapes could be seen upon the vines, and
generous samples were cut and brought to
the visitors. Everyone was delighted to find
that the much-discussed experiment had
succeeded. But, later, some envious scoun-
drel divulged the secret that Nature had been
assisted by tying imported grapes to the
unfruitful vines.
* *• * *
It is our bad luck that he should bloom
and burst in our time, but Evolution knew
what it was about when it produced the Hun.
Up on the North Coast (N.S.W.) the flying-
foxes have been growing too numerous for
the guns, and the residents were staring
helplessly at the pests and wondering what
could be done until some bright spirit sug-
gested that they be gassed. Now everybody
is smiling again and thinking more kindly
of Wilhelm Von Stinkpot.
* * * *
“Murrayite” : Australian timber? Well,
try this. A barge on the Murray was re-
cently docked and the examining surveyor
reported that there were not six inches of
unsound timber in her bottom. This after
67 years afloat. Capt. McDonald assured
me that when they jacked her up she lifted
straight as a die from end to end. The
timber is S. Aus. blue-gum, and the skipper,
who is also the owner, reckons on getting
another 5Q years’ service out of her.
The Government orchard at Mylor (S.A.)
records a weird bit of fruit storage. Last
season’s big apple-crop lay about on the
ground. In June a lot of it was dug in. In
November ploughing was begun, and up
came a lot of the apples “in perfect condi-
tion.” Nearly 50 inches of rain had fallen
in the record winter between.
* *• * *
“Kossy”: The most cheerful item in
recent cables was the instance of poetic
justice involved, in the case of the Polish
count who has been arrested at Posen in
the midst of his patriotic machinations. He
had nobly avenged the wrongs of his down-
trodden country by swindling the conquer-
ing Hun out of fabulous sums in grain deals.
Dr. Johnson’s dictum that patriotism is the
last refuge of scoundrels will now have to
be reversed. In Poland, anyway, scoundrel-
ism is the last refuge of patriots. And may
it continue “till like a sea of glory it spreads
from Pole to Pole” !
* * * *
“Ses”: Tobacco manufacture was one of
the first industries established in Australia.
There were 11 factories in N. S. Wales in
1861; yet leaf-growing had progressed very
little half a century later. In 1888-9 the
Australian crop amounted to 6641 acres, of
which two-thirds was in N.S.W.; in 1913
it had diminished to 3007 acres. Yet few
things offer better prospects than tobacco,
though more labor is required in raising and
curing the leaf than is demanded by most
weeds. It is also a product that can’t be
got rid of as soon as it is harvested ; it has
to be kept curing in sheds. This and the
hard-dying prejudice against the local pro-
duct were mainly responsible for the early
slump—an undeserved slump, for our black
tobaccos at least are quite as good as any
foreigner’s.
THE OLD BLACK-DIAMOND LINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The busy steamers ply their trade,
Their engines throb and hum,
And round the coast their tracks are laid,
Daily they go and come.
The coal-boats put to sea, and leave
The Nobbies far astern,
And, rough or fine, they shove and heave,
Good dividends to earn.
Their soot-fouled funnels spew forth smoke,
Their screws chastise the brine.
/ sing of ships of teak and oak,
The Old Black Diamond Line!
They had no “fair wind in the hold,”
No piean of whirling screw,
These little ships that pitched and rolled
Their ecal to Wallaroo.
And back again, with ore in bulk,
Leaping the Hunter bar,
Each waddling, battling brave old hulk
“Tied up” at Waratah.
Where are they now, these good old ships?
And Simpson, does he know?
Maybe he’s counting up their trips
Beyond the sunset’s glow.
Athena, Saxon, Moonta, too,
Kadina, and the rest;
The J. L. Hall and Wallaroo
Are shanghaied, Time-impressed.
Somewhere behind the furthest foam,
Where sheaves can never jamb,
Cruising, maybe, the Callaholme
And eke the Verulam.
These ships lived in a hempen time;
No beauty prize they’d earn ;
Their bows were cut off in their prime,
Likewise each old square stern.
Their skippers they were sailormen
In all that word could mean ;
And all of them who battled then
Have passed to Fiddler’s Green.
There’s Farrell, Christie, Cowan, Gadd,
Bunker and Trevett tall, .
And not forgetting my own dad—
Signed on for “good and all.”
Here where the smoke-stained steamers ply,
To serve the mart and mine,
Grey, ghostly ships go floating by—•
The Old Black Diamond Line!
Their sidelights burn dull red and green,
Seeming like dead ships’ eyes,
Seeking the tracks where they have been,
Old tracks, they used to prize.
And sometimes when the moon is bright,
And stars consent to shine,
I see them drifting through the night—-
The Old Black Diamond Line!
N.S.W.
WALT. J. SNADDEN.
AN ILL-TIMED VISIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We sit upon the table-top,
Our shoes and socks a-swill.
The rain that does not know to stop
Has oceans yet to spill.
Wild streams have drowned the dining-room;
And through a broken pane
A solemn cat with eyes of gloom
Floats in and out again.
Submerged is every bed, the chairs
Are floating one and all,
And wildly races down the stairs
A splendid waterfall.
The drowned man’s eyes do much affect
My nerves (they’re weak of late) —
He called this morning to collect
The half-year’s water rate.
VICTORIA.
NOAH.

WHEREAS by an Order of the High Court of
Justice Chancery Division dated 17tli July 1916
made in the matter of the Estate of GEORGE
POCOCK deceased and in an action of PITHIE and
another v. POCOCK and others (1916 P. 821) the
following inquiry was directed namely: —
“An Inquiry whether FRANCIS ALDERSEY
TURNOUR POCOCK (a son of GEORGE PEARCE
POCOCK) is living or dead and if dead when he
died and whether he died under the age of 21 years
and if he attained the age of 21 years whether he
died testate or intestate and who is now beneficially
entitled to his share under the Will of the Testator
GEORGE POCOCK.”
Notice is hereby given that the said FRANCIS
ALDERSEY TURNOUR POCOCK-or any person or
persons claiming- to be entitled under the said
inquiry are personally or by his or their Solicitors on
or before Tuesday the Ist May 1917 to come in and
prove their claims at the Chambers of Mr. Justice
Sargant and Mr. Justice Younger at the Royal Courts
of Justice Strand London England and to enter
their names and the particulars of their claims in
a book kept for that purpose in Room 299 at the
said Royal Courts of Justice or in default thereof
they will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit
of the said Order. Tuesday the Bth day of May 1917
at 11 of the clock in the forenoon at the said Cham-
bers (Room 297) is appointed for heaving and adjudi-
cating upon the claims.
Dated this 6th day of November 1916.
JtIOIID. WHITE
Master.
NOTE. —The said FRANCIS ALDERSEY
TURNOUR POCOCK is the son of George Pearce
Pocock and Fannv Pocock. He sailed from England
for Adelaide Australia in July 1863 being then of
the age of 16 years. He is believed to have gone
into the country stock keeping and when last heard
of he was going to New South Wales.
BIRD & ELDRIDGES,
10 Great James Street,
Bedford Row, London, W.C.
Agents for Moore Rawlins & Vicary, of Lymington,
Hants.

IN DIVORCE. IN THE- SUPREME COURT OF
1 NEW ZEALAND. NORTHERN DISTRICT. No. 991.
Between ELIZABETH MACKENZIE, of the City of
Auckland, wife of Alexis Gveig Mackenzie, Petitioner,
and ALEXIS GREIG MACKENZIE, late of Auckland,
Canvasser, Respondent.
To ALEXIS GREIG MACKENZIE, formerly of
Auckland, Canvasser, but now of parts unknown.
TAKE NOTICE that a Citation bearing date the
18th day of November, 1916, has issued out of this
Court citing you to appear and answer the Petition
of ELIZABETH MACKENZIE of Auckland, praying
for a dissolution of her marriage with you on the
grounds that you have deserted her for 5 years and
upwards without just cause. TAKE NOTICE that
by an Order of this point dated the Bth day of
December, 1916, it was ordered that personal ser-
vice on the Respondent of the Petition and Citation
be dispensed with, and that service be effected by
advertising an abstract of the Petition and Citation
to be settled by. the Registrar of this court at Auck-
land twice in the Sydney Bulletin and twice in one-
daily newspaper published in the City of Auckland,
and that an appearance and answer be filed within 90
days after the date of the insertion of the last of
such advertisements and that the trial be at the
First sittings of this Honorable Court in Divorce, to
be held at Auckland after the expiration of 110 days
from the last appearance of such advertisements as
aforesaid. If you wish to enter an appearance you
must within the time above-mentioned attend in
person or by a Solicitor at the Registry of this
Court at Auckland, and there enter an appearance
in a book provided for the purpose. In default of
your so doing the Court will proceed to hear the
said Petition, and you will not be allowed to
address the Court.
Dated at Auckland this 18th day of December,
1916.
(Sgd.) R. G. THOMAS, Registrar.
This abstract is published by Archibald Peak, of
Wyndham Street, Auckland, Solicitor ."or the Peti-
tioner. A copy of the Petition may be inspected at
the office of Messrs. Walker A Peak, Wyndham Street,
Auckland, or at the Auckland Registry of the
Court. Friends of Respondent are requested to for-
ward this- advertisement to him.

MAGNESIA HELPS DYSPEPTICS.
PATENT FOODS AND MEDICINES
. NO LONGER NECESSARY.
Many dyspeptics have now discontinued
the use of expensive patent foods, harmful
drugs, medicines and artificial digestants,
and instead, following the advice often given
in these columns, they just take half a tea-
spoonful of bisurated magnesia in a little
water after meals, with the result that they
not only save money but enjoy much better
health. Those who have once tried bisurated
magnesia never dread the approach of meal-
times, because they know that this wonder-
ful antiicid and food corrective, which can
so readily be obtained from any chemist,
will instantly neutralise acidity and prevent
all possibility of the food fermenting in the
stomach. Try this plan yourself, but mind
you get bisurated magnesia, as other forms
are unsuitable.— Advt.

Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless
recipe will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes A
very old, home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few
nights with a small sponge and you will
soon have the pleasure of seeing the greyness
disappear. This recipe is perfectly harmless,
is neither sticky nor greasy, and has given
perfect satisfaction for many generations to
those in possession of the secret.—Advt. [4
POLITICAL
POINTS
A cable message says that “Mr. Hughes
must go ’ to the so-called Imperial war con-
ference. Australia doesn’t mind. All it
insists upon is that Mr. Hughes does not go
in a representative capacity, unless Parlia-
ment expressly chooses him. And even
then he will not speak for Australia. He is
not an Australian, he is a stranger to Aus-
tralian sentiment, he has mostly been an
enemy of Australian aspirations, and he is
cordially mistrusted now by the only partv
tor which he was ever at all entitled to speak.
Apparently Hughes was thinking the other
day ot calling a secret meeting of Parliament
m order that he might “explain the reasons
which make necessary his immediate presence
in London. This looks like a repetition of
the clumsy joke perpetrated by Pearce a few
is°whni a fl?' , n n hat / h 2 P e °Ple want to know
what the talk at the other end is to be
Si™ Tl wl rV h< - atti tude of the represen-
Au ? traJla is going to be. Also what
it is wot going to be, and why the several-
times I rime Minister whom Australia pays
to represent it in London can’t do all the
talking that is necessary.

Hughes, under the War Precautions Act,
prohibited last week any mention of a cer-
tain matter of great national importance.
The first cable message concerning it was
given out by the British Government. On
ednesday the Censor was making appeals
to the newspapers to pull their final-edition
plates- to pieces for the purpose of taking
out the official message which had been
broadcasted during the day. There is a
limit to_ what the papers will stand, and
that limit is almost reached. It was reached
once before, and Hughes knows what hap-
pened then. Sometning will have to happen
again if this disordered person persists in
misusing the censorship for political pur-
poses.
P.M.G. AVebster explains that during the
past eight months he has saved “a quarter
of a million of last year’s deficit of £G40,000”
in the Post Office. Of course lie has not
done anything of the kind. That £040,000
lias vanished down the sink and emerged
as a full blown p.n. What the Post Office
has done is tq lose less this year, so far, than
it did in the year before. Which is all right
so far as it goes; but on Webster’s own
figures an absolute monopoly of the second-
biggest business in the country is still
making solid losses. In the circumstances
the result is simply something that any busi-
ness man must laugh at.
Fenton, M.H.R., makes the sensible sug-
gestion that, instead of going to England to
talk about a nebulous Imperial union,
Hughes should stay at home and get to work
ou recasting the broken-down Federal Con-
stitution.

Woodrow Wilson wants to leave his Villa
in Mexico for a mansion at The Hague.
If Hughes goes to England he will, of
course, have to explain why Australia
turned down universal service. ' In particu-
lar he might then quote how his colleague,
Jens August Jensen, helped the cause bv
saying:—
There has been a cry for conscription, but who
would suffer if it were adopted? The poor and
middle classes ! '
Then Hughes might add that he is such a
stalwart conscriptionist himself that, on
Jensen’s advice being taken, and conserip
tion being turned down, he rewarded Jens
August by reappointing him Minister for
the Navy.

the dissemination of canards. Having made
their bed they are bound to lie on it.

Dr. Florence, of Cootamundra (N.S.W.),
lately gave Billjim this certificate: —
As an examining officer lie had stripped between
five and six thousand men, and in only three or
tour cases found any traces of venereal disease. As
an old soldier and doctor he could assert that no
country in the world could say the same thing.
Good as far as it goes; but unluckily it
doesn't go far enough. No doubt tlie country
is cleaner than the city; but what Dr.
Florence doesn’t know is how many more
country Bilijims he might have stripped had
no-one been unclean.
Perth Chamber of Commerce, looking at
the matter for the business point of view,
has struck Westralian Liberals dumb by
proposing (1) abolition of State
Governors, (2) reduction in
number of legislators, and (3)
reduction in legislators’ salaries.
The first two suggestions were
once strong planks in Labor’s
platform; and the third isn’t
worth putting in any policy.
Least of all should a. body of
business men, convinced that a
concern is going to the dogs, pro-
pose to stop the rush by cutting
down the managers’ salaries.
Westralia needs not 80 legisla-
tors at an average of a couple of
hundred a year, but at most a
dozen at a couple of thousand.
At present nine-tenths of the
men who run the State get salaries which a
man who runs one of the big mines would
hardly offer to a competent engine-driver.
As for himself, he would expect about 10
times as much.
What nonsense this is of compelling
women going on a trip to Maoriland to take
out passports ! The only results are to give
Red Tape a futile job and add substantially
to a charge for transport that is already too
high. It is only in the brain of some mili-
tary official that the idea of forcing elderly
females to (1) get their photographs taken
and (2) produce the picture at the right
place and pay 10s. for a descriptive docu-
ment, can have been hatched. It is up to
someone else to squelch this conspiracy for
looting old women.
Germany is both war-maker and peace-
maker by Divine right. Says the Koel,-
nische Zeitung :—-
If the Entente refuses peace the German submarines
will be given the word “Go forward.” No American
will prevent us enforcing peace upon an enemv who
will not negotiate.
Two and a half years ago Europe was in-
formed by the Kaiser that awful things
would happen to it as punishment for having
compelled him to go to war. Now it is
warned of the more awful punishment in
store if it doesn’t permit Gott’s anointed to
go to peace. The Kaiser’s word is law
every time. .If he says war, then war it
must be. If it is peace, peace it must be,
even if it be only the peace of the graveyard.
The amended Licensing Act which re-
cently rumbled through' the Vic. Legislature
provides for the registration of barmaids.
Forms have been issued, and, according to
one Melbourne paper, “a full physical
description will be required for purposes of
identification.” However, the persons in
charge of the registration may be satisfied
merely to record the barlady’s peculiarities
of feature, complexion, hair and stature, and
let it go at that.
THE PROOF OF IT.
“Very smart young man dot Solinski!”
“Vy, vot does he do?”
“Dot’s just it. He’s so smart he gets along
veil and does nottings.”
The Admiralty is doing what it should have
done as soon as the war broke out, and no
more deep-sea shipping reports are now to
be read in the newspapers. It was out-
rageous, with the U boats cruising just out-
side the Thames, that lists of departures
should have been published each day: even
the big mail-boat’s sailing hour was given out
in plenty of time for the preparation of a
welcome in the Mediterranean or elsewhere.
No doubt the inconvenience from the suppres-
sion of this news is considerable, but it isn’t
half so inconvenient as being torpedoed.
The “direct-actioners” were not the only
Australians who wore disfranchised when
the conscription plebiscite was taken. The
three or four thousand Australians in the
British Army were deprived of the vote,
and so. according to a Billjim correspondent,
were A.I.F. men undergoing punishment for
military offences, no matter how trivial, and
those awaiting trial.

One prominent • trade unionist who wen,
for _ conscription without getting scalded is
“Mick” Connington, for 15 years secretary
of the Trolly and Draymen’s Union, Sydney,
M. M. Hughes was its president; and when
the union decided to expel Billy, Conning-
ton immediately threw in his resignation.
He was asked to reconsider it, but refused ;
and now he has tacked up a doorplate which
announces that he is in business as a wages
board advocate. He will get all the work
1 e wants- from the unions, as he is known
as the ablest - advocate before the In-
dustrial Court, and has made himself solid
by taking dozens of cases for weak unions
without payment.

TRAWLING HYMN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are better fish in the deep green sea
Than any that come to land ;
And tlie trawling-nets are wide and free,
As a fool may understand.
Oh, the trawling-nets are widely spread,
And the marble slab is cold,
And the place where the silver scales are shed
Could a tale of the deeps unfold.
Far under the waves on a lazy fin
The schnapper and shrimp went by,
Food for the people that zeal might - win,
And the pans were ripe to fry.
The statesman looked and his proud, coy smile
Spread over his ardent face,
For the worker’s breakfast in easy style
Pervaded the deep, wet place.
Ho, bring me paper ! Ho, bring me ink !
There are deeds for a man to do !
And the sea laughed loud to its sandy brink
As he scribbled the lOU,
As he wrote the call for the cash to buy
The trawlers to trawl the deep,
'That the fish might come to the pans to fry
And the world acclaim them cheap.
Cheap fish! Cheap fish! And the trawlers trawled,
And the fish were brought to shore;
And the wares in his shop the statesman bawled
Where the cray' hung red at the door.
The gay sun shone and the good sea rolled
And the bream on a silver fin
Came up to the net and with sea-strides bold
Led thither his kith and kin.
cheap fish ! Cheap fish ! ’Twas the pride of town ;
But the statesman’s brow grew tense
When the cod that was captured for half a crown
Had been purchased for eighteen-pence.
And a mist came down on the splendid scheme,
A mist that was grey and drear,
For the food that was cheap in the statesman's
dream
In the balanced accounts was dear.
Oh, heavily up and down he strode,
The man who had planned the thing.
And Cohen’s fear on his hot heart rode
And tortured it string by string;
And he wrote a minute to bring him cheer,
Or perchance to turn his luck :
But the fish that were cheap were doubly dear
When the men on the trawlers struck.
And somewhere behind he heard a shout,
’Twas a fearsome shout and loud ;
And a boot on a sudden urged him out
f rom his place in the Labor crowd.
With the Labor boot and the Labor bawl
His fishscape was wild and gay,
While he pondered the trawlers that would not trawl
Or that, trawling, refused to pay.
But the round eyes under the deep green sea
Smiled low as they viewed the land ;
And the trawling grounds were wide and free,
As a fool might understand
Oh, the things that a fool may com rehend
The heart of a fool may thrill;
But the kind fates know when the y st has end—
’Tis the public that foots the bill."
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
PARTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two new Labor Leagues with different platforms
are being formed in Sydney. A new Commonwealth
National Party is being formed in Melbourne \
readjustment of parties is looked for to keep the
Government in office during the Prime Minister’s
absence.
Let them all come! New parties by the dozen
Hammer the rusted nails in ancient planks
To build new platforms, sister, aunt or cousin
To other platforms now declined with thanks.
Jones has his caucus meeting to arrange for ;
Smith has declared his boats and bridges ’ burned,
And airs his latest fancies in exchange for
Those that he held when Thompson’s league
adjourned.
O Brien and his strong committee linger
In loud debate of fusion with McGuire;
Spirks braves his party’s wrath and shakes a finger
At Blithers, who has threatened to retire.
The National Abusives are devising
A cry to shake the country to its core,
The while the A.S.S. is organising
By methods strange and never tried before.
Leagues, parties, fusions, splits, associations
- Are plentiful as pubs along the street;
And their relation to the War of Nations
Each leader, states with eloquence and heat.
Let them all come ! New parties by the million
Hammer the rusted nails in platforms new.
There is a Parliament in every skillioh,
xlnd every hash-tower boasts a league or two.
We must get busy lest another baulk us.
Here on two boots / take my solid stand:
I am, my party, platform, league and caucus,
And go alone to serve and save the land!
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

LEMON PUDDING
1 '■
Ingredients.— l /z a lemon, 1 pint water, 2 eggs,
/■2 oz. butter, 1 breakfast-cupful bread crumbs, 1
tablespoonful sugar, 2 tablespoonsful NESTLE’S
CONDENSED MILK.
Method. —Dissolve the milk in the water. Cut
the rind of the lemon very thjn and put it into the
milk, allow to come to the boil, then remove the lemon
rind, and pour milk over bread crumbs. When
slightly cool pour in the beaten egg?; three-parts fill
a buttered pie-dish with the mixture, and add the but-
ter, divided into little bits. Bake in a moderate oven
for 30 minutes or until set. Whisk whites of egg to a
stiff froth and stir in sugar. Take pudding out of oven
and pour the juice of half a lemon over, then put the
whites of eggs roughly on the top and dredge with fine
sugar. Put back in the oven for five minutes.

QUEEN CAKES
Ingredients.—& ozs. flour, 3 oz*. butter, 2 ozs.
candied peel, 1 lemon rind, 2 tablespoonsful
NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK, mixed with ‘/4 pint
of water,3 ozs. sugar, 3 ozs. currants, 2 eggs, 1 table-
spoonful baking powder.
Method.—Sill the flour, add grated lemon rind,
currants, and the peel chopped fine. Cream the
"butter and sugar, and add the eggs and milk
alternately with the dry ingredients. Well grease
some moulds and bake 15 minutes.

SAGO PUDDING
Ingredients. —2 ozs. fine sago, 2 table-
spoonsful NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK,
44 P'nt water, 1 oz. castor sugar, 1 egg.
Method. —Soak the sago in cold water and
drain, then sprinkle into the water and milk
while boiling. Stir until cooked—about 10
minutes. Allow to cool, and stir in the
beaten egg and the sugar. Pour into a
buttered pie-dish. Bake in a moderate oven
for about half an hour. Dish up and serve
hot.
NOTE. — A little grated lemon rind or
nutmeg sprinkled over the pudding before
baking will give it additional flavour.
CUSTARD MOULD
Ingredients. —3 tablespoonsful NESTLE’S
CONDENSED MILK, 44 pint water, oz.
gelatine, 2 eggs, almond essence.
Method. —Heat the milk, dissolve the
gelatine in it and pour on the beaten
yolks of the eggs. Pour into a jar and
place it in a saucepan of boiling water;
then stir till the custard thicken*. When
nearly cold add the whites of the eggs
well beaten, and the essence, and pour
into a mould to set.
hot afternoon tea cakes
Ingredients. —lib. flour, 1 teaspoonful
cream of tartar, l /x teaspoonful bi-carbonatfe
of soda, 2 eggs, 4 ozs. butter, 2 tablespoons-
ful NESTLE’S CONDENSED MILK mixed
with x /x pint water.
Method.- Rub the butter into the flour,
then add other ingredients. Mix into a
light dough with the eggs and milk; cut
into small rounds with a fancy cutter, brush
over with egg, and bake in a very hot oven
for IS minutes.
RASPBERRY CREAM ICE
Ingredients. —J/j oz. ground rice, 4 ozs.
. raspberry jam or raspberry essence, a few
drops of’ carmine, 1 pint water, 3 table-
' spoonsful of NESTLE’S CONDENSED
MILK.
Method. —Dissolve the milk in the water.
Rub the jam through a hair sieve in order to
remove pips. Put the ground rice into a
saucepan with the milk, bring to the boil and
cook for five minutes, stirring all the time.
Add the jam or essence and enough carmine
to colour a pale pink. Allow to get quite
cold ; it is then quite ready for putting into
freezing machine. Keep sides of freezes,
constantly scraped or the mixture will freeze
too quickly and be unpalatable.

One often hears the argument that heat causes
sufficient expansion of the air in a tyre to produce a
blow-out, and many car-owners thus excuse their
habit of running tyres under-inflated. Even experi-
enced garage men sometimes advise that tyres be
run at 15.t0 20 pounds lower pressure in hot weather.
It is admitted, of course, that heat does expand air
and raises the pressure in a tyre, but in its practical
effect the rise in temperature is insufficient to cause
even the slightest danger. To satisfy themselves on
this point the Goodyear Tyre and Rubber Co. re-
cently made an unusually severe test, using a run-
about equipped with 32 x 4 and 815 x 105 millimetre
tyres. A very hot day was selected. The tyres were
pumped up to 801 b. with the car standing in the
cool of the garage. It was driven intermittently for
about an hour, and when not 'running was allowed to
stand in the sun. Then it was taken for a spin at
the rate of up to 40 miles an hour over a variety of
roadways including asphalt, wood blocks and just
common road. On measuring the pressure it had in-
creased. exactly four pounds. As far as the welfare
of the tyre is concerned this difference is really no
difference at all. Tyres are now built with such
high factor of safety that an increase in pressure of
many times this amount would not cause a blow-out
unless already ruined by overloading and under-
inflation. Goodyear Tyres have often been inflated
up to 30'Olb. (in tests of fabric strength) without
any damage to the tyre. The most serious variation
in tyre pressure is caused by leakage at valves,
though rubber is to a small degree porous, and will
allow air' under pressure to leak through it. A certain
amount of leakage through both inner tube and valve
is normal and to be expected. The tube may main-
tain pressure for a considerable "time, but it is never
safe to count on it. It cannot be put too' strongly
that under-inflation, not over-inflation, is the condi-
tion to guard against. The only sure way to get
big mileage is by frequent use of the tyre-pressure
gauge, which is recommended by the Goodyear and
all leading tyre-makers throughout the world of
motordom. —Advt.

KELLERMANN SEASON
SPORTING
NOTIONS
It was 12 to 1 bar one and some 100 to 1
m Sydney Tapersall’s Carrington Stakes on Saturday
an< t the animal barred was Balarang. A week
before, at the A.J.C. meeting, this horse, carrying
Bst. 111 b. in the High-weight Handicap, hurried over
six furlongs before the clock had passed lmin.
J2l sec. On Saturday he had no penalty and breasted
the barrier with 7st. 51b. among 23 others, and a
pretty slick lot they were too, Colugo, St. Rosaline,
Rosedell, La Steppe and Wedding Day being among
the company. The gelding made them look like
goats. After running a furlong he struck the front,
and going on without pressure arrived at the pole
in lmin. 11 3-ssec., the fastest-run six furlongs ever
registered in the Commonwealth, and the hottest
favorite seen at Randwiek for many moons. Bonnie
Plume and Royal Laddie were three lengths astern,
• but it really looked as if the gap was half a mile.
IV hat Balarang would have done to a Newmarket
Handicap if that event had been run last week is a
pity to think about.
About the be'st-pleased man on Randwiek race-
course on Saturday must have been John McDonald,
the Mungie Bundie studmaster. Balarang was bred
at his nine? cr.f! Bonnie Pluftie, who also first saw
the light at Mungie Bundie, was second to the
streak. Mr. McDonald owns Bonnie Plume, and
earlier in the day captured an event—Chelmsford
Handicap—with Lndv isolt. The three nags men-
tioned are not related, i.alaiang being by imported
Challenger from that great mare Lady Wallace.
Lady Isolt is by Derby winner Mountain King, from
another good racer, Isolt < M. L.), and Bonnie Plume
;s by Earlston (imp.) from Bonnie Crest. But
they all have a strain of Musket. The result was
something any breeder might be proud of.
Adrian Knox, chairman of the A.J.C., pur-
sues Costs with a long way more success
than his nags chase their oats bill. On Saturday
Three Star, on weight, recent running and appear-
ance, looked a certainty for the Denman Handicap ;
and Ikev’s offer of 3 to 1 was mentioned in Israel
without enthusiasm. But he went down again, the
despised-Naviro just struggling in ahead of Wood-
wild, who finished with a long, brilliant run. Three
Star ought to be a good horse. Yet with this race
apparently at his mercy, a furlong from home he
commenced to stop. Heaven knows what punters
have lost on him by this time, but it would have
been cheaper for them had they bought the animal
and presented the owner with something more expe-
ditious.
When horses trained by Paddy Nolan for the late
Mr.. Hebden—one of the whitest men the turf has
known—were put under the hammer by order of
the executors last September, Randwiek trainer
Billy Kelso paid 750 guineas for Narivo, a gelding.
This big price was due to Narivo having won a
couple of good races just previously at Randwiek,
and several people wanted to own him as a conse-
quence. They must all have shaken hands with
themselves that Kelso thought more of the neddy
than they did,- for until Saturday Narivo had not won
a race for his present owner, though he has been
a frequent competitor and often carried a heap of
stable confidence and money. On Saturday he was
ridden by apprentice W. Pike, brother of the once-
famoiis Jimmy. James, too, learned how to ride
under Kelso’s guidance.
Having run a first, second and third during the
month the Linacre colt Conacre came to light again
at Randwiek on Saturday and hopped over 4V fur-
longs with Bst. 91b. in 54jsecs., which happens to be
only half--a tick outside the record for the course
established by Wooralc in one of his earliest races.
At the distance Robkin, a winner over four furlongs
at Warwick Farm, looked-like taking the bacon, but
didn’t hang it out. There were 17 starters in this
event, and what a dozen of them were HoiSg there
is more than mortal man can tell. The same Teihark
applies to the couple of two-year-old races at the
A.J.C. meeting last month. There were 22" animals
released each day, and more than half of them had
no more chance of winning than flying. Apparently
the number of owners who think it does a young
horse good to parade their colors in public is grow-
ing.
Baronet Cooper’s imported Sydian has always been
suspected of liking it best when the pace is of his
own making, and what he can make it if he feels
disposed to hurry up was explained at Randwiek in
the Pace Welter. He had 9st.. 91b. aboard, and
jumping into the lead refused to wait for company
on the journey. 1-Ie was out four lengths on his
own and crossed the HnWln lmin. 39jsec-. This was
the unreliable quadruped’s first win in Australia.
* * *
On . New Year’s Day, noticing that
Sydian in the New Year Gift had the best part of a
stone less to carry than he had in the pace-welter,
the public leaped frantically at MacShortodds for
wagers. MacShortodds obliged—and Sydian did the
rest. The Cooper colors shot out from the tapes and
ran with a coupls* of lengths of daylight behind right
to the Leger, where that good but unlucky horse
Royal Laddie moved up, and Sydian, swishing his tail
in angry protest against running on, shut up like a
pub at 6 o’clock. Of all the uncertain brutes that
ever were foaled he is one of the best.
* * * *
It is up to someone to plaster the “Con-
sistency” label on hurdler Yellowboc. In her last three
starts she has had a second and two firsts in that order.
The defeat occurred at last Rosehill gathering when
Rongopai, after clearing the last hurdle alongside
Yellowboc, left her as if she had been anchored.
Yellowboc next went to Warwick Farm and secured
the Jumpers’ Flat Race. Then followed Tattersall’s
Hurdles at Randwiek on Saturday. She annexed
this event in easy style. Rongopai was favorite, but
was never in the hunt. The Maorilander was beaten
further than Abe can kick his hat after a bad day
at Randwiek—which is about a furlong.
*** * *
With the assistance of the imported
Chantemerle, Bananaland took Tatt.’s Cup this year
without much effort. But another imported nag,
Rushford, ran like a mule. Of course, the crowd
flung in on Wallace Isinglass, who never looked like
a winner, and finished third. He only did that on
sufferance, too.
“Mujik”: If the A.J.C. runs a place
machine at Randwick a considerable number of
people will get relief from a system of plunder
which simply yells for the axe. As place betting
is run now the public has no possible chance with
some of the odds merchants. In fields of 15 and 20
horses, with straightout prices ranging up to 30 to
1, the place fielders cramp the odds to a limit of 3
and 4to 1. Their favorite prices are level money,
3 to 2 and 2 to 1 against the rankest outsiders,, and
by preying on women and that large number of men
who punt in dollars they get away with hatsful. The
best price this writer could get the other day against
a 16 to 1 chance for a win was 2 to 1 for a place;
and against a 50 to 1 proposition he was offered
3 to 1. A place tote, with an even cut on 1, 2 and
3, would knock this brand of murder out, and no one
would feel inclined to put a tombstone over the
remains either.
This page hears on more or less reliable
authority that under “proper supervision” the
tote at some of the country race meetings is going
to consist of a blackboard and somebody armed with
a piece of chalk and a few tickets. If this is true
the sacred cause of fair dinkum demands at least
three things—first, that the man at the
be changed every five minutes; next, that' the chalk
be entrusted to a deacon of the local kirk ; finally,
that during the progress of. each race the blackboard
shall be audited by a chartered accountant and
surrounded by every individual on the course, each
of whom shall from the moment the horess enter
the straight until the winner of dividend are
announced keep both eyes on the arithmetic.
* •» * *
“Carfax” : In the days when good-natured
philanthropists carried on business on the Maoriland
racecourses and the public rolled up to have a go
at break-joint or Yankee sweat or something
easy like that, the tote at 2s. 6d. a ticket for the
convenience of the democracy was a regular institu-
tion as an interloping side-iine. The best hand at
tlie business was a very respectable-looking old
gentleman with benevolent whiskers. And a nice
comfortable little stunt he had. Supposing he issued
80 tickets on a race. Well, there was £lO then in
the pool. He appropriated 10s. for his services
and worked out the balance for division among
backers of the winner. If there were seven on the
winner each got £1 75., and the old gent, gave the
odd bob to the poor. That is how it worked —if it
didn’t work some other way. But if by some mis-
WARWICK ARMSTRONG (Victoria),
as he appears to the batsmen.
take the old gent, happened to hurriedly. find that
there were 14 on the winner, when everybody but
himself was yelling with excitement, then it panned
out thus: £lO 17s. 6d. allegedly in the pool;
10s. 9d. for the old gent, (ha, ha!); dividend
13s. 3d., claimed by seven backers. The seven other
backers claimed nothing—and with £5 3s. 6d. in
hdnd Old Age laughed softly up its sleeve and
began to take investments on the next race. It ran
'“for years, and you can take it from me that it was
Jtetter than selling peanuts.
The Bagot Handicap, run at Flemington
every New Year’s Day, keeps green the memory of
the first secretary of the V.R.C. Byron Moore suc-
ceeded Bagot in the job long years ago, when, by the
way, the V.R.C. voted a pension to Bagot’s widow,
which is still an item in the club’s accounts.
* * * *
The veteran owner, J. Wilson, sen., the
grand old man of the Victorian turf, had a birthday
in Christmas week. It was his 88th. Thirty-eight
years ago First Ring carried the old sportsman’s
colors to the fore in the three-miles Champion Race,
which was then run on New Year’s Day. The nag
was successful in the same race two years later. '
* * * A*. „
The V.R.C. stipendiary stewards, uncon-
scious humorists that they are, held an inquiry into
a Paralysing reversal of form at Caulfield on Boxing
Day. Two-year-old Blue Bolt was the animal in
question. His running at Moonee Valley three days
previously was the matter to be explained. At
Moonee Valley the youngster lost ground in the
betting, and cut up badly in the race. At Caulfield
things were quite otherwise. Opening at 3 to 1,
Blue Bolt advanced to even money, and won bv
several lengths. The money talked truth. The
stipes, after hearing witnesses for the defence, could
only say that they were not satisfied, but would do
nothing. They thought the jockey had been a trier
when the colt was badly beaten at Moonee, so their
finding amounted to a suggestion that the horse
was doped rather than roped. Melbourne dailies
feebly remarked that the finding put several people
in an “unpleasant position,” and gave promise of
further inquiry. But the matter seems to have been
dropped. Nothing calculated to improve the turf’s
moral tone has ever resulted from istipendiary
stewardship thus far. The salaried vigilance com-
mittee earns nothing but derision—undeserved or
otherwise.
* * * *
The Port Adelaide Racing' Club is. coming
on so well that it now has a weight-for-age race at
the Christmas Eve meeting. Mrs. Hawker’s Cyklon
being in a galloping humor, it was a soft thing for
him. Bu.t there was plenty of excitement over the
race. It was to be started by a new patent, which
includes the firing- of a gun, and the gup did not
fire. One horse was left, but the rest took the
barrier-release as a start, and ran it out. The
stipendiary stewards decided to let it stand. Then
the second horse failed by a whole pound to draw
the weight, and had to be disqualified. Cyklon
rather surprised his backers by paying 295. for a
sovereign only about 2 to 1 on. Where there is
an evident hot favorite, lots of men decline to lay
odds on, taking the chance of a big dividend on
an outsider, and thus the odds come down. The
totalisator also surprised itself and everybody else
by handling more money, by a good deal, than on
any occasion since the war started. There were some
useful dividends, the best being £35 4s. on Dr
Bennett’s Soultberg—with 30s. left over for each
backer of the second.
London Sporting Times gives an odd,
but very likely sound, reason against having stipen-
diar 3' stewards, in England. It admits that they are
successful in Australia, “where the. right sort of
independent and experienced official has been culti-
vated.” But in conservative England racing legisla-
tion remains in the hands of the aristocrats,” who
would appoint a man from their own ranks—in other
words, a “broken-down swell,” whom the professional
element would never respect, however honest he
might be.
* * * *
Melbourne Cup winner Sasanoff was given
a go with Maoriland’s crack mare Desert Gold in
the Islington Plate w.f.a., one mile, at Auckland last
week, and the lady was a length ahead at the end.
I’.ine, lmin. 38 2-ssec-. There would be more than
. three cheers for brother Lowry in Sydney if be
brought Desert Gold over to argue the point at
Randwick in the Autumn. And it would be a safe
thing to bet that, if he did, and if the mare hit
the front, the yell from Maorilanders present would
blow the grandstand roofs into the eye of the setting
sun.
* * * *
The new taxation is making Tatt.’s a
cheerful institution, and it looks as if before long
the public will be laying odds-on. As the thing
runs now, on a 25,000-ticket ss. sweep ( £5000) the
Australian citizens pay £521 to start with. Then
two separate bites each of £5OO are taken out —
and after £1521 has disappeared the 25,000 get a
chance. If there is a longer gamble on earth than
this there would be no harm in mentioning it for
the public benefit.
* * * *
Bill Squires looked in to see if he could
come back, and half an hour later Bill was knocked
up and knocked out. It happened at Sydney Stadium
on Boxing- Night. Dave Smith was the other party
to the transaction, and the crowd saw all it could
reasonably have expected. Bill was eager, game and
in poor condition. He roughed Smith all over the
ring to start with, landed some good weighty
punches, took frequent digs with the left on the
countenance, and smote the atmosphere many times
with a calm, deliberate wallop. It was a poor,
lumbering sort of scuffle by both men, with the odds
100 to 1 on Bill working himself to a standstill. By
the time the half-distance was reached he was a
worn-out, weary William—and Smith stepping up
assaulted a defenceless chin. That ended the experi-
ment. Under the circs, it was scarcely worth while.
But the cheers were for. Bill at the finish.
After having held the bantam champion-
ship a week, Vince Blackburn, the Balmain (Sydney)
youth, had to resist an attempt by Andy Maguire, of
Victoria, to become possessed thereof. They clashed
at Sydney Stadium on Saturday, and Blackburn had
a horribly narrow escape. Andy passed him some
good hard punches, boxed with courage and skill, and
generally behaved in a disrespectful manner. Black-
burn made the fight at first, but hardly showed up so
well as against Janesse. Arthur Scott gave it a
draw—and that was a fair thing. The probability
seems to be that this bantam championship is going
to cause a lot of excitement, and this page has a
notion that Blackburn has a deal of punching to do
yet before he gets a safe mortgage on the situation.
It looks as if Australia’s old acquaintance
Sam McVea will have to hunt round soon for a
chicken farm or a long-handled shovel to keep the
wolf from the door. Samuel was at Santiago de
Chile in September, and undertook to enter into a
disturbance with one Professor Devere in aid of the
funds of the Asylum of the Guardian Angels. ’Hie
learned professor took what was coming to hint in
the interests of Biff and Benevolence, and so great
was the rush to gaze on Samuel that the poor and
ilie suffering and the widow and the fatherless got
just £ll.
* * * *
“Bad Australian” (from Argentina) : The
recent Yankee pug. invasion of South America has
been a hard frost. Sam Langford, Sam McVea and
one or two more, finding nothing doing, shook the
dust of Buenos Aires off their feet and lit out for
Chile last week in the hope of connecting with a
few shin-plasters to keep thff wolf from the door.
The most notable thing about this great influx of
piebald pug. is that none of it seems anxious to get
after our own Jack Murray and his championship of
S. America.
* * * *
An old Sydney sport passed out last week
in his 73rd year—William Broughton Young. He
was an old Sydneyite, nephew of Joshua Young, one
of the city’s early softsgoodsmen. Chiefly he “was
identified with yachting and Rugby football, being
one of the old Wallaroos in the days of Monty and
Dick Arnold and Henry Brady.
* * # •
Frederick J. Brierley, who died the other
day at Cheltenham (N.S.W.), aged 44, was best
known as a stud-poultry breeder ; but a fortnight
before his death he won the championship cup of the
local bowling club to put alongside the innumerable
poultry prizes on his sideboard. A. B. Shand, K.C.,
is a brother-in-law. »
* * * »
Chief Justice John Madden (Vic.) has
worn on one of his fingers for many years a plain
gold band that was presented to him by his friends
to commemorate a successful tip for Flemington
which he' made after a remarkable dream.
* * * * -
With just a little luck, Norman Dodds,
who died at Hobart last week, would have had a
trip with the last Australian XI. He was the finest
wicketkeeper the Flyspeck produced, and also one of
the best batsmen ; but missing the limelight on the
mainland, dodged the selectors. The only “big”
cricket chance that came his way was a trip to
Maorilaiul with a seratched-up team, and in that he
quite justified himself.
* * * *•
H. Burrows, who put a little sparkle into
Vic. cricket the other day by taking 10 wickets for
seven runs in one innings, was a fine bowler in- tin*
long ago. It looks uncommonly as if the young old-
, chap was picking up his punch again.
F. Baring’s 144 (out of 299) at Mel-
bourne last week, was the highest individual effort
in Victorian “big” cricket this season. Baring was
going great guns in inter-State cricket when the war
interrupted the game. In premiership cricket last
season he piled up 525 at an average of 47.72 (with-
out a “riQt out”). For the other side, Warwick
Armstrong added 125 (out of 282) to his accumulated
thousands.
* # * *
An Albany-Armadale (AY.A.) motor-car
road-record which withstood onslaught for a long
while has been battered by Anderson Adams, driving
a 35-h.p. Overland over the 238 miles in shrs. 25min.
The 44-m.p.h, speed is claimed to be a long-distance
Australian record. Still, all Westralian record bursts
don’t get into “the book.” Friedman’s recent Perth-
Kalgoorlie 365-mile spin in 13hrs. 45min., although
two hours inside the previous best, is not to be
officially reeoghised owing to the Westralian Automo-
bile Club not doing the checking. All that the
Club could do was to hand out the cold honor of
congratulation.
Hydroplane racing is now a regular
feature of the regatta at Milang, on the lake near
the mouth of the Murray. The competition is
limited, but keen. This year Rymill’s 200-h.p.
Tortoise gave McFarlane’s Millawa 17sec. start and
an eight seconds’ beating over a 15-mile course.
Time, 23min. 52sec. These machines take time to
get going, so a single mile, even though over a
straight course and with a flying start, took the
comparatively slow clocking of lmin. 42sec.
* * * *
Victoria knows how bowls should be
rolled. During Christmas week her teams defeated*
South AuS"., in Melbourne, and Northern Tasmania, in
Launceston, winning the rubber on each occasion by
two out of three. Is it the climate or the flatness
of Melbourne or the mildness of the whisky that 's
the cause of the skill of the old ’uns of the Yarra?
Or the prevalence of cricket among the youngsters—
especially the youngsters of 20. years ago?

on active service. But I guess our man; Captain-
chaplain Bossence (R.C.), went a bit further than
most of them when he judged the boxing in the
sports on our troopship. And he .had a bit to do,
too, for there were 400 entries and £7O prize-
money.

“T. 0.1.”: Talking of walks (B.
28/12/’l6), how about that IO'OO miles in IOO'O hours
undertaken and completed by Mrs. Douglass in Mel-
bourne, in or about 1860, at the old Princess’s —or
was it at the Prince of Wales in Lonsdale-street ?
She was the wife of an old theatrical prompter who
had become paralysed. The introducer of this sort
of show was Allan McKean, who, at Victoria Theatre,
Ballarat, on September 19, 1858, commenced the feat
(or whatever you like to call it'), of walking 1000
miles in 1000 hours and finished on Nov. 27 at 10
o'clock, doing the last mile in 22J minutes.
* * * *
“John McLaren” : I met a man who was
doing a pretty good whlk, too. I made his acquaint-
ance during a wet season from the box seat of the
coach that bi-weekly ploughs through red mud
between Einasleigh and the Oaks Rush (N.Q.). He
was a little,' sandy-whiskered man in a nondescript
hat, muddy pants and a torn shirt, and he was
pushing a wheelbarrow overflowing with billycans,
frying-pan, swag, pick and shovel and other pros-
pecting paraphernalia. The coach-driver pulled up
to inquire the state of the road further on, and I
asked the man with the barrow where he was going.
He replied, nonchalantly, that, as his claim on the
Oaks had “duffered,” he was going prospecting
“about 40 mile out this way.” He pointed in an
indefinite direction at the virgin bush. As our
straining horses swung into their collars again I
looked back at the, wiry little figure in the dis-
reputable hat struggling with the handles of his
barrow and forcing’, a way through the heavy mud in
the direction of ‘About 40 mile out this way,” all
alone in an effort "to find a field to compensate for
his worked-out claim on the Oaks, and I heard the.
big, genial driveiY remark forcibly: “S’truth ! He’s
got guts!”

We are still devising new ways of wasting
money. We have film censors nowadays ; but
it isn’t sufficient to have one censor foi> Aus-
tralia. Each State must have its own
official, with the stupid result that a film
which was ruled out promptly in Sydney is
being shown in Brisbane at this moment.
Incidentally, what about a new incidence of
film taxation V At present, a tax of so much
per ticket has to he paid by the manager —
which is to say by his patrons-—no matter
what the nature of the film. This is all
wrong. There are some film shows that the
public ought to be encouraged to attend ; and
rather than tax them it would be better to
take, a cut out of every archbishop’s collec-
tion-plate. If the censor isn’t too busy, let
him classify the goods and let the tax he
graduated accordingly—from genuine indus-
trials free up to £lO a foot for seductions.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant” :—-
History is repeating itself. The Custom House in
Sydney is to be reconstructed on a grand scale, and
the job is already being flocked to by men whom the
bursting of the State's borrowing boom has thrown
on the rocks. The site is at the very door of the
port of Sydney, and the first Customs building was
erected there in 1844 in another time of great stress.
It was built by Governor Gipps, mostly to give work,
and no distinction was made in regard to artisans —
stonemasons, laborers and all the rest of them got
2s. 6d. per day, and no more.
Rupert Inglis, Anglican chaplain in the
8.E.F., who was killed by shell-fire while
helping to bring in wounded at the Somme,
was centre forward in the' Oxford Rugby
football team of 1882. C. G. Wade, of the
Ma State, played three-quarter in the same
team, and both he and Inglis were members
of the English international 15. Inglis, who
married a Miss Gilchrist, of Melbourne, had
been a village rector in Kent for some years.
He was 52.
An Irish-born officer of Australian Light
Horse sizes up his native land after 16
years’ absence: —
The country is sadly different. Beautiful Dublin
is in ruins, and the country is desolate. You can
drive all day and never see a soul except old, old
people. Grey skies, grey houses and grey old people.
The spontaneity of the Irish is gone. The country
reeks with dissension and hatred. War and the gin'll
Feiners, misrule and misunderstanding have pressed
on her with a heavy hand, deepening the old scars of
bygone years and inflicting many new ones that will
last as long. I don’t wish to enter into politics, but
would to God England could find some man of imagi-
nation and sympathy to handle Irish affairs ! Someone
to give a square deal, not as a bone thrown to a
dog, but as an earnest effort to compensate for the
injustice and oppression of the past. Produce a man
like that, and England would have no stronger ally.
But while there is one code of laws for the North
and another for the rest of Ireland, whilst some are
honored for deeds for which other men are shot,
whilst Ireland in peace is treated as a joke and in
rebellion with merciless severity, there can be no
hope. Clouds are gathering now throughout the
country, and I am afraid that, unless they are dis-
persed by the sunshine of understanding, they will
outshadow those of the recent Sinn Fein rebellion.
Poor Ireland!
The cableman continues to corrupt us with
lies. Of the German steamrolling of Rou-
mania says he :
The oil-wells at Ploeschi (which are now in the
enemy’s possession) were destroyed so thoroughly
that it will take a year to put them in working
order.
If the oil wells are “destroyed thoroughly”
or at all they won’t be put in order for 2500
years. But there is nothing to stop the
drilling of new wells, and unless the oil is
seven miles deep it’s no 12 months’ job.
The record in California for well-drilling is
2391 ft. in seven days. In the face of that
along comes this comforting liar with his
tale of 12 months. It isn't half the sentence
lie should have.
A recent local case shows that the
wanderings of a bottled letter can give even
the Postal Department points in divagation
—a feat hitherto deemed impossible. Lance-
Corporal Fagg, of Euroa, who left in Octo-
ber, dropped the usual bottle into the sea.
The message it contained reached his rela-
tives, but to do so it had first to come
ashore, then to be handled in its glass en-
velope by an unsuspecting bottle-oh, and
then to go. still undiscovered, to an aerated;
water factory. Here it was found by the
man who was cleaning the bottle for re-publi-
cation. He handed the note to his mother,
who in turn sent it to its destination, which
it reached on December 5.
having seen his sons for two years,
yVilliam Anthony, of North Melbourne,
joined the 23rd Howitzer Battery, and in
due course found himself in camp at Salis-
bury Plain. At the end of a hard day’s
drill he was musing on the whereabouts of
his younger boy Bill, who had left Aus-
tralia with the Second Field Ambulance.
Suddenly the father’s thoughts were inter-
rupted by “Hello, Dad!” and David stood
before him. The pair obtained week-end
leave and went to London. Anthony senior
arranging to meet Dave there. When he
turned up lie found both Dave and Bill on
the station. Bill had got 10 days’ leave
from France, where he had had a hot time
in Pozieres. The reunion of the soldiers
three made the return of the Prodigal Son
look like a vagrant surrendering to his bail.
Reginald Morgan, who died of wounds in
France the other day, was organist and
choirmaster of an Anglican church in the
Glebe (Sydney) till he enlisted last year.
A few weeks before his death lie had won
the Military Medal for gallantry in the
Somme disturbance.
The history of Armageddon will contain
no incident more humorous than that of the
disabled Zeppelin and its crew that came to
earth in Essex in the middle of the night.
The Huns hurried to the nearest farmhouse,
woke the occupant of his first sleep, and
cried with the voice of the spokesman: “Ve
vos brisoners of var, you understant.” The
descent from frightfulness to funk!
MY MORNING LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You died—you always die,
My love—and yet I do not sigh.
The roaring sun comes upward from the East,
And, as I scent the dew, 1 know, at least,
That dark has gone. And all the low’ring gloom
Is blazoned by the scarlet. poppies’ bloom.
H.S.W.

the, secret of a
good COMPLEXION
By MME. ST. CLAIRE.
A;v i woman nowadays may quickly reju-
venate her complexion at home by a safe and
painless process of gentle absorption. The
days of expensive and dangerous “operations”
are past, and each woman can be her own
beauty doctor. It has been discovered that
ordinary mercolised wax, applied like cold
i-V" j* 111 ' will cause the thin veil of
lifeless, sallow or blotchy cuticle to gradually
and gently flake off in invisible, flour-like
particles, revealing the fresh, vigorous and
beautiful young skin underneath. Mercolised
vvax absorbs only the unhealthy and un-
sightly outer film, and does not affect the
healthy tissue at all.
Ihe process defies detection, and unveils
a lasting and beautiful complexion, which is,
of course, a perfectly natural one. For this
purpose thousands of women are now using
mercolised wax, which may be obtained at
any chemist s. Naturally the process also
removes all such surface blemishes as
freckles, blotches, pimples, tan, liver spots,
etc. It makes the face look years younger
and prettier, and renders the use of face
creams unnecessary.
Another valuable piece of information,
which many women will appreciate, concerns
the success of the new treatment for super-
fluous hair. A remedy for permanently erad-
icating this ugly form of facial disfigurement
no w seems to have been found. It is simpli-
city itself. About one ounce of ordinary pure
powdered pheminol, which can be obtained
at any chemist’s, is all that is needed to put
one in the way of completely ridding oneself
of superfluous hair. When applied to the
obnoxious growth this causes it to immedi-
ately disappear, and the continuance of the
recommended treatment is said to finally
destroy the hair roots, so that there fill be
no renewal of the trnwHi ret

‘Carfax” hands in a confession:—
the suburban air was full of mosquitoes and
mud blaring- noises made by euphoniums, trombones
and cornets. It was the eve of Christmas Day, and
I lay on a bed of torture, eaten by buzzing animals
and maddened by musicians. Heaven only knows
how many bands were at large, but at one' moment
the strains of “The Rosary” were wafted from 17
places. At midnight a band arrived opposite the
iront gate and projected six carols into the pre-
mises. They woke the kids, who immediately dived
tor their stockings, produced 34 buzzers, Noah’s Arks
and squeaking dolls, and started a rival riot. After
strenuous exertion the uproar inside was quelled
with lemonade and a razor-strop, and outside by
2s - 6d - Two _ hol, rs later another band drifted to
close range with a 15-h.p.. edition of “Hark, Hark
the Shark,” or something. I was sane, cold and cal-
culating now. Throwing wide the dooi's I invited
the wretches to come within and drink. ' They
came, each with his awful implement under his
arm and on his dial the guilty look of one who has
assassinated a goddess. And as they entered the
door 1, the son of a race of Puritans, executed sum-
mary jurisdiction under the War Precautions Act
with a tomahawk. Then when the last had fallen
in his tracks.l dragged the corpses outside into the
yard, set ’em alight, gave the kids the musical in-
struments and went back to bed with blood on my
hands, the smell of frying musician in my nostrils,
and my soul filled with the sense of a social duty
well performed. It is only by resort to direct action
that these prowling noise-mongers and outragers of
the night can be got rid of. They are utterly
irrelevant, and usdless. What good citizen wants
people who sneak about in the gloom with 'trom-
bones? No one has missed that band. The news-
papers have not heard of it. The John-Hops know
nothing of what happened. But they were assassi-
nated and cremated right enough—and next time the
job will be done with a machine-gun in the open
air.

The Bank of North Queensland
Limited.
IyTOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that an
JJM Extraordinary General Meeting of The
Bank of North Queensland Limited will be
held at the registered office of the Bank, 308
Queen Street, Brisbane, on Tuesday, the 9th
day of January, 1917, at 12 o’clock noon,
when the subjoined resolutions will be pro-
posed.
Should the said resolutions be passed by
the required majority, they will be submitted
for confirmation as special resolutions to a
Second Extraordinary Meeting to be subse-
quently convened.
(i.) That it is expedient to effect an amalgamation
of this Company with The Royal Bank of Queensland
Limited.
(ii.) That with a view to the amalgamation of
this Company with The Royal Bana ‘of Queensland
Limited, this Company be wound up voluntarily.
(iii.) That John Mackenzie Lees of Brisbane be
md he is hereby appointed liquidator for the purpose
of such winding up.
(iv.) For the purposes of facilitating the carrying
out of such amalgamation the Directors of this 'Com-
pany, or a majority of them, may, and they are
hereby authorised, if necessary, to consent to the
registration of a new company to be named “The
Bank of Queensland Limited” with a Memorandum
and Articles of Association which have already been
prepared with the privity and approval of the Direc-
tors of this Company.
(v.) That the draft agreement expressed to be
made between The Royal Bank of Queensland Limited
and the liquidators thereof of the first part, this
Company and the liquidator thereof of the second
part, and The Bank of Queensland Limited of the
third part, submitted to this meeting, be and the
same is hereby approved, and that the liquidator of
this Company as aforesaid be, and he is hereby
authorised, pursuant to Action 151 of “The Com-
panies Act 1863,” to enter into an agreement on
behalf of this Company in terms of the said draft,
which contains, inter alia, a provision for paying
under the circumstances mentioned therein, the sums
and allowances for life specified therein, to the pre-
sent General Manager of The Royal Bank of Queens-
land Limited, and to the present General Manager of
The Bank of North Queensland Limited, with such, if
any, modifications as the said liquidators may think
expedient, and to carry the same into effect.
Dated this twenty-eighth day of December
1916.
J. MACKENZIE LEES,
General Manager.

IN
BANANA LAND
Premier Ryan has been congratulated by
the Brisbane Industrial Council because he
stood out at the Premiers’ Conference against
any tampering with the jury system in deal-
ing with the I.W.W. The same council a
few hours before demanded the release of the
I.W.W.’s who were convicted by a Sydney
jury. Now, which way will you have it?
“Cynicus”: Talking of Jack Hamilton
winning votes by stoush brings to mind
Arnold Wienholt, who represented Fassifern
a few years ago. The wealthy Wienholt was
notorious for his ability to use his dooks and
his love for old clothes. Charlie Jenkinson
on one occasion was fighting him for a seat,
and had secured the only hall in Boonah for
the night before the election. Wienholt
promptly circulated the glad tidings that on
that night he would box all and sundry
behind one of the local pubs. Jenkinson
had no meeting and was beaten at the polls.
“Dead Finish”: The Ross River meat-
works have an electric dog for persuading the
hornies up the race. Whenever a beast shows
an inclination to hang back he is given a
touch on the rump with the dog. He imme-
diately remembers he is needed further
ahead, and never comes back to see what it
was that hurt his feelings.
*‘Mr. Ryan intimated that a sum of
£378,000 was being provided in London on
December 23 by the Commonwealth for the
State' to pay interest.” Unless that
£378,000 is at once repaid from revenue it
will amount to this —that Queensland will
have borrowed money to pay its interest bill.
And that will mean, not only that the Kid
will presently have to pay for partly-worn-
out works, but also for some of the old man’s
railway travelling, etc. For when interest
is made up out of borrowings, the old man
really pays lid. when he ought to be paying
a shilling for a ticket, the other penny being
charged up to the next generation. One
way and another the next generation seems
in for a busy time.
Queensland, that home of tropical degen-
eracy, still makes a healthy showing in the
population business. Statistician MacLeod
reports that the birth-rate for 1915 was 29.35
per 1000, the highest of any Australian State,
while her illegitimacy rate was the lowest in
comparison with the legitimacy figures. Bris-
bane’s population is now about 161,000, with
some 3600 more women than men. MacLeod
suggests, in view of the probable falling-off
in marriages in the near future, that Banana-

cold eye on matrimony.
Private Herbert Ogden, away over in
France, appealed, through the Courier, to
some kind Queenslanders to cheer his loneli-
ness by writing. At latest the suppliant had
received a thousand letters, six bags of papers
and magazines and a big number of parcels.
Out of three mailbags for the unit two were
Ogden’s on a recent occasion.
“Eta”: Bill Hamilton will probably get
the job of President of the Council in suc-
cession to Arthur Morgan, deceased. Bill
is rep. of the Government in the House, and
in his spare moments looks after the Mines
Department. To hear him talk of “those
old blokes” in the Council, one would think
he was 10 or 15 instead of just about 60.
He has been a little of everything in his
time, and 30 years ago was ringer of more
than one shed in the West. He has also
done a bit of honorable gaol, having been
mixed up in the ’9l trouble.
Brothers from the Rhine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A recent proclamation issued by the German
authorities in Brussels advised the cultivation of
kindly relations between the Belgians and the occu-
pying Teutons.”
Don’d get black und snarly mit der vays of Karl
und Fritz;
Kind dey are im Herzen, if a leedle dull of vits;
Yot is dis dey’re telling of dose outrage oop und
down,
Hurting Fraus und Kinder in der grazy leedle town?
Schwein?
Nein!
Not a single sign!
Ve are yoost your Briider who are goming from der
Rhein.
You shall have a Konig from der Kaiser in Berlin,
You shall live on sauerkraut if you only drop your
din;
Learn to know der Deutscher you vill learn to love
him, too —
Love him for his Kultur und der lager he can brew.
Schwein ?
Nein!
Dot is how ve dine!
Gome und hug your Briider who are goming from
der Rhein.
Yot der Teufel matters it if Kirchen get ablaze?
Dot is yoost der Kriegsbrauch mit its old und funny
vays;
Cloth Hall up at Yyrcs is a ruin, as you say —•
Yhy der Teufel did der place get in der bloomin’
vay ?
Schwein?
Nein !
Gome und get in line,
Learn to love your Briider who are gomii)g from der
Rhein.
Ofer dere in Poland dings are vorkin’ like a scharm,
Poles are toiling for us hard on efery busy farm ;
Yhen ein Deutcher passes you should see dem all
salute,
Efery von respect der Volk dot know der vay to
’ shoot.
Schwein ?
Nein !
Fill der cup mit wein,
Toast your loving Briider who are goming from der
Rhein.
Drei years ist es nearly now since ve begin der var ;
Ve haf taught der vorld a lot it never knew before ;
Look at all der Frightfulness you see on efery hand —
Dot is how ve pave der vay for Deutschthum in der
land.
Schwein ?
Nein!
Soon you vill combine,
Gif your hands und Herzen to your Briider from
the Rhein.
England vill gome into line und drop der old
blockade;
Russland in der mortar by der Kaiser shall be
brayed ;
Belgians, aber, are a race der Yarmans love und
prize,
Stop your Flemish growling und be freundlich now
und vise.
Schwein ?
Nein!
Beople superfine
Full of tender feeling are your Briider from der
Rhein! ,
