F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 30, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
His Ex. and Lady Goold-Adams enter-
tained about 200 returned soldiers at the
Exhibition grounds this afternoon. The
Bills and the Jims went in for tugs-of-war
and other mild pastimes, and his Ex. handed
around prizes out of his own pocket.
AVhen the New Year settles into its stride
their Exes, are off to the bushlands. They
are going to camp under canvas somewhere
in the Warwick district, and while the billy
boils and the missus peels the spuds the
Governor will swap tobacco with the vagrant
sundowner.
Major Pendlebury, our popular D.A.A.G.,
has been moved on to Adelaide, where he
will occupy a similar position. He and his
wife will be missed in Bananaland. Mrs.
Pendlebury has given a helning hand or two
to pretty well all movements for the benefit
of soldiers.
Several of our notorious tabbies are trem-
bling in their slippers. An interesting item
of news concerning Mrs. X. Y. Blank was
prattled over the teacups as referring to
Mrs. Y. X. Blank, a recent bride. Charac-
ters were smudged and all was very satisfac-
tory until Mr. X. Y. Blank spoke harshly of
libel actions, and Mrs. Y. X. Blank did
ditto. Then it was learnt that there were
two Blank families, strangers to each other.
The rush to apologise and save further
trouble is seething.
Mr. and Mrs. Julian Mackay will arrive,
in Brisbane early in the New Year. She
used to be Miss Mollie Ferrier, one of Bris-
bane’s prettiest daughters, and for a time has
been nursing in a military hospital in Eng-
land.
Young Cupid continues to mark up his
own private casualty lists. Miss C. Young,
daughter of the George Youngs, of Clayfield,
has promised to wait for Driver C. E.
Longwill, of the Artillery, and son of the
Longwills of Albion, until the war is over.
Then —but steady!
Another promise binds Jim Scrymgeour,
of Callandoon station, who also is in khaki,
and pretty Marjorie Brown, of Toowoomba.
The Imperial Service Club, as usual,
proved a “home from home” on Christmas
Day for the soldiers in camp. To-morrow
another party is to be held at the Club, which
has been the most popular institution for
soldiers in town since the war began.
Miss A. Thorpe, 8.A., is the new town
clerk of Windsor. The B.A. was won at
Melbourne University. Nine years ago she
was made assistant town clerk of Windsor,
and she has been in charge since the deatli
of Mr. Ilenzel eight months ago. She is the
first woman in Queensland to hold such a
position.
Dr. Paul Loubet, who was badly wounded
at Mons, has bought Dr. L. M. McKillop’s
practice at Blackall. Dr. McKillop—an old
Burwood (Sydney) boy—is now in charge
of the military hospital at Enoggera with
the rank of Major. Blackall residents will
miss him and his wife, but the A.I.F. needs
him. Billzac has first claim these days.
Youi%. affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“D.F.”: Biljemima is just as resourceful
as her brother. One damsel in a Northern
town was sorely troubled by her otherwise
impeccable parents going on a periodical jag.
In the midst of the convivialities the female
parent would get into the streets in a cos-
tume as scanty as Eve’s on her honeymoon..
When entreaties were of no avail the
daughter would heat a flatiron and apply it
to the soles of ma’s feet. Ma was no fire-
walker, and the blisters stopped her perambu-
lations. P.S. —The idea is not patented.
Anyone can try it.
W. M. Lee-Bryce, Thursday Island’s Resi-
dent and P.M. and Torres Strait Protector
of Aborigines, passed out the other day.
leaving a fine record for good and wise
administration of the Aboriginal Act in the
Strait. For the five years of his regime, at
Thursday Island the vital statistics of the
discolored population of the islands show up
remarkably well. Births and deaths per
1000 for 1915 were 46.47 and 13.73
respectively, as against 38.46 and 24.10 for
1912. ■ And this despite antagonism shown
by departmental officials and others. His
whole big heart was in the endeavor to save
the Strait Islanders from the fate of the
mainlanders, and his success will be a stimu-
lus to those who follow.
Munro Hull, who has failed to get the
Queensland Dept, of Stock to give his tick
specific a thorough trial, is again raising his
voice and challenging officialdom. Hull
declares that his cattle are immune, while his
neighbors suffer regular losses from the
scourge. Surely that claim offers sufficient
inducement for the Dept, to clamber out of
its red-tape for a few minutes and investigate
the evidence.

“Vigilans”: Re German mission stations
in Australia. There are several scattered
about the continent. One of the Finke in
Central Australia spread a good deal of
German sentiment thereabouts. When I
was in West Kimberley a few years ago
there was an aboriginal mission station at
Beagle Bay subsidised by the Government.
German was the only “white” language
spoken there, though many of the natives jab-
bered a patois —G e r man -c;urn-Mahi y - c m i n -
Japanese-ci/m-English. Beagle Bay is one
of the most extraordinary places in Aus-
tralia. It has the strangest mixture of all
the northern coastal districts. At the mis-
sion station the children represent a con-
glomeration which even Thursday Island
cannot equal. Of course the pearlers and
trepangers are responsible. The children
are yarded up at the mission station, eight
miles from the sea. It is a beautiful patch
in the wilderness —a real oasis in a desert.
Well watered by springs, it grows fruits,
vegetables, maize and other cereals, includ-
ing rice. I have often wondered since how
these German Trappists spied out this fei-
tile spot. I-stayed there as their guest for
a month and marvelled at their industry.
“Mujik” wants rivers of ink to flow: —
All the world is scoffing at Wilson’s Notes, but as
the world always scoffs in the wrong place it would
be a fair thing to point out that Note-writing offers
the only method of securing relief from the burden
of armaments. Just think for a moment what the
world has lost by the current disturbance being-
carried on with bayonets instead of stationery—of
the excitement and opportunity for fine healthy criti-
cism lost through the sword being thought mightier
than the pen.
It is quite absurd for public meetings to throw
tomatoes at me when I point this out and try to
argue that wars could be still carried on and con-
ducted in a much more cleanly way if a resolution
was passed to give the pen a chance. Wilhelm could
still have gone mad —could have ravaged Belgium
and Northern France and torpedoed the Lusitania and
dropped Zeppelins on England to be examined by
the local constable. Also Australians could have
gone to Egypt and Gallipoli and the Somme and the
Russian to various other places. It would all have
been as easy as falling into sheep-dip. How much
better it would have been if William had suddenly writ-
ten that he was on the verge of Belgium and 6n succeed-
ing days had dashed off notes to remark that his army
was at Aerschot and Louvain and Brussels. At once
he would have received cold official documents stating
that the Belgians were retreating undismayed; that
J. Bull was coming on and had an army going to
Mons, and the Russian scouts were in the Carpathians
and in Poland. William would thereupon have
telegraphed -that the British were moving strategically
to the rear from Mons and Von Ivluck was encircling
Paris and preparing to attack Antwerp. At
this stage, after conferring with Kitchener and the
Daily Mail, Joffre would have written to Berlin
explaining the Marne, and Germany would say:
“Well, I defeat the Russians at Thannenburg. What
are you going to do about that?”
And so on and so forth, right down to the combing
of Percy out of a Government job in England, and
Australia struggling with “Yes-No,” and the Federal
Parliament hiding, and Hughes tearing up upas trees.
And as the list of casualities mounted up at Berlin
the German would have seen the hopelessness of his
position and tried to settle the matter; but Aus-
tralia would firmly say: “Go on to the bitter end,
I’ve 17 recruits,” arid the papers would proclaim vic-
tories arid high prices of food and tell of Von
Falkenhayn getting the sack and the Crown Prince
beans and —why, the mental picture of horror and
the war notes in the newspapers would’ have in seven
years brought the conflict to a conclusion, with three
cheers for the winners and three more for the losers
and frantic applause for the Note as a weapon of
defence against aggression! But now how many
cheers are there goirg to be on an earth soaked by
tears and blood, choked w-ith misery and woe? Every
time give me the Note.
The Kaiser in his offer of peace professed
■ himself willing to sign many documents,
among others an agreement limiting arma-
ments :
lie tore the documents to bits,
Indulging his destructive capers;
And now, grow'n weary of the “scraps,”
He’s willing to restore the papers.
Killed in action at the Somme, S. F.
Hutton, aged 32. Hutton was an Anglican
priest, son of an English rector, who, failing
to get a chaplaincy, enlisted as private in
the Royal Fusiliers with his bishop’s sanc-
tion. In the intervals of doing his bit in
the trenches he held unofficial services for
his mates, as the thousands of French priests
among the “poilus” are doing. He was the
grandson of old-time Sydney merchant
Henry Mort. An uncle, 11. Wallace Mort,
is a retired Anglican canon in Sydney.
Robert Semple, who has just been
handed a year’s gaol in Maoriland, was
over in Australia a few weeks ago ex-
horting the coal-miners to keep on strik-
ing. Then he ivent back to tell the com-
munity what a fine thing it was for Labor
to make a kind of hell on earth. Semple
is a queer mixture,, never thoroughly happy
unless all hands have knocked off work and
he is wildly urging them to cast their chains
away for evermore. He is about as construc-
tive as an enraged elephant, and in Maori-
land is regarded by Fat as the emissary
of the Devil. After being before the Beaks
several times he was nabbed for “sedition”
—which has been stretched till it is now
a very wide net in Maoriland.
CAPITAL PUNISHMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Melbourne missionary, who has been carrying
the glad tidings of great joy to numerous small
islands in the vicinity of the New Hebrides, says that
on two or three of these islands the common practice
is to eat. the criminal. “A practice I do not attempt
to excuse,” he adds.
The system's excellent, nor can
We see the need to frame excuses.
’Twcre hard to find a better plan
Of punishing the wicked man,
And putting him to better uses.
The method, there is no denying,
Than ours is far more satisfying.
For crimes against the people, or
For treasonable matter,
The crim’nal to the common store
Is passed, and in a week or more
Gomes up for judgment—on a platter !
There people on the felon batten.
Here felons on the people fatten.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The Inky Way.
: pou'pland Harding, of the Wel-
lington (M.L.) Post, dead. His was a
great spirit in a too-small body. In the
Maori wars he carried a musket for that
exacting and thankless father of ours. Then,
a printer for a living; but he made printing
an art where art was at the tail of ■ the
procession. I knew him best when I was
a cub on the Post, where he was reader and
I was his copy-holder. I remember him as
I remember O. Henry’s woman who wished
that she was as other women, having a
husband who would wallop her occasionally.
Harding used to listen to my asinine
opinions not only with tolerance but as
if he were interested. And I the while
wished that he would give me a solid
hiding. Yet, if Goupland Harding' had
given me a solid hiding once a day, I
would not have so benefited as I have from
the recollection of his gentle nature.
Arthur Morgan, Lieut.-Gov. of Queens-
land, who passed over lately, was already
proprietor of the Warwick (Q.) Argus
when “Billy” Conley, the present general
manager of the S. M. Herald, was a boy
“on the machine” in the office. The Argus
job was no sinecure, either then or at any
other time, and the youthful devils who
found their way there set up type, turned
the hand press, published, delivered, and
then got a day off to do reporting and collect
the accounts —all for about 10s. a week.
* * * ■ *
“Pen”: Mr. Burgoyne (B. 7/12/T6) and
I agree on the main facts concerning the
Perth Press Club (so called). I said that
the original A.J.A. Club members left out
of the constitution a clause that would have
kept the control in their own hands, and
were consequently flooded out. Mr. Burgoyne
says that every member of the committee is
“connected with newspaper work,” but only
“four are members of the A.J.A.” Precisely
my argument. Working pressmen in Perth
are, or were until recently, all members of
the A.J.A. A committee of which less than
a third are working pressmen cannot be
said to be controlled by pressmen. Everyone
knows, too, that the term “connected with
newspaper work” may include anyone, from
the boy who throws the rag over the fence
in the morning to “Pro Bono Publico.” It
is also a fact that the A.J.A. may hardly be
mentioned inside the Club, and Association
meetings cannot be held there.

With the help of the priceless comp.
Sydney Stock and Station Journal has
discovered the cause of rust in wheat: —
The reports generally from Riverina were to the
effect that rust was spreading from the flag to the
stems, but the parts of the country, and were all
traced to infected shaving-brushes, the bristles of
which were largely composed of mixed hair of
Chinese origin, chiefly goat, horse, pig and human
hair.

“J.F.I-I.” (London) : Lord Nortlicliffe’s
latest full-page boost is over the patriarch of
his big journalistic family—the Weekly Des-
patch, a Sunday paper that has just scored
its 6000th number. The first issue is dated
September 27, 1801, and the only copy of it
known to exist hangs in a glass frame on
the wall behind Northcliffe’s chair in Car-
melite House: the earliest copy preserved in
the British Museum is dated 1818. The
original price was eightpenee, afterwards it
was cut to sixpence, and when the absurd
newspaper-stamp duties were repealed it
came down (1870) to a penny, and lias been
there ever since. Northcliffe now reprints
the famous letter addressed by Charles
Dickens to the Weekly Despatch, in 1849,
describing the “ghoulish holiday” and the
horrible scenes of “brutal mirth and callous-
ness” he witnessed during an all-night vigil
waiting for an "execution in London. This
letter was largely instrumental in abolishing
public hangings. Thq old paper has a lot
of other interesting Inky Way history in its
files. For instance, G. R. Sims earned his
first guinea from the Despatch ; and it was
in a cradle in the office that the Referee
was first put to bed.
BELOW PAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was young and greener far
I’d always condescend
To scribble an attractive par.
To gratify a friend.
I wrote of Mrs. Weeler’s ball,
The finest function of them all : ,
Of Mr. Thompson’s escapade
I straight a two-inch item made.
And so it came about in time,
Did any fellow plan
To star his name in prose or rhyme
I was declared the man
Who would be glad to serve his turn.
And give the universe to learn
How once his wit convulsed a bar,
Or why he bought a motor-car.
For years the seekers buzzed around ;
I heard the postman’s wrongs,
Of go'oseberries that weighed a pound,
And Miss Letitia’s songs ;
And oft their doings to recite
The wretches kept me up at night.
But all would say on seeing me:
“A splendid writer, Billy T!”
But now I write no pars, for these,
I dodge the tales of Brown ;
The dates of Mrs. Smithy’s teas
I never more set-down.
I can no precious moments spare
From lofty rhyme and story rare,
And neighbors say as by I trot :
“Who reads, that fellow’s awful rot?”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

The King’s Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WATCH the night-lamp flicker on
the velvet-shrouded wall,
Or listen to the water-drops that tinkle
as they fall
When all the world t is dreaming and the
stars are growing* small.
Mary, pray for me!
When he that draws his even breath so near
is deep asleep,
Oh, then I lie and stare and think—l am too
sad to weep—-
Till on the figured tapestry the creatures
seem to creep.
Mary, pray for me!
Or rise and yearn above his face, so beauti-
ful to me,
My anguished love, a steady star, made bright
with tears. But she!—
Oh, all my life is a grey shore and a wide
moaning sea.
Mary, pray for me!
I cannot sob to the cool floor when my wrung
heart is torn ;
No, I must sweep among the court in silk
like painted morn—
They whisper, and look after me with pity
worse than scorn.
Mary, pray for me !
And when the silver trumpets blare, so sweet,
at some state ball,
To have him bend above my hand —his queen
-—before them all
When my whole being, reaching out to him,
is one wild call!
Mary, pray for me !
I see her pass from man to man, her bosom
half laid bare,
The shim’ring star he always wore a glory
in her hair,
Or watch him tend her covertly as down
the stately stair
(Mary, pray for me!)
She rustles—from her arching foot to her
rouged face a jade,
Of God and man and devil and the scandal
unafraid !
How should she prize his dearest head upon
her bosom laid ?
Mary, pray for me!
If there had come a child like him ! I used
to dream of one.
It was a dream. It gave me strength, and
she has borne him none.
He might have loved if I had been the
mother of his son.
Mary, pray- for me.
N.S.W.
DULCIE DEAMER.
How “The Keyhole” Fell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I ASCRIBE my ruin, and that of my
paper, to Edward McSnatch. He was
one of those large, profusely-whiskered,
impressive characters who have a knack of
involving, with the best of intentions, then'
own affairs and tho.se of all with whom they
come in contact. A more earnest fellow
never breathed. But in proportion as lie
was upright and enthusiastic, he was a peril
to himself and to others.
I was editor-proprietor of The Keyhole
when I met him first. The policy of The
Keyhole was to expose the machinations of
Rome and of the High Church secL. n of
Anglicanism. We featured escaped nuns, ex-
priests, ritualistic parsons who had taken
to drink, and so on ; and when there was
nothing doing in this connection, we wrote
up one of the Borgias or Torquemada or
someone of that sort. It made stirring read-
ing, and went well with the subscribers,
many of whom got additional excitement
from the delusion that the parties were still
alive and in pursuit of their nefarious prac-
tices. An up-country admirer wrote once
anc| asked by what authority, human or
divine, a man like Auto da Fe was allowed
to remain alive and at large, and he urged
us to 'call upon the godless Romanist Gov-
ernment of Spain to deal with him. Others
inquired angrily what steps Britain pro-
posed to take in regard to Alva and Louis
XI., and whether Papist influences were at
work in I lowning-street itself. Such com-
munications, though misguided, pleased me
as_ indicating that the paper was getting a
grip on its public, and week by week we
gained fresh subscribers, did bigger adver-
tising business and prospered increasingly
in other respects.
And then McSnatch arrived. He bore
high references from Westralia, where he
had been a lay preacher, a captain in the
Salvation Army, check-taker at a swim-
ming bath, tram-conductor, revivalist, secre-
tary to a political candidate who subse-
quently forfeited his deposit and was pro-
ceeded against for a number of offences
under the Electoral Act, an object-lesson in
the merits of a hair-restorer, and bi-weekly
visiting gardener to a convent. He also had
references from Tasmania and Queensland
and elsewhere. They went to show that
he had led a various and troubled life, but
that through it all he had preserved a spirit
of Christian fortitude and a desire to do
better for himself and for everyone around
him. He was, perhaps, 50 years of age. He
ascribed his abnormal hairiness (his face was
uot so much a face as an appurtenance to a
tangled forest of whiskers) to a lifelong
abstention from alcoholic stimulant of any
sort. He had been converted, recently, from
the Plymouth Brethren sect to Primitive
Methodism, after a brief term as an Eddy-
ite. He was born a Presbyterian.
A number of considerations persuaded me to
offer him the editorship of our “Aunt Martha”
column. I was finding it a tax upon myself
when undertaken in conjunction with the
leader-writing and the poultry 'Section. The
growing business of the paper called for an
extra hand, in any case. McSnatch was
transparently honest. I argued that his
wanderings must have given him valuable
experience. Also his job at tbe convent
would provide the office with a certain
amount of badly-needed inside information
on the subject of these institutions.
He proved to be a tiger for work. He
would arrive at 8.30, cut out of the U.S. files
accounts of the misrortunes that had hap-
pened to Romanist priests, Anglican vicars
and curates, Armenian bishops, monks of
the Greek and Russian churches, and so on,
and leave them, one on top of the other,
for me. In the event of a shortage of
clerical scandals, he would get down the
Encyclopaedia and turn up undesirables of
the Romanist persuasion hitherto almost
unknown. It was in this way that I came
to do my expose of neofric the Bloody (a
thegn of Harold the Ganger, the only Norse
chieftain who attenued the peace of Clair-
sur-Epte circa 912), and several other tell-
ing things of the sort.
The “Aunt Martha” column was a trifle
to one of his tireless energy. It was made
up of letters from children to a supposi-
titious relative, such as the following:
Dear Aunt Martha,— l live at Warrangoola with
my ma and pa. We have a dog named Harry, with
a beautiful long tail and a wall eye. I love Kim
dearly. I have a little playmate named Willie
Smith. He says when he is big enough he will go
and fight the Germans. Is he not brave? No more
at present, from your loving Mary Jones (aged
eight years and two months). •
Underneath, McSnatch would "write :
I was delighted to get your cleVer letter, Mary.
What a pleasant home you must have with your pa
and ma and the dog Harry. Certainly Wiliie is a
brave little fellow to want to fight the horrid
Germans, and if the war only goes on long enough
his wish may be gratified. Write again soon to your
affectionate old Aunt Martha.
As time went on I grew to depend more
and more upon McSnatch. He would write
with equal fluency on the Athanasian creed,
the idiosyncrasies of Muscovy ducks, and
the feeble quality of Newman’s mind as evi-
denced in his “Apologia.” Into the raids*
of all this peace came, suddenly, discord ir
the form of a letter from Messrs. Pergrumm
and Hizditche, solicitors, Nangkor, Vic. 11
ran:
Dear Sir, — Re the cruel and scandalous libel c i
our client, Archdeacon Harold Dusty, in your issi e
of Thursday last. We are instructed to demand a
full and immediate apology, failing which we wou d
be glad to know the name of a solicitor who wi 11
accept service of process on your behalf. —Yours,
etc., Pergrumm & Hizditche.
A hurried search revealed the following
in the “Aunt Martha” section :—■
My Dear Auntie, —I live in Nangkor, and I would
like to be one of your little nephews. I am the
most religious boy in the town. All my friends are
religious, too, or I would not know them, as I have
been taught that people are judged by their friends.
Almost every afternoon I go to the” vestry of our
church and meet my little friend Betsy Hobsmail.
We sometimes meet there in the evenings, too, and
have long chats about the good work we are doing
for the world. Betsy’s relations do not know of our
meetings. We think it such fun to have a Mystery.
When we cannot have the vestry to ourselves we go
for long walks in the bush. I hope I shall write to
you many times- again.—Your dear little nephew,
Harold Dusty.
Underneath McSnatch had written :—•
A very nice little letter, Harold, but I am grieved
that you should deceive Betsy’s relations even to
enjoy what you call a Mystery. Remember, Harold,
that no good comes of deception, and that Mr. Hobs-
mail, or whoever is responsible for Betsy’s welfare,
would be justifiably annoyed if he found out. I
trust, when next I hear from you, that I shall learn
that you have made a clean breast of it like the
little man I know you are. —Your loving Aunt
Martha.
Inquiries in Nangkor quickly indicated to
us that the hand of malice had been at
work. Archdeacon Harold Dusty proved to
be a local ecclesiastic of the Anglican per-
suasion. Mrs. Betsy Hohsmail was one of
his best-known and most-respected parishion-
ers.
McSnatch was unperturbed. He said:
“I am not unused to situations of this
kind. I was for seven months in the office
of Mr. Boulger, of Kariunda —now in
prison, and removed from the rolls on
entirely false charges of forgery and the
embezzlement of trust moneys. I will write
the customary form of apology and correc-
tion.”
This appeared on the leader page of our
next issue :—-
Apology and Correction.
Y ith reference to the charges of immorality
directed against the Ven. Archdeacon Harold Dustv
and Mrs. Betsy Hobsmail, both of Nangkor, 'we
never, at any time, officially proposed to level such
charges. We are assured by representatives of the
parties concerned that the said charges are baseless,
and therefore have the utmost pleasure in tendering
our apologies and regrets in respect of the same.
Three days later writs arrived from the
Archdeacon and Mrs. Hobsmail claiming
£5OOO apiece. McSnatch still preserved his
habitual calm.
“I deduce,” he said, “from the simulta-
neous arrival of these writs and the similar-
ity of the sums claimed that the parties
have been conferring.”
I said, “That appears to be so,” and
handed him a notice of an application to
have the suits consolidated. He was
delighted to find his judgment thus con-
firmed.
“Leave the whole affair to me,” he said
confidently. “Our present attitude is one
of quiescence. We have first to see how
they frame their case. That they will be
forced to disclose to me upon delivery of
their statement of claim. This case,” and
he rubbed his hands in a pleased manner,
‘ promises many features of interest. Mean-
while. it would perhaps be as well if I fur-
bished up my legal knowledge. If you will
advance me a few shillings I will endeavor
to pick up a couple of the necessary law
books.”
I handed him two shillings from the petty
cash. He came back, about an hour later,
returned me 7d., and showed me what he
called “a Roscoe,” for which he had paid
7d. at a second-hand bookshop, and Bullen
and Leake’s “Precedents of Pleading” (1857
edition), which had cost him lOd. He
appeared more gratified than ever, and said :
“Now, I think we have got them.”
The statement of claim arrived in due
course. McSnatch was delighted with it.
He said : "We are now on firm ground. We
(Continued on next page.)

BACKACHE, URINARY DISORDER AND
DROPSICAL SWELLINGS.
Mrs. W. Poulton, 23 Lamb Street. Lilyfield,
Sydney, says:—“My husband suffered terribly
from backache and kidney trouble for some time.
The pains in his back were often so bad that he
could scarcely move, and it was impossible for him
to stoop to lace his boots. If he tried to bend his
back in any way the pain was so awful that he
could not help screaming out. His ankles and legs
swelled a good deal, and be always felt dull and
tired, and could not get proper rest at night. The
kidney secretions were also affected, being thick
and cloudy, with a red sediment. He was really
far from well, but until he took Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills nothing did him any good. This
remedy was recommended to him, and it worked
wonders. After taking two bottles there was a
decided improvement, and by the time he had used
seven bottles of- these famous pills a perfect cure
was effected, and he has never suffered from back-
ache and kidney troubles since, a period of over six
months.”
Two years later Mrs. Poulton confirms the
above: “My husband is still well, and has not
suffered with his kidneys since Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills cured him over two years ago.”

gfg
know where we are. I propose ‘to meet
them by setting up every defence known to
the English law.”
The writing, or as he preferred to phrase
it, the settling, of our defence took him
about eight days. He began by denying
publication of the alleged libel, or any of it
the object of this move being, he pointed
out, to lay upon the -other side the burden
ot proof. Alternatively he tendered an
apology and paid a guinea into court,
while denying liability. Alternatively again
lie denied that the words charged bore the
alleged or any other meaning; and in the
further alternative lie pleaded that the said
words constituted fair comment on a
matter of public interest. He was careful
to point out to me that he had avoided a
jilda of justification as being hazardous in
the circumstances.
I had not suspected McSnatch’s inca-
pacity to handle the situation till there was
served on us an order for particulars of what
was termed our “idea of justification.”
McSnatch was bewildered. I pointed out
to him that lie claimed to have expressly
avoided pleading justification. His answer
was: “I fear that, lawyer-like, they are
attempting to construe our plea of fair com-
ment into one of justification.” He now
bore manv of the marks of the beaten man.
After further conversation of a depressed,
even alarmed, nature, he surprised me by
suggesting that I should consult a solicitor.
The solicitor said: “The situation is
grave. I can say no more at present. I
must consult counsel. How. much money
have you got?”
I went away to raise money on my only
asset, The Keyhole.
* * * *
Who shall describe the horrors and
degradations of the trial? I remember it
now as a disjointed series of nightmare
episodes. My cross-examination was one
long agony. Mr. Gammon, the K.C. for the
other side, cross-questioned me closely upon
my leaders of the previous six months. I
learned, during the luncheon interval, that
the Judge was a High Churchman, and that
the questions were calculated to inflame him
against me. One article, in particular, in
which I had compared Dr. Nugent, the
rector of the Judge’s place of worship, to
Heliogabalus, and also to “a clerical Nero
fiddling while the Anglican Church burned
in the fires of Romanism,” occupied the
Court for about an hour. The unfriendli-
ness—l had almost written disgust—of the
Judge became marked from now onwards.
The arrival of McSnatch in the box was
the signal for laughter which, it seemed to
me, the court usher did little to suppress.
Our counsel, a young fellow by the name
of Podgrind, appeared powerless to protect
the witness from the ribald jokes of his
more assured confrere.
At the conclusion of McSnatch’s examina-
tion-in-chief, Mr. Gammon called loudly
for the production of “Aunt Martha.” A
scene of bar-room levity followed in which,
to his shame be it said, the Judge joined
for several seconds. W 7 ith creditable dignity
McSnatch remained grave throughout the
sorry scene. Upon Podgrind explaining that
McSnatch was “Aunt Martha,” the man
Gammon affected incomprehension.
“I call for the removal of her whiskers,
yer Honor,” he cried. “It’s an attempt to
bias the jury.’”
He also persistently addressed McSnatch
as “Auntie,” “My girl,” and “Auntie Wire-
whiskers,” and at one stage so far forgot
himself as to demand a medical examination
to determine the witness’s sex.
McSnatch was among the few in coyirt
who appeared to appreciate the seriousness
of the proceedings. His self-command was
kingly.
The Judge’s summing-up can only be
described as malignant. He either was, or
affected to be, wholly incapable of under-
standing our point of view. He remarked
at one stage: '
When our forefathers struggled for, and estab-
lished, the liberty of the press, they never for one
moment contemplated that it would degenerate into
the grossest license. In this case the defendants had
not only disseminated a cruel and odious libel, but
had piled Pelion upon Ossa by persisting to the
last that it was true. It was only when the Law
itself, by means of an order for particulars, directed
them to disclose any fact or facts in their possession,
that they were compelled to admit that there was
not one shred of reliability in their scandalous
allegations.
It was at this reference to his unfortunate
error that McSnatch found the ordeal un-
endurable and left the court. The effect
of the Judge’s bitter words on the jurymen
was patent to all. They conferred for a
matter of minutes only. Their verdict was
for the plaintiffs and the damages were fixed
at £475. The foreman, speaking for the
jury, expressed sympathy with the Arch-
deacon and Mrs. Hobsmail, and said his
soul revolted at the conduct of McSnatch
and myself.
It was in this way that I became a book-
canvasser.
The funds realisable from The Keyhole
had just sufficed to carry me through the
trial. There was nothing in hand either
for the Archdeacon’s damages or for his
costs. These had been so heavy as to ex-
haust his last farthing, and more. A sum
of money having been placed in his bands
by a number of sympathisers, his solicitor
immediately filed a petition in bankruptcy
in respect of some costs which were out-
standing, and the sum, as it lay in the
bank, was attached. As the outcome of
these proceedings he was called before his
bishop, who explained that, while he had.
in a measure, cleared his character, yet the
occupation of the archdiaconate by one
who was financially unsound was unthink-
able.
Despite the ruin which he had brought
upon me, I could never feel other than sorry
for the, in a sense, unmerited calamities
which had overtaken this unhappy divine.
Too old to adapt himself to any other call-
ing he was in the end thankful to accept
an engagement with the Baptists.
McSnatch. earnest and active as ever,
was attached to a theatrical venture wlieD
last I heard from him.
J. B. D.
The Coming of Henry.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f is the history of Anna Matilda.
Anna Matilda went to the,township
called Tenpence when she was quite
a girl, and at the age of 58 she was still at
Tenpence. At 20 she was pretty enough,
thev say; at 58 she was a. gaunt, white
woman, as old as plenty of women at 70.
Tenpence itself could see nothing remark-
able in Miss Green for the reason that to
most of the inhabitants she had always been,
and, so far as recollection—went, had never
been extremely different. . But strangers
always paused to look after little Miss
Green, and were always’ concerned about
her, when they showed no manner of inter-
est in Beeton who owned the lime-kilns, nor
in fat Mrs. Carter whose husband had left
her a hotel and £ll,OOO.
Miss Green kept a small shop and did a
little dressmaking. She was also the sole
milliner of Tenpence, and the popular im-
pression was that she had a “nice bit” hid-
den away somewhere.
She was a woman who was waiting. For
close upon 40 years she had been waiting,
and the older she grew the more she looked
like a woman waiting. She was so obviously
waiting and expectant that the first question
of the stranger, after watching her pass,
was always flie same: “What the devil does
the woman want?”
Of course Tenpence had the answer pat,
and naturally the story was romantic and
conventional. When Anna Matilda was a
pretty girl of 23 she had fallen in love with
a young fellow who had previously fallen in
love with her. The young fellow had gone
abroad, to Melbourne or Sydney, to make
his way in the world, promising to return
for her. At 58 she was still waiting for her
young man, and still full of faith.
Little Miss Green’s actions corroborated
this tale to some extent. Every day, except
Sunday, she put on her funny old black hat
at half-past three in the afternoon, and
went up the street, over the log bridge, and
up the hill to Mrs. Carter’s gabled inn
(known locally as “The Ark”) and sat on
the stump under the big gum, if it were dry,
and on the deal form under the inn verandah,
if it were wet, and waited for the coach to
come in. No matter how late the coach
might be Miss Green waited. Once when
there had been a breakdown she re-
mained patiently on her stump till close
on midnight, with no sustenance but the
cup of tea fat and kindly Mrs. Carter forced
upon her.
The Tenpenny coach brought visitors and
homecomers up from the railway at
Cameron’s Flat, and when Anna Matilda
had seen all the passengers, and old Crowe,
the driver (who had been “Black” Crowe
but had turned “white”) had called out:
“Not this trip, Anner!” Miss Green turned
and went quietly home again, without a
glint of emotion in the steady, dark eyes
that lent a weird skull-like seeming to her
white face seen at some little distance.
At 58 the little woman still \Yent over the
log bridge and up the hill to meet, the coach
froiu Cameron’s. Three coaches had gone
to pieces in those 85 years, but old Crowe
and little Miss Green kept their unfailing
tryst.
On a bright spring afternoon in 1908,
young Woods went on the box seat with
Crowe from Cameron’s to Tenpence to in-
quire with regard to a certain 90-acre block
of arable land beyond Buttercup, up the
Shindy Creek, evidence of the existence of
which had been found amongst a small cart-
load of documents recently his by inherit-
ance as the son and heir of the late Henry
Woips, Esq., of Oldfield, Hawthorn.
Woods was 27. smartly-dressed, town-bred
and infinitely superior. He brought gloves
and a cane to Tenpence.
Henry Woods, stepping down from the
coach before the quaint, gabled inn in its
rich, spring setting, found himself con-
fronted by a small, slim, white old woman
who looked strangely into his face and put
a white, thin hand towards him. Henry
looked up at Crowe inquiringly, and liis
lips framed the word “Mad?” But old
Crowe was absorbed, looking at Anna
Matilda.
Miss Green put two thin hands out to
young Mr. Woods. “Henry!” she whis-
pered. She threw herself upon him, her
arms clasped his neck, her face was buried
iu his breast. “Henry!” she cried. “Henry!
Henry!” and then she slid from the dis-
gusted youth and lay, a small, limp heap,
on the yellow gravel.
Mrs. Carter knelt by little Miss Green.
She busied herself unbuttoning and freeing
the woman’s neck. Presently she held up
a small, old-fashioned photograph she had
taken from over the poor old heart of Anna
Matilda Green. “You !” she said to young
Mr. Woods.
Henry looked at the picture, his face be-
trayed sudden perplexity. “Me!” he said.
Certainly the face in the photograph was
curiously like that of young Mr. Woods, and
across the bottom of the figure in a bold
hand and faded ink was scrawled the sen-
tence : “Adoring you till death —Henry. u
Suddenly Henry understood. “By God,”
he said, “it is my old man !'”
They put Anna Matilda to bed. She
lived three days, and she kept up her illu-
sion and delusion to the end, and young
Mr. Woods was man enough to see it
through. Full of the vigor of 24, ruddy
with health, handsome as a wood god turned
tailor, young Mr. Woods sat by the bedside
of the little, white, old woman, and held her
thin hand as she passed.
“I knew you would come. It seemed long,
but now I know it was not long at all. My
dear one!” said Anna Matilda, “my dear
one, you have come !”
“Yes, love,” whispered young Mr. Woods,
“and now nothing shall part us.” . And like
a man young Mr. Woods kissed the thin, old
lips. Then the. smile faded and softened,
and Anna Matilda went to meet the other
Henry.
EDWARD DYSON.
